# The Winter Between Houses

# Chapter 1
The frost had come inside in the night.

Elsie saw it first on the parlor window, ferning up from the sill in white ribs that reached almost to the latch. When she touched the glass with one finger, her skin stuck for a second and pulled free with a sting. The room still held yesterday's cold, not this morning's. The hearth had gone black to ash, and the ash had a skin on it like old grease.

She kept still under the blanket and listened for the street. Milk bottles usually chimed against front steps by six. A bus usually pushed through the corner by half past, laboring in second gear up the rise. This morning there was nothing except a scrape and then another scrape: someone trying to clear frost from the inside of a pane with a teaspoon.

Tom slept on his back with his mouth open and a paper taste of ale on his breath. His left hand rested over his chest as if he were posing for a photograph. A conductor's whistle, cracked at one edge, hung on the bedpost where he could grab it before work. He had polished his boots last night and lined them up under the chair, toes toward the door, like if he pointed enough things at the day it might obey him.

Elsie eased herself up, one hand under her belly, and shuffled into her stockings. The baby sat low and hard as a loaf not yet risen. In the scullery, the tap gave her two coughs of air before a spit of brown water and then nothing. The bucket under the sink had a crescent of ice along one side. She snapped that with the poker and carried the pieces to the kettle.

By the time Tom came in, buttoning his shirt wrong and not noticing, she had got a reluctant flame in the grate with newspaper twists and the last two good lumps from the coal box.

"You'd think we'd moved to bloody Norway," he said, rubbing his arms. He had his old bus depot scarf on, orange thread gone gray, and he rubbed at the smoker's cough in his chest before he coughed. "Road's white from here to Bhatti's. Not one set of tracks. I've got to leave early."

"Your wages in the blue envelope?" she asked. She kept her tone flat, like she was asking about his clean vest.

"On the mantel. I told you."

"You told me Friday."

"It's Monday now, Els." He grinned as if she had set up a joke and he had delivered the punch line kindly. "I brought it, didn't I?"

He kissed the side of her head, missed by an inch, and took his mug to the fire. His hair had a parting combed straight with spit and determination, one side always trying to spring free. She watched him blow on tea that was mostly hot water and tannin and thought of how men could make comfort out of the shape of a mug even when it held nothing worth drinking.

When he left, stamping twice in the doorway to break the leather, she stood in the cold hall with the envelope in her hand before she opened it. She knew the weight of a week in notes. This did not weigh like that.

Two pounds ten should have sat in the fold after rent and his tobacco. There was one pound and a few shillings in silver, with a sixpence tucked in like a joke. She counted twice at the table because counting wrong had become the fear that shamed her most. Same total both times.

Outside, the lane had gone bright and hard, every slate roof edged white, every drainpipe bearded with icicles. The cold made the terraced houses look smaller, as if winter had squeezed them together. Elsie wrapped her coat over her belly, pinned her scarf with a safety pin that caught in the wool, and set off with the coal sack folded under her arm.

At Bhatti's corner she found the door propped open with a crate of turnips, each turnip crusted with ice that glittered under the strip light. Mrs Bhatti stood at the till in fingerless gloves she had knitted from maroon wool and mended twice at the thumb. She kept a carpenter's pencil behind one ear and licked it before she wrote in the credit book, no matter how dry her tongue must have been in this weather.

"No milk till noon if we're lucky," she said, before Elsie asked. "Van got stuck by the viaduct. Bread lorry is not coming at all."

"Any paraffin left?"

"Three tins. Two spoken for. One for Peter Walsh because he has the voucher letter and he waved it at me like a warrant." Mrs Bhatti sniffed and slid a packet of tea over the counter. "You can have this on tab. Pay me when the world thaws."

Elsie laid down two shillings anyway, because credit in ink felt heavier than coin in hand. The coins looked small and helpless against the black till tray.

At Calderbank Fuel Yard, the queue bent round the brick office and halfway up Chapel Street. Men with sacks stood stamping and swearing through their scarves. Two women had brought prams with the hoods taken off so they could heap coal where a baby should have gone. Lorry wheels spun in place by the gate, sending up black slush.

By the time Elsie reached the hatch her toes had gone numb in her shoes, and the baby had found a rib to press against as if to remind her she had no right to stand this long.

The clerk knew her by name. Mr Lomas had a fingertip missing on his right hand from a loading accident years before; he used the flat of that finger to turn ledger pages and left gray marks where coal dust sat in his skin.

"Morning, Mrs Harrow." He opened the club book and ran his flat finger down the lines. His forehead folded. "You are in arrears."

"That can't be right. I paid last Thursday."

"You paid three and six. Then Saturday there was a delivery ticket signed Harrow, two sacks, and no balance settled." He turned the ledger to show her. Tom's quick signature slanted over the line like it was trying to run downhill. "You are one pound two shillings overdrawn."

The words made no sense for a second. Overdrawn belonged to other people, people who had accounts and pens and men behind desks. Not her. Not on coal.

"I've one pound and six," she said.

"Then I can put you down for slack and a quarter sack when stock comes. No full bags till arrears are covered. Maybe tomorrow if roads clear."

A man behind her swore at the delay. Someone farther back shouted that children would freeze before paperwork warmed them.

Elsie gathered her notes slowly so her fingers would not shake. "Put the quarter, then."

"It won't carry you long."

"Nothing does."

He almost smiled at that, then looked away in the way of men who did not know where to put sympathy when it came.

The quarter sack looked pathetic in her hallway. Coal crumbs had already leaked through the seam and marked the linoleum like spilled pepper. She stood over it, coat still on, and did arithmetic against her own breathing. If she banked the fire through the day and fed it only at dusk, she might keep one room livable. If the temperature dropped further like the radio said, she would not.

The radio had called it a historic front before Tom left, the announcer careful and bright over the words severe and prolonged. Schools likely to close. Trains delayed. Nonessential travel discouraged. It had sounded like someone reading from a card in a warm booth while his tea steamed beside him.

She lasted an hour before she went next door.

Noreen opened to the second knock with a dishcloth over one shoulder and flour on both wrists. Her hair was tied back with green ribbon, the ribbon darkened in a damp patch where steam from the kettle had caught it. She had taken to wearing Peter's old cardigan because it reached over her stomach and still buttoned. One button was missing, and she had replaced it with a smooth brown one from a different garment, too big and slightly comic.

"You look half froze," Noreen said. "Come in out of that."

"I can stand a minute." Elsie kept to the step so she would not have to look at Noreen's warmer hallway. "Could you lend me a scuttle? Just till tomorrow. Lomas has put us on quarter till stock comes in."

Noreen did not ask why quarter, or why tomorrow. She glanced over Elsie's shoulder toward the lane, where snow was already filling yesterday's boot prints, then disappeared and came back with a full black scuttle in both hands.

"Take this and bring it back when you can," she said.

"I'll settle it."

"I know you will."

The weight of it pulled on Elsie's shoulder all the way home. Not just coal. The fact of being seen needing it.

By afternoon the routines that made a day had collapsed one by one.

No post came. The washing she had hung in a burst of optimism came in stiff as boards and smelling of iron. She could not scrub the hall tiles because the bucket water skinned over between trips to the grate. She chopped one onion and stretched it through potatoes and stock until the pan looked full enough to pass for supper. Then she turned the empty flour sack inside out and shook the corners for the last clinging dust.

She pushed the back row of tins to the front in the pantry and set the jars in a neat line, labels forward. One tin of salmon to look prosperous. Two jars of pickled beetroot from summer to look deliberate. No one needed to know there were only three potatoes in the sack and none of them clean.

When Tom came in after dark, he brought cold with him and pub noise still in his throat. Snow had melted into his coat and then refrozen at the hem. He smelled of damp wool, tobacco, and the sharp sweet reek of betting slips handled all day.

"Depot shut early," he said, hanging his scarf. "Buses like ice skates out there. Saw a lorry kiss a lamp post. Whole town's gone to pot in six hours."

"You stopped at the King's Arms," Elsie said.

"For ten minutes to thaw my fingers. You'd have done the same."

He rubbed his hands over the grate and gave her that stage grin he used with inspectors and pub men and anyone else he needed to win before they asked a question.

She set bowls on the table. Thin stew steamed and then stopped steaming before either of them sat.

"Lomas says the coal account's overdrawn," she said. "He has your signature for two sacks Saturday."

Tom blew on his spoon and tasted before he answered. "Does he now."

"Did you take two sacks?"

"I sorted it."

"With what money?"

"With money that was mine before it was his and yours and anyone else's." He grinned again, smaller this time. "Don't look like that. I had a line to cover, that's all."

"A line?"

"A marker. Christ, Elsie, you know how it is. One week bad, next week good."

"We're in a freeze. This isn't one week bad."

He pointed at her bowl with his spoon. "We've got food. Fire's lit. You're warm enough to nag."

"I'm not nagging. I'm asking where the pound went."

"And I'm telling you it'll come back. You pick the worst time to start counting pennies like a headmistress."

The wall between their dining room and the Walsh parlor thinned when voices rose. Everyone on Barrow Lane knew that without saying it. Elsie lowered hers and hated the lowering.

"I'm counting because the child kicks when the room drops and because I stood in a queue for two hours to be told I'm in arrears on my own hearth," she said.

"Our hearth," he said quickly, as if pronouns could repair things. "And you weren't told that on your own. Everyone's in arrears this week."

"Not everyone signed for two sacks and left me with six shillings."

He stared at her for a beat, spoon half-raised. The banter fell off him so fast she heard the clink when it hit the table.

"I said it'll come back." His voice had gone low and urgent, the one he used when he needed sympathy from men he had already lied to. "Don't make this bigger than it is."

She could have said then that she had found the betting docket in his coat when she hung it by the fire, folded twice and damp at the edge: Regal Turf, stake two pounds, race three, lost. She could have laid it between them beside the bread crusts and made him look at his own handwriting.

Instead she heard a laugh from next door stop in the middle. Noreen's laugh, cut off like a thread bitten through. Then Peter's chair scraped once and went quiet.

Everyone was listening.

Elsie stood to fetch more bread she did not have. She opened the pantry door to its careful display and held it there as if choosing, as if there were choice.

"We'll manage," she said, without turning. "I just need the real number."

Behind her, Tom pushed his bowl away. "You're doing my head in." He tried for light and missed. "What number do you want? Tell me that."

"The one that keeps us warm."

He snorted through his nose, almost a laugh, almost contempt. "Then you should've married a foreman."

Something tapped on the shared wall. Once. Then again, softer. Not an accident. A signal from next door that they could hear and would pretend they could not, if given half a chance.

Tom glanced at the wall and raised his voice deliberately. "Anyway, Walsh says council's moving coal wagons by priority now. Might get easier. Might all blow over by weekend."

He wanted performance restored, neighbor ears reassured, the script back in place where husbands knew things and wives kept pans hot.

Elsie turned from the pantry with the empty bread board in both hands and matched his volume exactly. "Let's hope so," she said.

The argument ended the way a lamp goes out when paraffin runs dry. Not solved, just dark.

Tom went to smoke by the back door, collar up, muttering at the weather. Elsie cleared bowls into gray dishwater and felt each scrape of spoon against pottery as if she were filing metal. Her fingers were numb and then abruptly burning when blood came back.

In the scullery she took his coat from the peg to shake off snow and found the docket again in the inner pocket, soft with damp. She flattened it on her palm, read the stake, read the race, read LOST in block capitals stamped hard enough to bite through paper.

The heating money had not vanished into confusion or wages held back. It had gone down a bookmaker's hole in ten minutes.

She folded the slip smaller than before and pushed it into the hem of her apron where the seam had come loose. Then she went back to the sink.

When Tom came in he was bright again, all weather talk and gallows jokes.

"Road'll clear tomorrow," he said. "You'll see. I'm on early turn. I'll square Lomas before dinner."

"All right," Elsie said.

He kissed the side of her neck and reached for tobacco. The affection felt like a hand placed on someone else's shoulder by mistake.

Later, when he slept, she lay awake and counted debts in shapes instead of numbers: the quarter sack in the hall; the scuttle from next door; the damp betting slip stitched into her apron like a hidden label; the sound of Noreen's laugh stopping at the wall.

A marriage could keep standing long after the joints had gone loose. She had seen chairs do it for years, one leg packed with folded newspaper, everyone careful not to lean too hard. It still counted as furniture until someone sat without thinking and the whole thing collapsed under them.

In the morning she woke before dawn to the baby pushing against her ribs and the cold pressing the blanket flat against her shins. Tom had already gone, or not yet come back from whatever errand he had invented before first light. His side of the bed held no warmth.

The tap in the scullery was silent. Ice had locked the basin drain. She chipped at it with a spoon until the spoon bent.

From next door came the thud of a pan set down too hard, then Noreen's voice, low and rapid, and Peter answering with that schoolmaster calm he used when he wanted a room to submit. Elsie could not make out words through the wall, only tempo.

She told herself she would keep her head down today. Sweep, mend, stretch soup, bank the fire. No scenes. No borrowing. No more evidence gathered in secret pockets.

At half nine, a knock came at the back.

Noreen stood there with a tin caddy in one hand and a shawl over her cardigan. Her cheeks were bright from cold, and she had taken the trouble to pin her hair with two pearl-headed clips that did not match, one cream and one yellowed near brown. She wore them anyway, like a private insistence that the day would not strip everything down to utility.

"I've made tea too strong for one house," she said. "And Peter's gone to the office to be useful about nothing. Can I come in?"

Elsie stepped aside. "Course."

Noreen looked once at the fire, once at Elsie's face, and set the caddy on the table without comment. She moved through the kitchen as if she had known it all her life, found two mugs, rinsed them in the little water left in the kettle, and poured.

"You two gave the wall a proper show last night," she said eventually, not unkind.

Elsie stared into her tea. The surface shivered with each wind gust up the flue.

"Sorry," she said.

"Don't apologize to me. I heard enough to know sorry isn't the useful part."

That was Noreen's way when she stopped joking: the line came quick and square and left no room to wriggle.

Elsie wrapped both hands around her mug. Heat seeped into the heel of her palms and stopped there, as if unsure it should go farther.

"He took money meant for fuel," she said. The words scraped. "He told Lomas he'd settle. Didn't."

Noreen nodded once. "I guessed as much."

"I don't want a row in front of the lane."

"Then don't give them one." Noreen blew across her tea and took a careful sip. "But don't starve your grate for his pride either."

A kettle lid rattled next door as the boil rose and fell. Somewhere down the lane a child cried, then coughed hard enough to stop herself. Boots passed outside, slow and crunching.

"Peter got paraffin from Bhatti because of his letter," Noreen said. "He'll crow about it all week. Let him. We can use what we have and keep him thinking he's saving us." She leaned back and pressed one hand into the small of her back, wincing before she smiled again. "I've got an extra sack from my brother's van run last month. It's in our shed behind the old pram frame. Take half today and half when this front decides to leave."

Elsie looked up sharply. "I can't take that."

"You can and you will. The baby doesn't care whose initials are on the coal ticket." Noreen's tone warmed, then hardened at the edges. "And if you try to pay me this minute I'll be insulted for real, and you don't want me insulted while I'm this tired."

Despite herself, Elsie gave a short laugh. It hurt in her throat from disuse.

"You'll get it back," she said.

"I said I know you will."

They drank in silence for a while, the good silence, with the table between them and the weather pushing at the windows like an animal that had found the weak spots in every frame.

Noreen broke it first. "My ankles are twice themselves by tea time," she said, matter-of-fact. "If this keeps up I won't see my own knees till Easter."

"You should put them up."

"On what? Peter's principles?" She snorted and rubbed the side of her calf where the stocking elastic had left a deep red groove. "I didn't mean that for your wall."

"It can have it," Elsie said.

Noreen studied her over the rim of her mug. "You don't have to tell me everything. But if he leaves you short again, you knock. Day or night."

The offer sat between them, plain as a loaf on a board.

Elsie nodded. She did not trust her voice not to crack into gratitude, and gratitude made her feel young in the worst way.

After tea they carried the coal together because doing it separately would have looked furtive from the lane. Noreen took the shovel and filled Elsie's sack in short measured scoops, resting every third scoop with a hand on her back. Elsie held the sack mouth open and felt grit collect under her fingernails. Their breaths smoked together in the shed air.

"That's enough," Elsie said when the sack reached half.

"That's what you said at the caddy and look where we've ended up," Noreen replied, and tipped one more shovel in.

They crossed the shared yard with the sack between them, each gripping a handle, waddling and swearing softly when they slipped. By the time they reached Elsie's door both were laughing through their breathlessness.

From an upstairs window across the lane, Mrs Clegg twitched her curtain aside and then let it fall. News in winter traveled on less than that.

Inside, Elsie poured the coal into the box and watched the level rise to something that looked, for one afternoon at least, like competence.

Noreen tugged on her gloves and headed for the door. "I'll send over a heel of yesterday's loaf if Peter has not eaten it with his sums," she said.

"I've got potatoes."

"You have three. I heard the sack when you moved it." She lifted a shoulder. "Don't be proud with me. Be proud with men if you must."

After she left, the house seemed larger and emptier at once. Elsie wiped the table, folded the dishcloth, and took her apron from the peg.

She unpicked the loose hem with a bread knife tip and slid out the betting slip. The paper had dried stiff and wavy from yesterday's damp. The ink on LOST had bled slightly at one corner, making the word look as if it were trying to run.

She held the slip over the fire for a second, close enough to curl the edge, then pulled it back. Burning it would not return the pound. Keeping it would not either. But a thing with handwriting on it could anchor memory when charm and apology started doing their work.

She tucked it into the bottom of the tea caddy under the old leaves, where Tom would never look. Then she sat at the table and opened the housekeeping notebook to a clean page.

I owe Noreen half a sack of coal.

She stared at the line until the nib blot spread, then added below it:

Lomas arrears are one pound and two.

Tom said he would square it tomorrow.

She left a gap and wrote one more sentence in smaller letters, pressing hard enough to score the page beneath.

Do not ask tonight. Keep heat first.

The rule looked sensible in ink, almost calm. On the lane, a council lorry ground past, chains clattering against frozen road. Somewhere a man shouted for someone to mind the curb. The sound bounced house to house and came thinner by the time it reached her kitchen.

Elsie closed the notebook, slid it under the breadboard, and fed two careful lumps into the grate. Flame climbed, caught, and settled low. She held her palms out and watched the skin over her knuckles pink back to life.

Normalcy, she thought, was not a state. It was work. It was choosing what to hide and what to keep breathing long enough to face tomorrow.


# Chapter 2

Noreen woke before the alarm because the baby had found a hard corner under her ribs and kept nudging until she sat up. The room was black except for the pale rectangle where the frost had thinned on the window. Peter still slept on his back, lips parted, breath dry and regular, one hand on the counterpane as if he was waiting for someone to pass him a file. She swung her feet down, felt cold boards through the thin soles of her slippers, and stood very still until the room stopped tipping. The child shifted once, then settled.

In the kitchen she struck a match and cupped it to the fire grate. Coal caught slowly, then all at once, a low red mouth under clinker. She set the kettle on, pulled her cardigan tight over the front of her nightdress, and reached for the morning post where it had slid halfway under the door with a line of blown snow. There was a milk circular, a parish notice, and an envelope with heavy red letters on the front.

FINAL DEMAND, the red stamp said.

The paper was damp at one corner, and the ink on the address had bled where a glove must have pressed it. She could make out the lane, not much else. Peter sometimes had rate-office correspondence marked urgent in angry print, and he hated when post sat unopened. She slit the flap with the blunt knife she used for butter and eased out two folded sheets and a carbon copy slip that left blue dust on her fingertips.

Calderbank Baby Goods, Hire Purchase Department. She read the name once and then again because her eyes refused the words the first time.

Account holder: Mrs Elsie Harrow.

Arrears outstanding: GBP 12 8s.

Final notice before retrieval action.

She held the letter closer to the stove light. The paper had that office smell, stale paste and ink ribbon and hands that had counted too many forms. Beneath the demand was a typed line about an extension request, approved on the strength of a signed authorization dated the tenth of December. The carbon slip was attached to prove it. She flattened it against the table with the heel of her hand.

Signature of purchaser: Elsie Harrow.

It was not Elsie’s hand. Noreen had seen Elsie write lists for the shop, careful block letters pressed so hard the pencil point snapped. This signature slanted right as if it was leaning into a pub counter. The first E was broad and lazy. The y in Harrow hooked under itself with a flourish that tried too hard. Noreen knew that y. She had watched Tom make the same tail when he signed for two sacks of coal in November and laughed about not being trusted with chalk, let alone money.

The kettle knocked its lid and rattled like teeth. Noreen lifted it off too late and water hissed across the ring, then ran in a line toward the edge of the range. She reached for a cloth, wiped, and stood there with the damp rag in her hand while the red stamp on the envelope sat accusing her from the table.

It had come to the wrong door, and she had opened it, and now she held something that could not be unknown.

She told herself she would carry it straight next door, hand it to Elsie, and that would be that. There would be no talk at the threshold, no faces at windows, and no chance for Mrs Prentice three houses down to tell her sister that bailiffs were circling Barrow Lane. She could do this in two minutes and keep the morning ordinary.

By the time she had dressed, tugged her wool skirt over her hips, and pinned back her hair, the lane had started its daily scraping noises. Buckets knocked against step-stones. A shovel edge grated on packed snow. Someone coughed deep and wet through the terrace wall. She slid the envelope inside her cardigan, where the paper scratched when she breathed, and stepped out the back way to avoid the front path where everyone could see everyone else.

She was halfway along the side ginnel when Mrs Bhatti lifted her shop shutter from the other end. Metal banged, then rose in jerks. Mrs Bhatti wore her husband’s old army greatcoat over a flowered house dress and had wrapped a green scarf over her hair so only her eyebrows showed. She kept a tin of clove sweets in her pocket and sucked one before she opened each morning; Noreen could smell it now, sharp and sweet in the frozen air.

“Morning, Nora,” Mrs Bhatti called. “If your Peter wants his Players, tell him he’s paying cash today. I’m not a bank.”

Noreen smiled because that was what the lane required. “I’ll tell him. Has the milk come?”

“It came,” Mrs Bhatti said. She set a crate down and rolled her shoulders. “Half the bottles were cracked. The driver said the road to Ledley is glass. We will have turnips and complaints and that will be our feast.”

She always said bleak things like jokes and then watched your face to see whether you could carry your end. Noreen usually could. This morning the envelope rubbed her skin like a rash.

“Put me down for two turnips, then,” she said.

“Done. You look pale.” Mrs Bhatti squinted at her. “Did the baby keep you up?”

“All night boxing.”

“Good lungs, then. Better than most men on this lane.”

Mrs Bhatti laughed at her own line, a sudden bark that turned into a cough. Noreen laughed too, too late, and kept moving before another question landed.

At the Harrows’ back door she paused with her fist raised. Heat leaked around the frame in a thin greasy smell of boiled cabbage and damp coal. She heard a chair scrape, then a pan put down harder than needed. She heard no voices. She knocked once with her knuckles and waited.

Elsie opened after a moment with her sleeves rolled and soap up to her elbows. A line of wet nappies hung over the fireguard behind her, steam lifting from them. Her hair had worked loose from its pins and curled damp at the temples.

“Nora. You’re early.”

“I’m early. I’m sorry. I won’t keep you.”

Elsie glanced past her into the yard, then back. “Everything all right?”

Noreen had expected to hand the envelope over with one sentence and go. She had not expected Elsie’s face, open and tired and trying not to ask for anything. She pulled the envelope out from inside her cardigan. The red stamp flashed between them.

“This came through ours. I opened it before I saw.”

Elsie did not take it. Her eyes dropped to the red print and came back up quick, as if the paper had thrown heat.

“Was Tom with you?” she asked.

“No. He went out before light, I think. I didn’t see him.”

“Right.” Elsie wiped one hand on her apron and reached at last. She held the envelope by the corners as if the center might stain. “Thanks.”

Noreen should have let it rest there, but the carbon slip in her head would not sit still. She heard herself say, “There’s a form in it. About the pram account.”

Elsie’s jaw moved once, not quite a swallow. “He said he settled that Friday.”

“He didn’t.”

Steam from the drying nappies made the doorway close and wet around them. Elsie turned her body so Noreen could step in out of the yard. Noreen hesitated, then came through. The room was only a few feet wider than two outstretched arms. Every surface had a task on it: cracked bowl of pegs, iron turned on its side, stack of folded flannels, two pennies set apart from other change on the sill.

Elsie opened the letter there at the table without taking off her coat. She read the first page in silence, then the second, then the carbon authorization. She put the pages down very carefully, one over the other, lining edges as if neatness could alter amount due.

“I didn’t sign this,” she said.

“I know.”

Elsie touched the signature with the side of her finger, not the tip. “That is his hand. He does that tail on y. He says it looks smart.”

Noreen felt relief and nausea in equal measure. Relief meant she had not misread it, and nausea meant being right made everything worse.

“Then he signed in your name,” she said.

Elsie gave one short laugh with no humor in it. “He signed in my name and spent the week telling me not to fret because he had it sorted. Christ.” She stared at the figure again. “Twelve pounds and eight. We haven’t had twelve pounds in this house at one go since August.”

Noreen looked at the two pennies on the sill. She did not need asking to know what they were for: tomorrow’s bread when Mrs Bhatti stopped carrying the tab another day. Economic shame was not dramatic on Barrow Lane. It sat in plain view, two coins on a windowsill, while women talked about weather.

“If anyone else sees that envelope…” Elsie said, then stopped.

Noreen thought of the lane, which loved a crisis provided it was next door. “No one saw me come in.”

Elsie folded the letter once and once again, too small, then flattened it back out as if folding had offended her. “I need to think.”

Noreen nodded. “I’ll go.”

At the door Elsie spoke behind her. “Don’t say it to Peter yet. Please. Let me speak to Tom first.”

There it was, the ask sitting between politeness and plea. Noreen turned. Elsie was still by the table, one hand over the paper, the other braced on the wood near the iron.

“All right,” Noreen said.

She meant it when she said it. She did not yet know what “all right” would cost.

Back in her own kitchen she set about the noon meal with the discipline she used when she needed her hands to occupy her head. She peeled potatoes, saved skins for stock, and cut onions thin so they would sweeten before they burned. The baby rolled once and then lodged low, heavy as a wet loaf. Outside, children shouted where the lane widened near the corner, then one started crying with the outraged force of a fresh fall on ice.

Noreen took Peter’s shirt from the line near the range, still damp at the cuffs, and pressed it against the warm oven door to finish it. When she reached for the sewing basket to mend a loose button, she found last Christmas’s cards tied with red thread and shoved into the side pocket. Tom’s card to Peter sat on top, because Peter kept cards by sender and year like receipts. Noreen untied the thread, slid Tom’s card free, and looked at the signature under the printed robin.

To Pete and Nora, keep warm, keep laughing.

Tom.

The same hooked y, jaunty and false-confident. Her stomach dropped hard enough that she had to sit down on the flour bin.

By four the light had thinned to lead. She lit the lamp and set the table for two. Peter came in at half past, stamping snow from his boots, collar up against his jaw. He laid his ledger satchel on the chair before he took off his coat. He always did that first, as if the bag needed to be sheltered before his own hands.

“You are late with the lamp,” he said, rubbing his glasses with his handkerchief. “The room is dim.”

“It’s lit now.”

He hung his coat, sat, and looked at the stew pot. “Is this all the beef we have left?”

“It’s what there was.”

He nodded once, the way he did when recording a figure he did not like. They ate for a few minutes with only spoon and plate noise between them. Noreen could feel the envelope through the table, though it was not there. Elsie had it. The fact of it still sat in the room.

Peter set his spoon down. “Mrs Bhatti told me you were at the Harrows before eight.”

Noreen kept eating. “I was.”

“For what purpose?”

“To return a bowl.”

He waited, eyes on her face, not blinking enough. “You did not bring home a bowl.”

“I left it there.”

“That is inefficient.”

She almost laughed at that, at the perfect Peter word dropped into a winter kitchen. Instead she took another spoonful and burned her tongue.

He said, “Noreen, I am asking you directly. What is happening next door?”

“Nothing that’s yours to manage.”

He leaned back, folded his hands over his stomach. It was the posture he used when a clerk brought him figures that did not add. “What happens next door becomes mine when it touches this house. Debt collectors do not stop at property lines. Reputation does not stop at property lines either.”

“There’s no collector at our door.”

“Yet.”

She met his gaze then because avoiding it made him stronger. “You want me to report on Elsie now? Is that where we’re at?”

“I want transparency in my own house.” He said each word with clipped care. “I bring in the wage. I am entitled to accurate information.”

Noreen felt heat rise that had nothing to do with the stove. “I’m not your office, Peter.”

“You are my wife.”

“And I’m the one on my feet all day in this place while you balance columns and call it life.”

For a second he looked honestly surprised, as if a cupboard had spoken out loud. Then his jaw set.

“You will lower your voice.”

“No.”

Silence held, stretched thin as spun sugar. Through the wall someone next door coughed, three short barks, then a kettle whistled and was lifted. Terrace life did not permit private battles; it only let people pretend.

Peter picked up his spoon again, laid it back down untouched. “If there is financial irregularity with Thomas Harrow, and you know of it, withholding that information from me is foolish.”

She could hear the bureaucrat in him choosing terms, each one polished to avoid the words he meant: shame, scandal, leverage.

“He’s not your case file,” she said.

“Do not be childish.”

“Don’t talk to me like that.”

He stared at her for a long moment, then said, very calm, “I will ask once more. Did you see correspondence addressed to the Harrows?”

Noreen kept her face still. Lying to Peter felt different from lying to anyone else. It felt like stepping onto ice that might hold and might not.

“No,” she said.

His eyes narrowed behind the clean circles of his glasses. He knew she was lying. He also knew that proving it would require making a scene large enough for the lane to taste. He valued authority, but he valued appearances more.

He pushed his plate away. “Very well. If a man from collections comes here because you decided to protect your friend, you will answer to me first.”

He stood, took his satchel, and went upstairs with the careful, angry tread that meant he was controlling himself by force. The bedroom door shut. A minute later the tap ran, then stopped.

Noreen sat at the table with her hands flat on the wood until she could feel the grain under her palms again. The baby kicked once, hard and offended, as if she had been left out of a vote.

She cleaned the plates, banked the fire, folded tomorrow’s tea towel. Routine returned the room to shape. Nothing in the room said what had just happened except the spoon Peter had left with stew drying on it, brown edge crusting where he had set it down.

At nine, with Peter’s breathing thick overhead, Noreen wrapped a scarf over her hair and let herself out through the back. Snow had crusted to ice in the yard, and each step made a small crack she felt in her teeth. Light showed from the Harrows’ scullery. She crossed, knocked once, and went in when Elsie opened without asking who it was.

Elsie had changed into an old cardigan that used to be blue and now was every color of washing water. A needle was stuck in the front, thread trailing down her side. She had been darning one of Tom’s socks and stopped halfway.

“Is he back?” Noreen asked.

“No. Maybe not till late.” Elsie gestured to the chair by the table. “Sit. You look frozen.”

Noreen sat. The scullery smelled of soap, coal smoke, and the faint sourness of milk gone just off. On the table lay the opened letter, weighted by the sugar tin.

“I read it three times,” Elsie said. “It doesn’t change.”

Noreen pulled Tom’s card from her pocket and set it beside the carbon slip. “I brought this from ours.”

Elsie looked from one signature to the other. Her mouth tightened.

“Same hand,” Noreen said. “The y, the r, that long cross on the t. It’s him.”

Elsie nodded without lifting her eyes. “I told myself maybe I signed and forgot. That was stupid.”

“It wasn’t stupid. It was hopeful.”

Elsie gave her a look that said hope had become expensive. “He said they wanted a witness for a delivery date. He took my name off the hall hook, you know, on that little card Nurse Keane left, and practiced my writing while he smoked. I told him to stop dafting about.” She pressed her thumb into the corner of the table until the nail whitened. “I watched him do it and I still didn’t see it.”

Noreen let the words settle. The betrayal had not arrived in one dramatic blow; it had been built in ordinary gestures, in a husband entertaining himself with his wife’s name.

“What can they do?” Noreen asked quietly. “On the letter it says retrieval action.”

“They can take the pram before the baby’s in it. They can send a man to stand in this room and list what’s worth carrying off. They can send it to court if they want.” Elsie laughed once, air only. “A court over a pram. Imagine.”

Noreen could imagine it too easily: a man in a hard hat clipboarding through dishes and blankets while neighbors pretended not to watch. Poverty always had witnesses.

“You could go to the shop manager,” she said. “Show this. Say it was forged.”

“And then what?” Elsie asked. “They call police. Tom says I’m lying because I’m angry. We spend Christmas in statements. Whole lane hears by dinner. My mother hears by Sunday and tells me I’ve made my own bed with a gambler.”

Noreen rubbed her fingers together under the table. Carbon paper dust still clung in the lines of her skin. “You didn’t make this.”

“I married it.” Elsie looked up then, eyes bright in a face gone gray with tiredness. “That still counts against me.”

There was no clean argument against that. In Noreen’s own house, legality and duty arrived as the same sentence in Peter’s mouth.

Elsie reached for the letter again, smoothed it flat, and spoke without looking up. “Did Peter ask?”

“Yes.”

“What did you tell him?”

Noreen thought of Peter’s hands folded over his stomach, the polished words, the warning about answering to him first. “I told him nothing.”

Elsie stopped smoothing the page. The room held still.

“Nora,” she said, and now there was plea in it plain and unhidden. “I need you not to tell him. Not him, not Mrs Bhatti, not anyone. If Peter knows, he will call it prudence and then he will use it when he needs to. He won’t even mean to half the time. It’ll just come out as fact in the wrong room. Please.”

Noreen looked at the sugar tin weighing the letter down. There was less than an inch of sugar left; she could hear grains slide when Elsie shifted the tin. Everything in these kitchens was measured: tea, paraffin, what could be said aloud.

“If I keep it,” Noreen said, “I’m lying in my own house. You know that.”

“I know.” Elsie’s voice cracked, and she cleared it. “I’m asking anyway.”

Noreen did not answer at once. She listened instead: the faint thump of Peter turning in bed through one wall, a bicycle passing at the lane end, wind finding the gap under the back door and making the flame in the lamp bend. The house pressed close on all sides, as if bricks themselves were listening.

“What will you do?” Noreen asked.

“Tomorrow I’ll go to Baby Goods before opening. Catch the manager while he’s setting books, before the girls come in and before anyone from lane sees me. I’ll ask for a hold till month end. I’ll pay something. Anything.”

“With what?”

Elsie looked toward the mantel where two envelopes sat behind a china spaniel missing one ear. “I’ve seven shillings from cleaning, hidden from Tom since October. I’ve got my wedding chain. Not the ring. The chain.”

Noreen made a face. “Don’t give him the ring either, if he asks.”

Elsie gave a tired half smile. “He already asked last week for coal money and called it borrowing.”

Noreen felt anger rise, sharp and clean. “He forged your name. That’s not borrowing. That’s theft with your body attached to it.”

Elsie closed her eyes briefly, as if the plainness of that sentence hurt more than the letter. “Say it quieter.”

Noreen realized she had leaned forward and raised her voice. She sat back. “Sorry.”

“No.” Elsie opened her eyes again. “Don’t be sorry. I just can’t bear hearing it loud.”

Noreen reached across the table without planning to and put her hand over Elsie’s. Elsie’s fingers were raw from soap and cold, knuckles split in two places, and still warm underneath. For a moment neither of them moved.

“Fine,” Noreen said. “I’ll keep it. From Peter too.”

Elsie squeezed once, quick and hard, then let go. “Thank you.”

“That doesn’t mean I like it.”

“I don’t need you to like it.” Elsie pulled the letter toward herself and folded it along the old creases. “I just need to get through this week.”

Noreen watched her tuck the papers into an empty tea packet, then slide the packet behind the flour tin where no man on a casual search would look. It was a small domestic trick, learned and refined in houses where women hid things to keep them alive.

At the door Noreen pulled her scarf tight and turned back once. “If he comes in drunk tonight and starts promises, don’t let him near that paper.”

Elsie gave a brittle little snort. “When did promises ever require sobriety?”

Noreen smiled despite herself. “Fair.”

She stepped into the yard. Snow-light gave everything a false glow, as if clean. Behind her, Elsie set the bolt. Ahead, in her own house, Peter waited in the dark with his low, regular snore and his demand for accurate information. Noreen stood between the two doors long enough for cold to bite through her stockings.

Complicity did not feel like romance or rebellion. It felt like this: a damp envelope opened by mistake, a lie told over stew, a hand on a hand in a room that smelled of soap and debt. She crossed back to her kitchen carrying all of it.


# Chapter 3

Elsie set six plates on the Walsh table and held the seventh over the oilcloth, turning it until the crack caught the light. The line went from rim to center and forked near the flower in the middle, as if the plate had drawn its own map out of trouble. If she put that one in front of Tom he would joke and eat; if she put it in front of Peter he would look at it like an insult with gravy on top.

Noreen came in from the scullery with her sleeves rolled to the elbows and a damp lock stuck to her cheek. She carried a stack of saucers that did not belong to either house, all pale green with a hotel crest rubbed almost away.

"Mrs Bhatti found these in the back room," she said. "Said they were from her cousin's cafe and we can have them till New Year if we don't chip the lot."

Elsie looked at the crest again. "Cafe saucers for Christmas dinner. That's class."

Noreen grinned. "We're continental now. Stop staring and lay them out."

The room smelled of onions, coal smoke, and the paraffin heater Peter had insisted on moving closer to the wall "for safety," though it made the wallpaper sweat. Outside, snow had crusted hard against the step. Every time somebody opened the front door the cold came in like a person with a bad temper.

Tom stamped in with his cap in his hand and his ears pink as raw ham. He did not have a butcher's parcel.

Elsie felt the gap where that parcel should have been before he spoke.

"Queue wrapped twice round the block," he said. "Then Hargreaves comes out and says the delivery lorry never got through from Leeds. No turkey, no goose, no nowt but two bony rabbits and a tongue that looked embalmed."

"You put our order in three weeks ago," Elsie said.

"I know what I did." Tom dropped his cap on the sideboard and rubbed his hands over the paraffin heater without taking his coat off. "He says he'll make it right after Boxing Day."

"After Boxing Day is when nobody needs a Christmas bird."

Tom blew a breath through his teeth. "Don't start, Els."

She wanted to start. She wanted to pick up his cap and beat him over the face with it for that soft don't start that always meant do the hard bit for me. But Noreen was there, and Noreen had her own husband and her own performance to mount before dusk. So Elsie said, "Fine. Then we need another main."

Noreen had already reached for the flour tin. "Lentil pie."

Tom laughed once. "On Jesus's birthday."

Noreen pointed the flour scoop at him. "Jesus was a carpenter's son. He'd eat what was put in front of him and ask for seconds."

He held up both hands. "I surrender to theology."

Elsie caught herself smiling and hated that, because smiling felt like agreeing to the terms again. Noreen saw it anyway and bumped her hip with the edge of the flour bin, a small deliberate shove that said keep moving.

By ten o'clock both kitchens were running hot and mean. Elsie chopped onions till her fingers stung; Noreen rinsed lentils in three changes of cold water, shaking the colander so hard the metal clattered. Tom peeled potatoes with the speed he only had when there was an audience. Peter, still in his pressed shirt with his tie on because he believed in proper lunch even on Christmas morning, stood in the doorway with a notebook and asked practical questions in that clerk's voice of his.

"How many portions are we serving exactly?"

"Eight if Arthur comes," Noreen said. "Seven if he sends his apologies and eats at his mother's."

Peter wrote 8/7 in pencil. "Then use one and three-quarter cups for the dried lentils. If you use two, we shall have leftovers and nothing for tomorrow's supper."

Tom snorted. "Listen to this. Christmas by actuarial table."

Peter did not look at him. "Food planning is not theatrics, Tom."

Elsie kept chopping. The knife nicked her thumb and she sucked the blood quickly so nobody saw. She knew that if she looked up she would catch Noreen's eye and they would both have to decide whether to laugh or shut up. Both options cost something.

At half eleven Noreen tied on her coat and said she was walking to the shop for more onions and black pepper. Elsie knew what that meant and followed her into the street before Peter could object.

The lane was white packed down to blue where boots had polished it. Breath smoked out of mouths and chimneys alike. Mrs Bhatti's corner shop had a queue that reached past the empty milk crate by the door. Inside, the bell gave its thin frantic ring each time someone squeezed through.

Mrs Bhatti stood behind the counter in a maroon cardigan with one sleeve patched in darker wool, her reading glasses at the end of her nose and a yellow carpenter's pencil hooked behind her ear. She did not write totals till she had licked her thumb and flattened each receipt against the counter with unnecessary care. Her left thumb wore a strip of white sticking plaster where she had sliced herself opening a crate of turnips; she used the good hand to pass change and clucked every time someone tried to hurry her.

When Noreen asked for lentils on credit, Mrs Bhatti did not pretend surprise. She reached under the till and brought out a sack already weighed into a paper parcel, as if she had expected exactly this morning to require exactly this mercy.

"You settle after New Year," she said. "Not before. Before New Year, you're all liars."

Noreen laughed. "Fair enough."

Mrs Bhatti peered past her at Elsie. "And you, Mrs Harrow, no carrying two bags. I saw you last week with paraffin in one hand and potatoes in the other. You think I'm blind because I'm old?"

"You're not old," Elsie said. "You're terrifying."

"I'm both." Mrs Bhatti slid two onions and a packet of pepper across the counter, then, after a beat, added a small jar of preserved lemons with a chipped lid. "For brightness. Your men will call it foreign and eat all of it."

On the walk back, Noreen tucked the jar inside her coat like contraband and said, "She'll put that on the tab and fight me if I mention it."

Elsie looked down at their two sets of footprints crossing and recrossing where the lane narrowed. "She's right about one thing. We're liars."

Noreen did not answer at once. Wind pushed loose snow off the roofline in a dry hiss. When she spoke her voice stayed light and almost convincing.

"Today's not for truth. Today's for looking like we never had to ask anybody for anything."

Back in the Walsh kitchen, they built the lentil pie in a deep enamel dish whose handle had been repaired with wire. Noreen fried onions and celery in the bacon fat she had saved since November, scraping every browned bit from the pan with a wooden spoon worn thin at one edge. Elsie mashed boiled carrots with mustard powder and margarine and folded that over the lentils for a crust that would brown if the oven held its heat.

Tom came in, sniffed the pan, and said, "Smells better than turkey."

Peter, entering behind him, corrected him without pausing. "Smells stronger than turkey."

Noreen said, "If either of you criticizes this dish before you've eaten it, you can go gnaw frost off the back wall."

Tom gave a theatrical shiver. Peter sat down at the table and polished his glasses with a folded handkerchief as if dust offended him personally. Elsie watched the two of them and thought, not for the first time, that men could hold grudges like cold water in the mouth, swilling it around for hours.

By two the house had warmed enough for the window glass to run at the edges. Elsie and Noreen set the table with mixed crockery and the cafe saucers. There were seven knives that matched and one blunt table knife with a bone handle. Noreen wrapped the handle in a napkin and put that place setting at Peter's chair without comment.

She looked at what they had assembled: lentil pie, roasted potatoes in goose fat saved from last year and rationed spoon by spoon, braised red cabbage with cloves, one jar of beetroot, and a pudding small as a child's head. It looked meager and stubborn and alive.

"It'll do," Elsie said.

Noreen adjusted a fork by half an inch and said, "It'll pass."

The knock at the front door came exactly as the church bells started in the distance, muffled by snow. Time to perform.

* * *

Noreen opened the door with flour on her sleeve and a smile already pinned in place. Vera and Len Brennan stood on the step stamped white to the knees, both carrying parcels wrapped in newspaper. Vera's lipstick had bled at one corner where she kept worrying it with her teeth; she did that when her chest tightened, along with tapping the lid of the peppermint tin she carried in her coat pocket. Len smelled of damp tobacco and the antiseptic violet he dabbed on his racing pigeons' feet every winter, and his fingers carried the stain in the half-moons under the nails.

"Merry Christmas, love," Vera said, handing over a squat bottle. "It's not sherry. It's cooking brandy. Don't make faces. It burns either way."

"It'll do us fine." Noreen took the bottle and kissed Vera's cheek, then Len's. "Get in before your knees freeze solid."

They crowded into the front room with coats and steam and the raw happy noise people make when they are determined to be good company. Tom shook Len's hand as if closing a deal. Peter inclined his head and took Vera's parcel to the sideboard, where he lined up gifts by size before opening any of them. Elsie had seen him do this before and thought it looked like an inspection.

For ten minutes the afternoon worked exactly as planned. Tom produced paper crowns from a box of stale crackers and put a green one on Peter's head when Peter was not looking. Len told a story about trying to cycle on black ice with two sacks of bird feed hanging from the handlebars and ending up in Mrs Kendrick's privet. Vera laughed hard enough to wheeze, then thumped her chest and said she had given up cigarettes "except on days ending in y." Even Peter smiled at that, thin but real.

Noreen moved between table and stove, touching shoulders as she passed, refilling cups, keeping the room in motion. She had always been good at this kind of choreography. If people were laughing, they did not listen for the scrape under the laughter.

When she brought in the pie, Tom whistled.

"Now that," he said, "is a proper center piece."

"It is not a center piece," Peter said. "It is supper."

Noreen set the dish down and gave him a look sharp enough to shave with. "You can call it what you like after you've thanked me."

Peter folded his napkin on his lap. "Thank you, Noreen."

The serving started well enough. Elsie spooned cabbage. Vera passed beetroot. Len reached for potatoes and said, "Give me the burnt ones. Burnt's where the flavor hides." Noreen nearly dropped the spoon laughing when Tom announced in his bus-conductor voice, "Move down inside, please, make room for gravy."

Then Peter began to portion.

He held the carving spoon over the pie and measured each scoop by eye as if he were balancing accounts at the council office. One rounded spoon for Len. One and a half for Tom "because of his shift work appetite." Three-quarters for Vera. Three-quarters for Elsie. One for himself. Whatever remained scraped to Noreen's plate in a thin crescent.

"It's easier if we keep things even," he said.

Noreen stared at the crescent and said, lightly, "Even seems to mean hungry where I'm sitting."

Peter adjusted his glasses. "You were tasting while you cooked."

Tom let out a low laugh that might have been warning or agreement; with him it could be either. Elsie looked at Noreen's plate and then away. She knew the math of the dish. Peter's arithmetic was not about hunger.

Vera, trying to bridge the moment, lifted her glass. "To making a feast out of nowt, then."

"To ingenuity," Peter said, and drank.

The conversation limped, then found its feet again because people wanted it to. They moved on to church services, frozen pipes, rumors about the buses restarting by New Year. Tom told a story about a passenger smuggling a live hen under her coat; Len topped it with one about a fox in his loft. Noreen fed the room little lines, small jokes, prompts. Elsie watched her do it and thought of somebody stitching a split seam while wearing the dress.

Peter waited until pudding was served to make his move. He set down his spoon and turned to Len with that mild schoolmaster tone.

"Len, you are from Leeds, are you not?"

"Born and bred."

"You can hear the variance in local speech better than I can. Tell me, does Noreen still say 't' for 'th' when she gets excited, or has marriage corrected that?"

Len froze with custard halfway to his mouth. Vera's peppermint tin clicked once in her pocket.

Noreen smiled as if she had not heard properly. "What a thing to ask over pudding."

Peter kept his gaze on Len. "It is an ordinary linguistic observation."

Tom cut in too loudly. "Pete, give it a rest."

Peter lifted one hand. "No, really. I am interested in pronunciation drift. Council office gets all sorts now. You can almost map postcode by vowels."

He turned to Noreen at last. "Say 'thirty-three,' darling."

Heat rose up Noreen's neck under her collar. She felt it as a rash, not a blush. "No."

"It is harmless."

"Then you say it."

Peter gave a tiny impatient sigh and enunciated, "Thirty-three."

Tom laughed into his glass, desperate and ugly. "There you are. A Christmas parlour game from the grammar school."

Noreen kept her spoon in her hand because setting it down would have shown it shaking. "You're making a show."

Peter's voice went gentler, which meant crueler. "I am trying to help you, Noreen. People judge. You know they do. If you wish to be taken seriously by better circles, the old Ballymuck habits do not assist."

There it was. Ballymuck. The estate outside Galway where her father had worked road crews and her mother scrubbed floors at the convent when there was work. Peter used the name when he wanted to remind her he had rescued her from something. He had never seen the place. He pronounced it like a stain.

Vera stared at her plate. Len drank water as if it were medicine.

Noreen looked across to Elsie. Elsie had gone very still, not the soft stillness of listening but the held-breath stillness before someone breaks a glass in their hand. Tom watched both women and did not know where to look next.

Noreen heard herself laugh. It came out thin and metallic.

"Better circles? Peter, we're eating lentils off borrowed saucers while wind comes through the skirting board. If that's society, I must've missed the invitation."

Len barked a laugh before he could stop it. Vera kicked him under the table.

Peter did not smile. "Mockery is a poor substitute for improvement."

Noreen leaned forward. "And contempt is what, exactly? Good husbanding?"

Peter's jaw moved once. "Do not dramatize."

She opened her mouth to answer and then closed it. She felt the room tilt by half a degree, not enough for anyone else to notice, enough for her to know if she spoke now she would either cry or spit. She did neither. She lifted her pudding spoon and finished the last of the custard as if taste still mattered.

Elsie broke the silence by asking Vera whether her sister in Huddersfield had got coal through this week. Vera answered with grateful speed. Conversation restarted, shaky and overbright. Noreen stayed in it, because not staying in it would have been a scene and a scene would have been exactly what Peter would later call her fault.

When Len stood to help clear plates, Peter said, "Leave those. Noreen and Elsie can manage. Let us not upend all tradition in one afternoon."

Len sat back down slowly. Vera's lips pressed white.

Noreen carried dishes to the scullery with her face arranged in pleasant neutral. She could hear each voice in the front room through the doorway. Tom too loud. Peter steady. Elsie clipped and polite. The sound of cutlery against china was a small ongoing attack.

She stood at the sink, hands in cold grey water, and whispered under her breath in Irish, one line her mother used when storms took the roof tiles: God save us from the men and their good intentions.

* * *

Elsie followed Noreen to the scullery with the stack of plates too high for safety, because if she did not move she would march back in and throw Peter's careful face into his own pudding.

The scullery window was furred white except for a scraped oval at eye level. Through it she could see the back lane where bins sat buried to their handles. Noreen had her forearms in suds up to the wrist and was scrubbing the same plate twice.

"You all right?" Elsie asked.

Noreen did not look up. "Fine."

"Liar."

"Professional."

Elsie set the plates down and lowered her voice. "You don't have to keep this going."

Noreen gave a short laugh with no humor in it. "I do for another hour. Then they all go home and call it a lovely afternoon."

Before Elsie could answer, Tom's voice boomed from the front room. "Here she is, the kitchen marshal. Come rescue us from his worship's lecture on municipal budget deficits."

Noreen wiped her hands and lifted her chin. "Back to the front, then."

The men had moved from pudding to cigarettes and brandy. Smoke hung below the ceiling like gauze. Len was telling Tom that racing pigeons were better at finding home in fog than any bus driver alive. Tom countered with a story about navigating the Calderbank roundabout in whiteout conditions "by instinct and profanity."

Peter corrected him. "By luck."

Tom grinned at him with too many teeth. "Luck's part of the skill set, mate."

"Luck is what men invoke when they have not prepared."

"You sound like a pamphlet."

Vera said quickly, "Anybody fancy another bit of pie?" though there was almost none left.

Peter ignored her. "Preparation keeps households intact, Tom. Wages logged, bills met, habits controlled. It is really not mysterious."

Elsie felt the words like fingers searching for her pocket. Noreen had gone very quiet beside the sideboard. Elsie waited for her to soften it with a joke, to rescue Peter from himself the way she always had. Noreen did not speak.

Tom sat forward, grin thinning. "You giving me a sermon in my own neighborhood now?"

Peter folded his hands. "I am making conversation."

"No, you're taking attendance. Seeing who passes your little exam."

Len cleared his throat. "Come off it, lads. It's Christmas."

Tom pointed his cigarette at Peter and ash dropped on the rug. "You think because you wear a tie to eat lentils you get to grade the rest of us."

Peter's voice sharpened. "At least I do not gamble away heating money and call it weather."

The room snapped tight. Elsie heard Vera inhale, a tiny drag of breath through her teeth. Noreen's hand found the edge of the sideboard and gripped.

Tom stood up so fast his chair skidded back. "Watch your mouth."

Peter did not stand. "Sit down."

"Say it again."

"I have no need to repeat myself."

Tom took one step round the table. Elsie moved too, and so did Noreen, both women reaching without knowing for what, plate, sleeve, arm, anything to stop the line before it crossed.

Then the front door banged twice under a hard knock and a gust of cold ripped through the hallway. Everyone froze.

Tom swore and strode to the door. He yanked it open to reveal a man in a navy overcoat powdered white at the shoulders, cap in one hand, parcel in the other. The tip of his left ear was missing, a smooth old notch that made him look as if winter had taken a bite years ago and kept the mark.

"Arthur Pike," he said, teeth chattering. "Sorry I'm late. Buses stalled at the viaduct. Had to walk from town."

He held up the parcel with an awkward little bow. "Your mum sent trifle sponge and strict orders I wasn't to eat it en route."

Tom stared a beat too long, then barked out a laugh that landed like a dropped pan. "You beauty. Get in before you die on my step."

Arthur stepped inside and stamped snow from his boots with careful precise taps, as if each one had a number. He glanced round the room, read it in one sweep, and deliberately brightened his voice.

"Blimey, this smells better than the depot canteen. They've served us boiled turnip three times this week. I was one meal away from chewing the timetable."

Len snorted. Vera laughed for real. Even Elsie felt the pressure in the room ease a fraction, like a belt notch loosened after dinner.

Arthur took off his coat and revealed a cardigan darned at both elbows with different colored thread. He rubbed his hands over the heater and said to Noreen, "Mrs Walsh, if you've any work left in that kitchen I'll earn my plate. I'm no use at carving but I can wash up like a church widow."

"I'll hold you to that," Noreen said. Her smile was polite; her eyes were elsewhere.

Peter stood then, finally, to shake Arthur's hand. "You are very welcome."

Arthur nodded, gaze flicking from Peter's face to Tom's and back again. "Cheers. And before anyone asks, yes, I brought my mother her tablets this morning. She sent me out with enough reminders to fill a hymn book."

He said it with affection and embarrassment mixed, and Elsie, who knew exactly what it was to be nagged into virtue by someone who feared for you, liked him for that against her better judgment.

The trifle sponge was set out, sliced thin to make it stretch. Conversation resumed in safer channels: bus routes, frozen lines, rumors about council grit. Tom sat down again, still charged, and talked too much. Peter corrected small facts with irritating calm. No one mentioned money again.

But the fight had not gone away. It had only moved under the table like a dog waiting for dropped meat.

By five the Brennans had their scarves on and were thanking everyone too warmly. Vera hugged Noreen hard and whispered, "You ring if you need owt," peppermint and brandy on her breath. Len clapped Tom's shoulder and avoided Peter's eye. Arthur left with a wrapped slice of pie for his mother and promised to return the dish tomorrow, "unless I eat it on black ice and lie about it."

When the door finally shut and footsteps crunched away down Barrow Lane, the house exhaled.

* * *

Noreen stacked cups in the sink and listened to Peter bolt the front door. The click of the bolt sounded final in a way that had nothing to do with weather.

She and Elsie worked shoulder to shoulder without speaking. Grease cooled on plates in iridescent skins. The tap water came out thin and bitterly cold; the boiler had given up an hour ago. In the front room Tom said he ought to check the fire at home and Elsie answered, "A minute," then dried her hands and came into the scullery.

"I'll leave you to it," Elsie said softly. Her eyes searched Noreen's face as if looking for a place to stand. "You send through if you need anything tonight. Anything."

Noreen wanted to say stay. She wanted to say if you go next door I will be alone with him and I do not have enough skin left for another hour. Instead she nodded because wanting and saying had never been the same thing in this house.

After Elsie left, Peter came in and closed the scullery door behind him. He removed his glasses, polished them, and put them back on. He only did that when he was preparing a statement.

"That little scene at table was unnecessary," he said.

Noreen kept drying a plate she had already dried. "You mean where you asked me to perform my accent for your friends."

"They are not my friends. They are neighbors."

"Fine. For the neighbors."

Peter leaned one hand on the back of the chair, posture tidy, voice tidy. "You were over-sensitive. I made an observation. You chose to turn it into a grievance."

Noreen set the plate down with care because if she set it down hard it would shatter and then he would talk about temper for a week.

"I want an apology," she said.

He blinked, surprised not by the request but by the plainness of it. "For what specifically?"

"For making me small in my own house."

Peter's mouth tightened. "This is exactly the theatrical language I mean."

"No. It's plain language."

"You are tired. We all are. Supplies are erratic, the weather is intolerable, and everyone is under strain. I will not be put on trial because I corrected your pronunciation."

She stared at him. "You think that's what happened."

"I know what happened." He straightened a fraction. "I have carried this household through three weeks of shortages and panic. I will not apologize for maintaining standards."

Standards. The word landed like a shut drawer. Noreen saw, with a calm so clean it frightened her, the whole mechanism laid out: the tidy voice, the ledgers, the way he could make hunger sound like discipline and cruelty sound like correction. He was not having a bad holiday. He was being himself with witnesses.

Behind the wall, someone in Elsie's kitchen dropped a pan and swore softly. The everyday sound slid through the plaster and sat between them, intimate as breath.

Noreen put the tea towel over the chair back.

"All right," she said.

Peter frowned. "All right what?"

"All right. I heard you."

He waited for the rest: the argument, the tears, the bargaining he could win. It did not come. Noreen moved past him to the pantry and began putting away leftovers. Two spoonfuls of lentils in a jam jar. Half an onion wrapped in paper. A heel of bread hard enough to plane wood. She labeled the jar in pencil, not because anyone needed labeling but because writing gave her hands a job.

Peter spoke again, sharper. "Do not sulk. It is childish."

She did not turn. "I'm not sulking."

"Then what are you doing?"

"Accounting," she said, and let him decide whether that was a joke.

He stood there another moment, then gave a short disbelieving exhale and left the scullery. She heard him settle in the front room with the evening paper, pages snapped straight.

Noreen stayed where she was until the cold reached through her stockings and up into her knees. Then she wiped the table, banked the kitchen fire, and went upstairs. In the bedroom Peter had already laid out his shirt for Monday and hung his tie over the chair back. He had done it every Sunday and holiday of their marriage. Prepared, always.

She undressed without turning on the lamp, folded her dress over the end of the bed, and slid under the blankets with her back to his side. When Peter came up ten minutes later he smelled of smoke and brandy and Pears soap. He got into bed carefully, as if not to disturb her, and after a while put a hand on her shoulder through the blanket.

"Nora," he said, using the short name he wore like proof of tenderness. "You know I only want what is best."

She looked at the wall. Paint bubbled above the skirting where damp had come through in November. From next door came the muffled sound of Tom coughing and Elsie moving coal in the grate, shovel against scuttle, three measured scoops.

Noreen lifted Peter's hand and set it back on his own side of the bed.

"Sleep," she said.

He lay still a moment, then turned away with a rustle of sheets and a quiet irritated huff through his nose. Soon enough his breathing settled into the shallow rhythm she knew better than prayer.

Noreen stayed awake. She counted the sounds through the wall: one cough, two floorboard creaks, the kettle lid rattling, then silence, then Elsie's low voice saying something she could not make out. She had spent years translating muffled noises into stories that made marriage look solid from outside. Tonight she did not translate. She let each sound remain exactly what it was.

In the morning she would pour tea, warm his shirt by the fire, and answer to Nora when he called. She would do the tasks because tasks kept the day moving and because the baby turned heavy under her ribs and needed calories, not speeches.

But as she lay there with her back to him and the cold seam of mattress between them, something quiet and irreversible settled in. She did not need him to admit what he was. She had seen it with eight place settings and witnesses, measured out beside lentils and pudding, as tidy as accounts.

Downstairs the house ticked and cooled. Outside, snow tightened again on Barrow Lane, hard as crockery.


# Chapter 4

By eleven the frost had grown a second skin on the inside of Elsie's kitchen window, and Tom still had not come through the back door with his boots stamping and his voice already explaining where he'd been. She had scraped a clear patch in the glass with the edge of a spoon and watched Barrow Lane turn whiter by the minute. Snow came sideways now, not down, a chalky sweep that erased doorsteps and swallowed the gaps between houses. The lane looked less like a place people lived and more like something abandoned and filmed over.

The wireless on the shelf above the sink hissed through a weather bulletin from Leeds. The announcer sounded too calm for the words he was reading. There was severe drifting. Non-essential journeys were discouraged. A local authority notice was expected by afternoon. Elsie stood with one hand under her belly, thumb pressed into the seam where her coat buttons strained, and listened for the part where they said it would ease by tea time. They did not say that part.

She had gone to bed at half one with Tom still out and had woken at six convinced she had heard him at the latch. It had been the milk crate in the yard banging in the wind. Since then she had made tea, washed two cups out of habit, poured one away, and checked the clock so often that the battery had become the loudest thing in the room.

Anger had carried her through the first two hours. It was useful anger, the kind that let her fold nappies and count potatoes and curse him in complete sentences while she split a heel of bread with the old knife. By late morning the anger was thinning into something meaner. She knew Tom's tricks. She did not know this silence.

She layered herself in wool, then her brown coat with the mended cuff, then the scarf Noreen had handed her across the wall at Christmas because it was warmer than pride. The hem of her skirt snagged on a nail by the pantry door. She pulled free, swore once under her breath, and made herself stop at the table long enough to write on a torn envelope: Gone to find Tom. Back soon. She weighed it with the sugar tin in case Noreen looked in and panicked.

On the lane she moved carefully, boots feeling for kerb through packed slush. Her breath made the scarf damp against her mouth. At number six, old Mr Henshaw was already out with a coal shovel, carving a trench from his step to the road in inch-by-inch strokes as if he thought effort alone could keep the weather honest.

"Morning, Mrs Harrow," he called, not looking up. His cap was pulled down to his eyebrows and one sleeve had been darned with green wool that did not match the coat. "You heading far?"

"Depot first," she said. "You seen Tom?"

He shook his head and leaned on the shovel for air. "Haven't seen my own son since Sunday and he lives two streets over. If you've got someone indoors, keep him there. This lot's got a nasty mind to it."

That was as close to sympathy as Henshaw ever gave anyone. Elsie nodded and kept moving.

Mrs Bhatti's corner shop was dark except for the back room, where a strip of yellow light lay across stacked crates of onions. The front shutter was down to shoulder height. Elsie bent and ducked under.

Mrs Bhatti stood at the till in fingerless gloves, ledger open, pencil tucked into the knot of her braid like a hairpin. She had tied her sari hem up over her ankles for traction and was still in her house slippers, the felt soaked at the toes. A kettle steamed on a single gas ring near the biscuits.

"You're out in this?" Mrs Bhatti said. "You should be at home with your feet up."

"Have you seen Tom this morning?"

"Not since last night, and he was not buying groceries then either." She snapped the ledger shut with two fingers and studied Elsie's face, not her belly. "He was with Blackwell and that little ferret from the tyre place. Smelled like stale stout and damp dogs."

Elsie felt the answer land low in her stomach. "What time?"

"After ten. Before I closed." Mrs Bhatti poured tea into a chipped mug and pushed it across the counter. "Drink this. Your lips are blue."

"I can't stay."

"Then take it with you." Mrs Bhatti lowered her voice. "My eldest rung from Bradford at dawn. Said roads there are already blocked with lorries jackknifed from bridge to bridge. This isn't a day to go chasing men who don't want to be found."

Elsie wrapped both hands around the mug anyway, letting the heat bite her palms for three breaths before she set it down. "If he made it to the depot, he'll have to be there."

Mrs Bhatti gave a short, irritated hum, the sound she made when a customer asked for credit she could not afford to extend. "If you pass back this way, knock hard. I'll be in the storeroom counting tins and pretending I trust people to pay me in spring."

That, too, was kindness.

The bus depot sat behind a wire fence at the edge of town, a yard usually loud with engines, conductors shouting routes, men smoking in clumps by the gatehouse. Today it was muffled and wrong. Three buses stood angled in the snow like grounded boats, windscreen wipers lifted, windows furred white. The big doors were chained. No diesel tang, no roar, only the clatter of a loose sign on one hinge.

A man in a railway coat emerged from the gatehouse when she rattled the chain. She recognized him after a second: Stan Doyle, dispatch office, a narrow face and the same nicotine cough as Tom but deeper in the chest. He wore his scarf twice round and still kept pressing two fingers at his throat between sentences.

"Mrs Harrow?" he said. "Shouldn't be out."

"I'm looking for Tom. He was on early shift."

Stan's eyes flicked away toward the frozen yard, then back. "No one had a shift after seven. They pulled all runs."

"He signed in before that?"

Stan moved his weight, boots crunching. "Didn't sign in at all."

For a moment she thought she had misheard him because of the wind. "Check again."

"I did." He hesitated. "Look, I'm not trying to be difficult. We had four men sleep in the break room last night because they couldn't get home. Tom wasn't one of them."

She gripped the chain so hard the links imprinted her palm through the glove. "Who was he with yesterday?"

"Could've been anyone." Stan coughed into his fist, spat into the snow, then seemed to remember who he was speaking to and wiped his mouth with the back of his hand. "Maybe try the Lion. Some of the lads went there after wages."

"After wages were late again."

He gave a bleak little half-smile. "Aye. Hard to spend what you haven't got. Doesn't stop them trying."

She watched him, waiting for him to add something else, something useful, some scrap that would let her walk in one direction instead of all of them. He only pulled his coat tighter and glanced toward the office door where a telephone sat on a shelf inside. She followed his look.

"Can I use your line?" she asked.

"Dead since nine. Whole exchange keeps dropping."

He said it quickly, like apology and warning together. Elsie nodded once, turned, and started back down the hill.

By the time she reached Calderbank High Street the weather had thickened into white grit. She kept one hand on walls when she could, reading shopfronts by memory because signs blurred out at ten feet. The butcher had sandbags piled against his door. The pharmacy window had been boarded from inside. A boy no older than thirteen skidded past hauling a sack of coal on a pram chassis and grinned at her, face red with cold and triumph.

The Crown and Shuttle had half its windows steamed over and the other half filmed with frost. Someone had spread sawdust across the threshold to keep people from breaking their necks on the flagstones. Elsie pushed in and the warmth hit her hard enough to make her eyes water.

The room smelled of wet wool, pipe smoke, and spilled bitter gone sticky at the edges.
