Noreen's voice came through plaster, muffled but clear enough if you knew the tune of it. "You lit yours?"

"Just now." 

"Arthur got the pipe free. He's nicked one of my good spoons to lever ice." 

"He can keep it if it keeps water running." 

Silence, then Noreen again, softer. "I meant what I said upstairs." 

Elsie leaned her forehead against the wall, paint cold as tin. "So did I." 

She heard Noreen move away, boards creaking under her weight, then the kettle whistle rose and fell in two houses at once like an argument and an answer.

By ten the lane had woken into its freeze-day habits. Shovels scraped. A pram wheel squealed somewhere down by the corner. Mrs Lilley shouted at one of her twins for licking an icicle off the drainpipe. Elsie took in coal, shook out a rug, peeled potatoes for broth, then carried two jars of rendered fat and a strip of bacon rind next door in plain sight. They moved around each other in Noreen's kitchen with the ease of women who had worked side by side for months, only now every accidental brush had a second meaning under it.

No one would have called it dramatic. That was the trouble. A life could split without fanfare, right in the middle of peeling vegetables.

---

By afternoon the sky had gone the color of dishwater and stayed there. Elsie stood in her scullery with both sleeves rolled above her elbows, washing nappies in water she had boiled twice and still did not trust. Soap grated under her nails. The line in the yard was stiff as wire. When she pegged cloth up it froze into shape before she reached for the next one.

Noreen rapped at the back door and came in carrying the blue enamel basin between her hip and forearm.

"Can I steal your dolly peg? Mine's split," she said, then shut the door behind her with her heel and lowered her voice. "Peter's still not back. Arthur's gone to the substation. We've got half an hour." 

Elsie wiped her hands on her apron. "I said we'd talk."

"Then talk." Noreen set the basin down. Her cheeks were wind-burned from hanging wash and her hairline was damp with sweat. Pregnancy made every chore two chores. "Don't make me guess what face you'll wear tonight." 

Elsie glanced at the window. The frost she had scraped from the pane an hour ago had grown back in white veins. "In here?"

"No. Yard. If anyone hears, it'll sound like washing talk." 

They went out to the strip of backyard between privy and coal shed where the wall blocked half the wind. Elsie could smell damp brick, smoke, and the sharp ammonia of old snow where someone had let chamber water out too near the door.

Noreen pulled a basket of clothespins close and started sorting them by size, quick hands, eyes on the task. "All right," she said. "Terms. You first. You're better at lists." 

Elsie hated that Noreen knew her so quickly. She looked down at the basin, at her own chapped knuckles red from lye. "First: we keep the houses running. Food, coal, water, appointments. None of that stops because of last night." 

"Agreed." Noreen snapped a peg in half and swore softly. "Second?"

"Second: babies come first, always. If anything with either pregnancy turns bad, we don't hide it to protect pride. We call Keane, we call each other, we ignore the men if we have to." 

Noreen nodded once. "Yes." 

"Third..." Elsie swallowed. This was the line that would either keep her alive or ruin her. "Third, we don't pretend it didn't happen." 

Noreen's hands went still over the pegs. She looked up slow. "Say it plain." 

"I'm trying." Elsie took a breath that hurt her ribs. "I wanted you. I still do. That's true. Also true: if anyone on this lane gets sure of it, they'll eat us alive and call it piety. Peter could lock you in that house with a prayer book and an account ledger and no one would call it cruelty. Tom would sell the story for drink if it paid his tab. So we keep our mouths shut in public and we keep our hands to ourselves where there's walls and windows." 

Noreen held her gaze without blinking. "You make it sound like we're planning a theft." 

"We are," Elsie said. "We're stealing time we weren't given." 

A half-smile pulled at Noreen's mouth, then dropped. "You left out one term. Mine." 

"Go on." 

"Don't come to me only when you're freezing or frightened. Don't use me as emergency coal and go back to being good by daylight." Her voice roughened. "If we're doing this, we're doing all of it. Even the waiting. Even the bits that feel rotten." 

Elsie felt the back wall at her spine, cold through wool. "I don't know how to do all of it." 

"Neither do I. That's not a veto." 

From two gardens down came the squeal of a gate and a child singing off-key about oranges. Both women turned their heads by reflex, then back.

Elsie said, "All right. No pretending, no claiming what we can't keep, no vanishing on each other." 

"Better." Noreen dug in her apron pocket and brought out three clothespins painted black at the tips with stove polish. "Code." 

Elsie stared. "You've already made code."

"I was awake at dawn, wasn't I?" Noreen held one peg up between finger and thumb. "One black peg on my line, left side by the drainpipe, means I need to see you when it's dark. No knocking till the top room curtain's shut. Two black pegs means don't come near, house is unsafe. If you need me, same on yours." 

Elsie reached for the peg. The polish smudged her fingertip. "And if someone asks why black pegs?"

"We say I ran out of proper ones and Arthur gave me these from the depot stores. Men are always bringing home ugly bits of something." Noreen shrugged. "It's a stupid code. That's why it'll work."

Elsie turned the peg over, feeling the spring bite. "We need a spoken one too. For when we're at the front and eyes are on us." 

Noreen thought for a beat. "'Blue soap.' If I ask, 'Have you got blue soap?' it means I need you alone. If you answer, 'Used the last of it,' it means not safe yet. If you answer, 'I've a sliver left,' it means yes, later." 

"Too clever?"

"Maybe. But we're women on a lane talking laundry. No one hears intelligence in that." 

Elsie put the peg in her apron pocket like it was hot. "Fine. One black peg for tonight, two for danger. Blue soap for words." 

Noreen leaned back against the wall and shut her eyes for a second. "There. Domestic espionage. My mother would laugh herself sick." 

"Mine would pray till her knees gave out." 

Noreen opened her eyes. "Do you want to pray?"

Elsie surprised herself by answering fast. "No. I want to get through winter with both babies breathing and both of us still speaking to each other." 

"That's my religion as well." 

They stood with the basin between them and did not touch. The wanting sat in the yard like another person, patient, inconvenient, impossible to send home.

Mrs Lilley's voice cracked over the wall. "Nora? You got any bicarbonate? Little Alfie swallowed snow with soot in it and he's sick as a dog." 

Noreen's face changed in an instant to neighbor-bright. "Coming, love!" she shouted back. To Elsie, quieter: "See? No one lets you brood on this lane." 

She grabbed two nappies from the basket and walked to the fence with the easy sway she used in public, half flirt, half harmlessness. "I've got some, aye. Tell him if he licks gutter ice again I'll box his ears myself." 

Elsie took that cue and lifted the basin, headed for her own back door, and nearly collided with Peter Walsh coming through his gate from the alley.

He wore his office coat though it was Saturday, collar buttoned, hat rim rimed with sleet. His shoes were city shoes, wrong for this lane, thin leather already whitening at the seams. He had a packet of papers under his arm tied with green tape and a fold-out umbrella snapped at one rib.

"Mrs Harrow," he said, tipping his head exactly enough. He did not smile. "You are industrious." 

Peter always sounded as if he were dictating minutes to an invisible clerk.

"Trying to stay ahead of it," Elsie said, shifting the basin on her hip.

"Quite." His gaze moved from her face to the basin to Noreen by the fence and back again, calculating. "Noreen informs me there was a power failure of uncommon duration. I gather you sheltered here." 

Elsie kept her mouth neutral. "My grate died before midnight. Noreen had enough coal to keep one room warm, so I came across."

"Sensible in extremis." Peter's glove creaked as he tightened his hand around the paper packet. "I prefer prior notice when my household acquires overnight guests, but weather is untidy by nature." 

Noreen turned from the fence and came toward them carrying a little orange biscuit tin of bicarbonate for Mrs Lilley. "Peter, don't start accountancy with breathing people," she said, light as butter over hot bread. "Elsie would've frozen next door. You'd have done the same if it'd been Mr Hale with no heat." 

Peter looked at her for one long beat, then at the tin in her hand. "You are dispensing our supplies again." 

"Half a spoon for a child. We're not financing a revolution." 

His jaw moved. "You are always amusing when you are refusing instruction." 

Elsie felt anger flare, clean and dangerous. She tamped it down under practicality. "I'll send over a pinch from mine tonight," she said.

Peter inclined his head. "Unnecessary. We are not destitute." 

Noreen's laughter thinned just a fraction. "No one said we were." 

He stepped aside to let Elsie pass but did not take his eyes off her until she had crossed into her yard. She could feel them between her shoulder blades like cold nails.

Inside her kitchen she set the basin down and stood still until her hands quit trembling enough to lift a kettle. Through the wall came muffled voices: Peter's measured baritone, Noreen's quicker answers, then cupboard doors and the scrape of a chair. Not shouting. Worse, in some ways. Shouting spent itself. Measured voices kept accounts.

Elsie peeled turnips for stew and nicked her thumb. Bright blood welled and looked black in the low light. She sucked it clean, wrapped it in rag, and went on. The day narrowed to tasks: stir, bank fire, mend hem, count coal, fold cloth, check window putty, scrape more frost, listen without seeming to listen.

At dusk she took washing from the line with numb fingers and brought the pegs in by handfuls. Mostly plain wood, swollen from wet. One black-tipped peg fell into her palm and left a dark stripe across her skin.

She looked up.

On Noreen's line, left side by the drainpipe, one black peg held nothing at all.

Elsie stood in the yard with the basket pressed against her belly. Across the wall she heard Peter's chair legs drag, heard him clear his throat, heard Noreen laugh at something in a voice too bright to trust. The laugh cut off quickly, as if someone had put a hand over a bell.

She should have taken the peg inside and pretended not to see it. She should have banked her own fire and stayed put and been sensible for one night out of ten.

Instead she set her basket down, chose one black peg from her handful, and clipped it to her own line where it could be seen only from Noreen's scullery window, not from the lane.

The sign did not mean now, and it did not mean never.

Then she went in, shut the door softly, and began to lay out two mugs, two spoons, and enough tea for one more conversation that would cost them both.


# Chapter 11

By eight in the morning the front step had turned to black glass again.

Noreen saw it through the lace where the frost had not sealed the pane yet: a hard strip of shine from threshold to curb, no grit on it, no ashes, nothing the council promised in handbills and never managed on Barrow Lane unless a man from the council lived on that street. Peter had left heel marks from when he came in at half one, weaving and broad-backed in the hall, one shoe missing and his breath full of stout and peppermint. Those marks had frozen into pale commas in the dark.

She set his mug on the table anyway. Tea gone stewed and bitter because she had brewed it early and forgotten it while she pulled her stockings over swollen ankles that did not look like her ankles anymore. She had her clinic card tucked in the Bible on the shelf, not for piety, for safety. Peter never opened it unless visitors came and he wanted the room to look decent.

The kettle rattled. In the spare room Arthur coughed once, turned a page, and coughed again. He had taken to reading railway timetables in the morning as if trains still obeyed them. Noreen loved him for that useless hope and pitied him for it.

Peter came down with the careful dignity of a man pretending he did not have a hangover. He had shaved a path through one cheek and left white stubble on the other. His glasses sat crooked. He put both palms on the table and looked at the tea as if she had wronged him with it.

"You let that stand," he said.

"I’ll pour fresh. Sit down before you tip over."

"I am not infirm." He drew himself up, then winced at the kitchen light. "Do not begin your fussing."

There it was, the schoolmaster voice, his collar-button voice. If he had gone soft in the mouth with drink he would have been easier to steer. Formal Peter was a harder weather.

"Fine," she said. "No fussing. Drink this rot and enjoy it."

Arthur laughed into his cough in the next room. Peter ignored it with effort. Noreen saw the muscle jump in his jaw.

He drank half the mug to prove a point and grimaced. "I shall fetch the paper."

"You’ll fetch your death on that step. Wait till I throw ashes."

"I am capable of walking ten yards in daylight."

He reached for his overcoat one-handed because he did not want her help and did not trust Arthur to see him ask for it. He got his arm in wrong, cursed under his breath, and straightened the lapels like a clerk presenting himself at a window. Noreen came around with the coal scuttle anyway.

"Peter, just stand there two minutes."

"No."

He opened the door. January light hit the linoleum in a flat blade. She smelled iron air, chimney smoke, the binman’s cart three streets over where no binman should have been because the roads were still half shut.

"Peter."

He gave her that thin smile he wore when he meant to be obeyed. "Do not call after me like a fishwife."

He stepped down.

His foot shot out as if someone had yanked the leg from under him. For a second he hung in a ridiculous half-squat, coat flared, glasses flashing. Then he came down hard on his right side. The crack Noreen heard was sharp as kindling snapping under a boot.

Peter made a sound she had never heard from him, not even drunk: a raw bark that jumped to a high animal cry before he strangled it off. He curled around his wrist in the slush-dirted snow and tried to stand. Failed. Tried again.

Noreen dropped the scuttle. Ash scattered over the frozen step in a useless gray fan. She was beside him before she felt the cold through her slippers.

"Stay down."

"Do not touch me."

"You can order me later." She took his elbow and felt him shudder. "Let me see."

"It is a sprain."

The wrist was already rising, wrong shape, a hump where there should have been a clean line from hand to forearm. She did not need Mrs Keane for that one. Peter saw her face and looked away.

Arthur stood in the doorway in his vest, one boot on, one boot unlaced. Frost had turned his hair pale at the edges. "Need me?"

"Get his scarf and my handbag," Noreen said. "And my clinic card from the Bible. Please."

Peter snapped, "No clinic. I have to report to the office by nine-thirty."

"You’re not holding a pen with that hand for six bloody weeks."

His eyes cut to Arthur at the swearing, then to the neighbor wall where Elsie could hear if she was near the sink. Shame moved through him quicker than pain.

He pushed himself upright with his left hand and nearly blacked out. Noreen caught his coat front and took his weight. She felt how heavy he was when he stopped pretending.

"Inside," she said.

"No hospital."

"You want this set crooked? You want that hand like a hook?"

"Do not dramatize."

She got him over the threshold inch by inch, his boots scraping the sill. Arthur came back with the scarf, bag, and card, and handed her a folded towel too.

"Wrap it," Arthur said softly. "Not tight."

Noreen nodded. Arthur’s own hands shook in the cold. She noticed, absurdly, that he had darned the thumb of his glove with bright orange wool, the wrong color entirely. He had done it neat as surgery.

Peter lowered himself into the kitchen chair and set his wrist on the towel as if it were someone else’s limb attached by mistake. Sweat stood on his top lip.

"Get Dr Melling to come here," he said. "A home call is adequate."

"Home calls are for people with lungs full of water and babies coming out sideways," Noreen said. "You’re going to casualty."

"I am not sitting in that waiting room with drunks and children."

"You are a drunk with a broken wrist."

His face hardened so fast she heard the breath catch in Arthur’s throat. Peter spoke to Arthur, not to her. "Mr Hale, will you explain to my wife that language and hysteria do not improve outcomes?"

Arthur looked at Noreen first. "Outcome’s better if he gets x-rayed today."

Peter stared at him for three full beats, betrayed by grammar and class and pain all at once. Then he bent over his arm and breathed through his teeth.

Noreen moved to the telephone in the hall. The line gave her two dead clicks, then a tone, then nothing. She rattled the hook, dialed again. This time she got exchange and waited through a hiss of weather until a woman’s flat voice answered emergency control.

"Ambulance?"

"For a fractured wrist, no vehicle available," the woman said before Noreen finished. "Road priority is chest pain, collapse, labor, and children under five with breathing difficulty. Can family transport?"

Noreen looked at Peter, at his jaw shaking though he kept his mouth shut. "Yes," she said. "If I can find one."

"Then do that. Keep limb supported. No food in case sedation is required."

No food. Peter had eaten nothing but pride and stale tea. Good.

She rang Bhatti’s shop. No answer. Rang again and got Mrs Bhatti’s son, breathless, shouting over radio music. Yes, their cousin still had the Ford van. No, it might not start. Yes, he would try.

When she came back Peter had buttoned his coat wrong, left over right like a child dressing in the dark.

"Take it off," she said.

"Do not instruct me in dressing."

"Then dress yourself correctly."

He could not. His fingers fumbled at the buttons; he sucked a curse and dropped his left hand to his lap. Noreen rebuttoned him without asking. He sat rigid, as if each touch cost him money.

A knock thudded through the wall first, then at the door. Elsie came in with no coat, just Tom’s old cardigan stretched over her belly and snow in her hair. She held a tin mug with hot water and salt, steam curling into her face.

"Arthur said he’d gone down," she said. Her eyes skimmed Peter, took in the wrist, the sweat, the carefully controlled mouth. "Christ."

"Mind your language," Peter said automatically.

Elsie gave him the mild look she used on men digging holes in the wrong place. "You can dock me a shilling later. Noreen, I’ve got bandage roll and sling pins if casualty’s crowded."

Noreen took the mug. "Thank you. Can you ring clinic for me? Nine-ten slot, Mrs Walsh, antenatal review. Tell them I’m delayed at casualty and I’ll come if we’re out in time."

Elsie glanced at Peter and then back at Noreen. They did not need more words. "I’ll keep trying till someone answers."

Peter said, "My medical business does not concern the neighborhood."

"It concerns your wife," Elsie said. "And she concerns me."

Something hot and dangerous passed across Peter’s face, gone before Arthur reappeared in the doorway with Peter’s hat and Noreen’s boots.

Outside, Bhatti’s cousin got the van started on the third attempt. He was a narrow man with one ear bitten short, maybe by frost years ago, maybe by a machine, and he kept tapping the dashboard twice before each gear as if convincing it to cooperate. He told Noreen his name was Dev and asked no questions once he saw Peter’s arm.

The ride to Calderbank Infirmary took forty minutes for two miles. Dev crawled down untreated stretches while Peter swore at every skid and then apologized to Dev in courtly full sentences when a pothole jolted his wrist. Noreen braced one hand on the seat in front and held Peter’s forearm with the other, feeling each tremor that ran through him. Outside, people walked in the ruts buses had left days earlier, bent into the wind with milk bottles clinking in bags.

At the infirmary doors, Peter straightened and let go of her arm before anyone saw how hard he had leaned.

The casualty clerk had a mustard scarf wrapped twice around her neck and three fountain pens clipped to her cardigan like medals. She took Peter’s details without looking up.

"Name, date of birth, occupation, next of kin."

Peter gave each answer with clipped precision. "Municipal accounts clerk," he added, and the clerk nodded as if that settled the seriousness of his wrist.

"Time of injury?"

"Approximately eight fifteen."

Noreen said, "He slipped on untreated ice outside our house. Landed right-handed."

The clerk finally looked up. "You the wife?"

"Yes."

"Any head knock? Vomiting?"

"No."

"Pregnant, are you?" Her eyes dropped to Noreen’s coat front. "You should sit when you can. We’re short on chairs, mind."

Peter began, "My wife will remain with me," as if preempting a slight.

The clerk pushed a clipboard over. "Sign here, Mr Walsh. Or make a mark if hand won’t do."

He froze. Noreen saw the humiliation land, neat and exact.

"I’ll sign for him," she said.

Peter said, "No." He took the pen in his left hand and wrote with the fury of a man forging his own name. The letters looked like fence posts in thawing mud.

They waited among boys with split chins, an old woman cradling a teapot wrapped in newspaper, and a stevedore with his thumb tied in string. Peter refused to sit until his face went gray, then sat and pretended he had chosen to. Noreen watched the wall clock move toward nine-ten, nine-fifteen, nine-twenty.

At nine-thirty she found the pay phone by the lavatory and fed it her last sixpence. The clinic line rang, rang, rang, then died. She dialed again. A receptionist answered on the fifth try, voice flattened by static.

"Calderbank Maternity, hold please."

Noreen held. Men’s boots squeaked behind her. A child cried as if being peeled.

"Maternity clinic." New voice, brisk. "Mrs Walsh? You were due at nine-ten."

"I know. My husband’s fractured his wrist. We’re at casualty. I’m trying to get across after."

Paper rustled. "You are not seen if you arrive after ten. We have sixteen women booked and two staff absent."

"Please, I only need blood pressure and urine done."

"And edema review, yes." The woman sounded tired, not cruel. "Your notes say swelling increased at last contact. If you develop headache, flashing lights, pain under the ribs, or reduced movement, call immediately. Do not wait."

Noreen pressed her forehead to the cold metal box. "Can you fit me later today?"

"No. Earliest routine slot is Thursday at twelve-forty, if roads hold. I can put a red flag on your file because this is second disruption in three weeks."

Red flag. Peter would hear it as accusation; she heard it as a thin line over a drop.

"Put it on," she said.

"Bring urine sample if possible. Rest your feet elevated in the meantime."

Noreen nearly laughed at that. Rest in whose house.

She hung up and stood still one extra second to keep from crying in a corridor that smelled of carbolic and wet wool.

X-ray showed what she had already known. "Distal radius fracture," said a young doctor with ink on his thumb and a wedding ring too loose for his hand. "Clean break, no displacement after splinting. We’ll plaster from knuckles to elbow. Six weeks, maybe seven. Keep it dry. No lifting."

Peter asked, "Can I return to office duties tomorrow?"

The doctor blinked, then smiled without humor. "Not if you’d like your bones to stay where I’ve put them."

By the time plaster set and discharge papers were signed with Peter’s left-hand fence-post signature again, the clinic had closed and snow had started in fine hard grains.

In the van back home, Peter rested his cast in Noreen’s lap without asking. She kept both palms around it to stop the vibration from the road. Each jolt went through the plaster into her wrists, her shoulders, the small sore place under her breastbone where panic had been living for weeks.

At the corner of Barrow Lane, Elsie stood under a shared umbrella with Mrs Bhatti, waiting. Elsie raised one hand when she saw them. Peter looked away.

Noreen did not.

She held Elsie’s gaze until Dev turned the van and the lane swallowed her from sight.

***

By evening the house had reorganized itself around Peter’s cast as if the plaster carried legal authority.

Noreen set up his chair near the range because cold made him "throb" every five minutes and because he claimed the draught at the table made healing impossible. She moved the mending basket, Arthur’s boots, her own stool. She shifted the medicine bottle to the mantel where Peter could watch it. She lined up spoon, cup, and folded handkerchief on a tray by his left side so he would not ask for each item separately and then blame her for delay.

He asked anyway.

"Water."

"Now tea."

"Not that cup, the chipped one catches at my lip."

"Where is the circular from Accounts? I had it in my briefcase."

"Do not overboil the cabbage."

Arthur came in at six with his hair damp from sleet and a parcel of cod wrapped in yesterday’s news. He set it on the counter and flexed his fingers over the range.

"Queue was out the door," he said. "Woman in front of me paid in coppers she’d warmed in her glove. Thought they’d stick less to her skin."

Noreen smiled despite herself. "Did it work?"

"No. She swore like a docker and gave the fishmonger two poetry lines about his mother."

Peter said without looking up, "Arthur, would you please not encourage profanity at table."

Arthur rubbed water from his eyebrow with his sleeve and gave a tiny bow. "Wouldn’t dream of it."

Noreen fried the cod in the shallow pan with the last spoon of dripping. Peter complained about the smell, then ate all of his portion and half the potatoes she had meant for tomorrow’s breakfast. When he finished he sat back and examined his cast as if it were a document to be audited.

"You will have to write this week’s payment book entries for me," he said.

"I can copy numbers."

"Not copy. Accurately transcribe."

"Same thing if I do it right."

"Language matters, Noreen. Accuracy begins in terms."

He said it calmly, as if he were teaching a child to tie laces. She bit down on the answer that rose up: Accuracy began in not getting drunk enough to crack your own bones. Instead she took the ledger from the sideboard and opened to the right week.

Peter dictated household figures while Arthur washed his plate in the scullery, loud enough that neither of them could pretend not to hear.

"Coal, two sacks on credit, one pound six and four."

"Mm-hm."

"Do not say mm-hm while writing numbers. Repeat them."

She repeated. Her hand cramped halfway through and she flexed it over the paper. The baby rolled low and hard across her pelvis, a movement like a fish turning in a net.

Peter watched her stop. "Do not start that performance. If you needed to sit, you should have paced your effort."

Arthur came back in with a plate towel over his shoulder. "I can take dictation if that helps."

Peter’s smile stayed polite and sharp. "This is domestic accounting, Mr Hale, not a union meeting."

Arthur lifted both hands and turned toward the spare room. As he passed Noreen he set a small folded square of paper by the ledger, hidden under the edge. She waited until Peter asked for his second tablet, then slid it into her apron.

It was from Elsie, three lines in pencil:

`Heard from Bhatti boy: fracture set. I can come by after dark with broth if safe. Tap twice on wall if yes.`

Noreen kept her face blank and fed the note to the range, watching the paper curl black and vanish. Peter noticed the movement.

"What was that?"

"List of washing."

"You burn lists now?"

"Saves clutter."

He held her eyes a second too long. "I have no appetite for neighborhood conferences in my convalescence."

There it was. He had not seen the note, but he smelled movement like a dog scents thunder.

"Elsie asked how you were."

"Mrs Harrow has become unusually invested in this house."

"She heard you hit the step. Everyone heard you hit the step."

Color climbed his neck above the collar. He set his glass down with care that cost him.

"From this evening," he said, "there will be no unsupervised visits between you and Mrs Harrow."

Noreen stared, waiting for the laugh line that did not come.

"You can’t be serious."

"I am entirely serious."

"She is next door."

"Then she is conveniently close for supervised exchanges through proper channels."

"Proper channels." Noreen gave a short, disbelieving laugh. "What are we now, two departments in a ministry?"

"Do not mock me in my own house."

"I live here too."

"You are my wife," he said. "Our household must not become a public corridor. You have shown poor judgment in company lately."

She heard what he did not dare say plain: her laughter with Elsie in the yard, doors closing at odd hours, the way she had started answering him directly after years of smoothing everything over. He did not know the shape of it yet, but he felt the ground move.

Arthur’s door clicked shut down the hall. Not loud, not theatrical, just enough to mark that he had heard and removed himself.

Noreen lowered her voice. "I missed clinic because you broke your wrist. I sat in casualty four hours. I hauled you in and out of that van. I held your cast over every rut in Calderbank and did not drop it once. Do not stand there and tell me who I can speak to."

Peter stood too fast, pain crossing his face before control sealed it.

"I did not ask to fall."

"No, you asked to be treated like a magistrate afterward."

"I asked for order." He breathed once, carefully. "Order is what keeps people alive in this weather. Disorder kills. Improvisation kills. Gossip kills reputations and then livelihoods. I will not have this house run on impulses."

"Your order put me off clinic."

"One appointment is not catastrophe."

"You do not know that."

"I know this: if you wander next door whenever emotion dictates, this house cannot function while I am incapacitated."

Incapacitated. He used the word like a court filing.

She looked at his cast, then at his intact left hand, his intact legs, the mouth still perfectly capable of issuing decrees.

"You’re not incapacitated," she said. "You’re inconvenienced."

His voice dropped to a softness that frightened her more than the shouting would have. "Do not try me tonight."

Silence sat between them, thick as wet wool.

She thought of tapping twice on the wall and hearing Elsie tap back. She thought of not doing it and feeling that omission in her chest all night like swallowed ice.

Peter spoke again, mild now. "Tomorrow you will ring the clinic in my hearing and request another appointment. You will explain the delay respectfully. Then you will remain here unless I accompany you. Is that understood?"

Noreen could have said no. Could have thrown the ledger at him. Could have gone next door in her apron and slippers and dared him to stop her in front of Arthur and the whole lane.

She pictured the lane. Mrs Bhatti’s sharp kind eyes. Mrs Doyle’s front-curtain twitch. Peter, cast white as a flag, telling them all his wife had become unstable in pregnancy. In this year, on this street, men won that story first.

"Understood," she said.

He sat again, relieved enough to show it. "Good. Bring the nighttime tablets."

She brought them. Later she made up Arthur’s camp bed in the spare room because Peter said he might call in the night if his arm cramped and "a witness in residence" was sensible in case she needed help lifting him.

Arthur accepted the extra blanket and looked at her for a long moment. "You all right, Mrs Walsh?"

"No," she said before she could smooth it. Then, because truth felt dangerous in an ordinary kitchen, she added, "I’m tired."

Arthur nodded as if she had handed him a tool he knew how to use. "If you need me, knock twice on the wall. I sleep light."

The same code as Elsie’s note. Noreen almost smiled.

When she climbed into bed Peter was already on his back, cast propped on pillows like an honored guest. He did not touch her. Near midnight he woke her twice for water and once because the sling strap scratched his neck. At two he asked for the chamber pot though he could have managed to the lavatory. At three he slept, finally, mouth open, breath sour with medicine.

Noreen lay awake and listened to the shared wall.

No taps came.

***

In the morning Peter insisted on being shaved before she went out.

"I cannot present myself at the surgery with stubble," he said.

"You’re not presenting anywhere. You’re on a chair by the fire for six weeks."

"Decency is not contingent on mobility."

She warmed water, set a basin, and shaved him with the old safety razor while he lectured her on blade angle. Her own hands were numb from rinsing in cold water. By the time she tied his scarf and settled his sling, the first bus to town had already gone, if it had run at all.

"I will accompany you to the clinic office," he said.

"No you won’t. You can barely get your boot on."

"Then Mr Hale can attend."

Arthur, buttoning his coat in the doorway, said, "I’m on emergency call till dusk. If I miss rotation, half the north feeder stays dark."

Peter gave a thin nod that meant offense logged for later. "Very well. Do not linger, Noreen."

"I won’t."

He held out his left hand, palm up, not to touch her but to receive. "Fare money. I will allocate."

She stared. "Allocate."

"You emptied your purse at casualty. I am managing this week’s cash flow until we understand the wage impact."

She wanted to laugh and spit in equal measure. Instead she watched him count coins one by one from the tobacco tin, each piece clicking table to table: bus there, bus back, two pence margin. No extra for tea, no extra for a call.

"If buses are off?" she asked.

"Then walk directly there and directly back."

"On this ice."

"You are not made of sugar."

Neither was he, and still he had cracked.

She tucked the coins into her glove and left before she said it.

The lane looked beaten, not cleared. Footpaths were rutted with frozen boot prints and cart wheels. At the corner she saw Elsie at her gate shaking a rug, breath smoking in little bursts. Elsie glanced up, saw Noreen, and touched two fingers to her own wrist in a question: him?

Noreen gave a short nod and a tiny twist of her hand to mime plaster. Elsie’s mouth tightened. She took one step forward, then checked herself when she saw Peter’s silhouette at the Walsh window.

Noreen walked on.

Halfway to town she turned back once. Elsie was still at the gate, rug over the rail, watching until Noreen rounded the bakery and disappeared.

The clinic office had moved winter triage to a side entrance because the main steps were iced solid. Women queued in coats over nightdresses, flasks tucked under arms, children clinging to hems. A hand-lettered sign on the door read: `IF YOU HAVE HEADACHE + FLASHING LIGHTS + SWELLING, TELL DESK IMMEDIATELY.`

Noreen stood twenty-three minutes before reaching the hatch. The receptionist today was a young woman with bitten nails painted red and a tiny silver thimble hanging from her necklace. She kept touching it between calls as if for luck.

"Name?"

"Noreen Walsh. Missed yesterday. Casualty with husband."

The woman flipped through cards, found hers, and frowned. "You were due for pressure, urine, and edema review. Mrs Keane marked urgent follow-up after prior bleed episode."

"I know. I need another slot."

"Earliest is Thursday twelve-forty, but that’s contingency clinic. If weather closes roads, it cancels first."

Thursday. Four days if the buses ran, six if they did not. She pictured her feet by Thursday, skin stretched over fluid till her shoes cut lines she could not press out.

"Is there anything sooner?"

"Not booked." The receptionist lowered her voice. "I can put you on the cancellation board. Means if someone doesn’t arrive, you must come within an hour when called."

Noreen almost said yes, then remembered the house telephone that worked when it wanted, Peter by the range answering before she could, Peter deciding which messages were relevant to household order.

"Can you ring Bhatti’s shop if our line fails?"

"We can try one alternate number."

Noreen gave it.

The receptionist wrote in red pencil: `ALT CONTACT: BHATTI STORES`. Then she looked up, expression changing from clerical to human. "Mrs Walsh, I have to read this exactly. If you get bad headache, lights in your vision, pain under right ribs, sudden sickness, or the baby goes quiet, do not wait for Thursday. You come straight to triage or send for ambulance and say antenatal red flag. Say red flag. Understand?"

Noreen nodded.

"Say it back so I know."

"Antenatal red flag. Straight to triage, no waiting."

"Good." The woman slid her a card with the time written in block letters. "Keep your feet up as much as possible."

Noreen tucked the card inside her glove beside Peter’s counted coins.

As she turned from the hatch, she nearly walked into Mrs Keane herself, coming through the side door with snow on her hat brim and that heavy canvas bag dragging her shoulder down. Keane recognized her instantly.

"You missed yesterday."

"Peter broke his wrist."

Keane’s eyes went to Noreen’s face, then to her hands, then down to her boots where edema had made the laces bow outward. "How bad’s the swelling this morning?"

"Worse by night."

"Headaches?"

"A bit."

"How bad a bit?"

"Like someone tapping a nail at the back of my eye. Comes and goes."

Keane swore softly. "That’s not a bit. You needed seeing yesterday."

"I know."

Keane shifted the bag and stepped closer so other women could not hear. "Listen to me. If your husband blocks transport again, you send word through Bhatti or Hale or the police if you have to. I do not care who gets offended. If those symptoms spike, this turns from inconvenience to seizure risk fast. You understand that word? Fast."

Noreen felt heat flush up her neck despite the cold hallway. "He didn’t block. He broke."

Keane held her gaze. "And now he is home, and you are still the one carrying everyone. Do not confuse injury with innocence."

Noreen looked away first. Through the side window she could see two orderlies chipping ice from the ramp with shovels, one singing under his breath in a voice gone hoarse from smoke.

"Thursday," she said. "They gave me Thursday."

"I’ll flag your notes again." Keane touched Noreen’s sleeve once, quick and dry. "If you get those signs, you come in. No permission slips from husbands."

Noreen nodded, because speech had thickened in her throat.

She spent her last two pence on a call from the corridor phone, not to home but to Bhatti’s, asking the boy to pass a message to Elsie: `Thursday 12:40. Red flag. Don’t come over while he’s watching.` The boy repeated it wrong twice, then right.

By the time she reached Barrow Lane her calves burned from walking the final stretch where buses had stopped. Peter was at the table with his cast propped on the account ledger, reading one-handed and underlining with angry strokes from his left hand.

"You took one hour and forty-two minutes," he said without greeting.

"Queue was out the door."

"Appointment?"

She took off her gloves slowly. "Thursday at twelve-forty."

"That is acceptable."

"If weather holds," she said.

"Weather will hold sufficiently."

She almost admired his confidence in the sky taking instruction.

He reached out. "Give me the card."

She hesitated, then gave him the card. He read it, turned it over, and set it under the salt cellar where he put bills due and things he wanted visible.

"You will rest now," he said. "After you prepare lunch."

Noreen stood by the range, one hand on the mantel to steady herself while heat climbed her shins and pain thudded behind her right eye in slow, deliberate taps.

Through the wall came the faint clatter of Elsie’s pans, the rhythm she used when she was worried and trying not to show it. Three fast, one slow, then silence.

Noreen listened to that private code while she peeled potatoes for Peter with fingers gone clumsy from cold and swelling.

Thursday, she told herself.

Four days if roads held.

Longer if they did not.


# Chapter 12

Noreen woke before the alarm because her left hand had gone numb under her cheek. The fingers were swollen into thick pale sausages, her wedding ring buried so deep the skin folded over its edge. When she tried to turn onto her back, a weight settled high under her ribs and stayed there, stubborn as a fist.

The house had not warmed in the night. Frost had feathered along the inside of the bedroom window again, and the blanket smelled of damp wool and Peter's hair oil. She sat up slowly and waited for the dizziness to pass. The kettle knocked against the hob downstairs, a dull iron tap each time the flame shook. Arthur was already up in the spare room, moving in his careful linesman way that never quite made a full thud. She could tell him from Peter even through the wall: Arthur placed his boots side by side before he stepped into them, and the soles hit the boards in matched beats.

Her appointment card was tucked into her prayer book on the chair, Thursday 10:40 at Calderbank Maternity Clinic in Keane's block capitals. She had smoothed that card flat three times since Tuesday night, as if the ink might blur and give her one more week. Her ankles had doubled in size anyway. No amount of pretending shifted them.

Peter was at the table when she came down, cast propped on a folded tea towel as if it were something expensive that might scratch. He had shaved and put on his office shirt despite not going in. A man in a sling looked softer, she had once thought. The sling had done nothing to him except make him crueler with his good hand.

He had his little accounts ledger open beside the sugar tin. It had two columns, neat as parish notices, and each figure was pressed deep enough to mark the page below.

"You are late," he said without looking up.

"It isn't eight." She put the kettle straight and reached for the teapot. "I've clinic at ten-forty."

"I know what day it is."

He said it like a correction from a headmaster. The cast turned his right sleeve into a stiff white wing. He moved the ledger with his left hand and drew a line under yesterday's sums.

"Bus fares have increased again. We are not paying for two returns and your tea-shop gossip after."

"It isn't gossip. It's my blood pressure review."

"You were reviewed last month. Then you missed one. Then you claim emergency." He clicked his tongue and made another note. "If you require rest, rest here. It costs nothing."

Noreen set cups out in a row to keep her face level. One for Peter, one for Arthur, one for herself. The fourth cup, blue enamel chipped at the lip, stayed on the shelf where she could see it. She had not put it out since Peter's fall, not with him watching every movement as if kindness itself were contraband.

"Keane said I had to come in when they gave me this slot," she said. "It's not optional."

"Everything is optional if one can count." He pushed the ledger toward her with his good knuckles. "Coal. Bread. Cast plaster fee. Lodger meals. Tell me where the money appears from, and I shall applaud your clinic outing."

Arthur came in from the scullery with his coat over one arm and the smell of outside still clinging to him, wet rope and metal. He set a jar of screws on the dresser before he spoke, automatically sorting them by size while he waited for tea. He did it even half-asleep, thumb and forefinger finding heads and threads as if they were rosary beads.

"Road's slick at the corner," he said to no one in particular. "Bus was ten minutes behind yesterday."

Peter gave him a look that could freeze paraffin. "Thank you, Mr Hale. We can manage our household timetable without commentary."

Arthur did not answer. He took his mug, nodded once to Noreen, and left with the jar in his pocket, screws chiming faintly against one another with each step.

Noreen poured tea and did not drink. Her mouth tasted of pennies and sleep.

"You said you would pay fare when I rebooked," she said.

Peter snapped the ledger shut. "I said we would assess it. We have assessed it."

His voice had that careful municipal tone he used with complaint letters. It was not loud. It was worse than loud, a tidy refusal dressed as reason.

She hated that she knew his patterns well enough to hear the hinge turning. If she asked again, he would move from arithmetic to morality, then from morality to accusation. By noon he would be telling her she had endangered the baby by upsetting herself.

The back door rattled once. Two short knocks, a pause, then one. Elsie's knock, the one they had worked out months ago to spare themselves shouting through walls.

Noreen crossed the kitchen before Peter could stand. Her ankles throbbed inside borrowed boots, each step thick and hot at the joints. Elsie waited under the lintel with a shawl over her coat and her hair pinned anyhow, cheeks red from cold. Snowmelt darkened the hem of her skirt. She held up a tin measure.

"You left this with me," Elsie said, pitching her voice neighbor-bright. "Thought you might need it back."

Peter had followed as far as the doorway to the front room. He did not bother hiding his expression.

"Mrs Harrow," he said. "My wife is not receiving callers this morning. She requires quiet."

Elsie gave him a smile that showed no teeth. "It's one cup measure, Mr Walsh. I won't keep her from what she's got on."

"She has no errands."

"Clinic," Noreen said.

Peter turned his head toward her and did not blink. "No."

He gave her one flat word. He had discovered that plain command carried farther than shouting.

Noreen felt the room tilt for a second, not from fear but from that deep under-rib ache returning with bright edges. She gripped the doorframe and looked past him to the mantel where the clock ticked too fast, always three minutes ahead because Peter liked to arrive first and make people wait.

"I'm going," she said.

"On whose money?" Peter asked. "On whose authority?"

His cast brushed the chair back. Plaster dust came off on the varnish, a white crescent. He did not notice.

Elsie glanced at Noreen once, quick as striking a match. Then she reached into the inside seam of her coat and pulled out a folded bit of wax paper. Coins slid into her palm, silver and copper warmed by her body.

"On mine," Elsie said.

Peter laughed once through his nose. "You can barely keep your own house heated."

"Aye, and still." Elsie counted into Noreen's hand without looking away from him. "Four shillings and six. Return fare and a cup of tea if the clinic runs late."

The coins were heavier than they looked. Noreen felt each edge bite her skin as she closed her fingers.

"I don't need tea," she said under her breath.

"You need not be sentimental, Mrs Harrow," Peter said. "Debt between neighbors becomes gossip by supper."

Elsie shrugged. "Then write it in your ledger if that helps you sleep."

Peter's mouth hardened. For a second Noreen thought he would step forward and block the threshold with his shoulder. Instead he lifted his cast slightly, the smallest reminder of his injury, and spoke with exquisite politeness.

"If anything happens on that road, you will not tell people I approved this outing."

"You don't approve anything unless it has a column and totals," Noreen said, surprising herself.

His face went still. He was most dangerous when still.

"Be back before noon," he said. "Mr Hale requires his dinner, and this house does not run on sentiment either."

Noreen tucked the coins into her glove and stepped outside before he could add one more term.

The air knifed straight through wool. Elsie took her elbow as they crossed the patch of black ice by the drainpipe, not fussing, just steady pressure. They walked in silence past the Bhattis' shuttered shop, past the chapel steps drifted to half-height, past two boys breaking frozen puddles with a broom handle and shouting when the shards jumped. Every few yards Noreen had to stop and shift her boots because her feet no longer bent where feet should bend.

At the bus stop, five people waited under the bent timetable board. A woman with a toddler wrapped in a blanket kept stamping circles into the snow to keep the child warm. A man in a porter cap smoked down to the paper and lit another from the stub.

When the bus finally came, it crawled in second gear and was full to the door. The conductor leaned out, cheeks raw, and shouted that he could take two standing at most. The porter and the blanket woman climbed on. The rest of them watched the rear lights fade into white grit.

Noreen checked the coins again. If they waited for the next, they would miss the appointment and lose the slot. Keane had said plainly on the phone that if Noreen missed again she might not be seen for another week.

Elsie flagged down the first black cab that looked alive enough to move. It skidded half past them before the driver caught, reversed in a spray of slush, and leaned across to crank the passenger door open.

"Clinic?" he said. "Road by the market's blocked. I'll take Chapel Street and you can pray." He had one knitted glove and one bare hand red as meat on the wheel. A canary pin hung from his rear-view mirror, tiny yellow paint chipped off the beak.

Elsie said, "How much?"

"Two and eight if we don't get stuck. Three if we do and I've got to dig us out."

Noreen made a sound she could not hide. Elsie squeezed her wrist.

"Get in," Elsie said.

They rode with their knees pressed together on cracked vinyl, the heater dead, windows filmed white at the corners. The cab smelled of stale tobacco and damp dog. Noreen watched terraced roofs slide by in jerks and thought how easy it was to disappear from one street to the next in weather like this. A missed bus, a delayed call, a woman deciding she did not want to start a fight at breakfast, and by afternoon blood pressure could go from numbers in a notebook to a body on a kitchen floor.

When they reached the clinic, Elsie paid from the same folded packet, adding two pennies tip as if normal generosity could disguise emergency spending. Noreen tried to hand half back.

"No," Elsie said. "You keep your hand shut. We'll sort it later."

"I already owe you for coal and paraffin." Noreen's voice shook from cold and something meaner. "I'm not stacking up like one of his pages."

Elsie looked at her properly then, eyes narrow with worry. "Then don't call it owing. Call it me getting you seen."

Noreen wanted to kiss her, hard, right there under the clinic awning with frozen grit blowing into their collars. Instead she tucked the coins away and followed Elsie through the door.

The waiting room was overheated in bursts, a radiator hissing and stopping like an asthmatic chest. Wet coats steamed on hooks. A pram stood by the wall with no baby in it, just folded nappies and a jar of talc in the tray. Somewhere down the corridor a woman was retching into enamel, the sound thin and furious.

Keane called Noreen in twelve minutes late with her glasses sliding down her nose and a pencil behind her ear. She always smelled faintly of Pears soap and the clove lozenges she sucked to keep from smoking on shift. Today there was blood on one knuckle where the skin had split in the cold, sealed over with clear collodion that caught the strip light.

"Mrs Walsh, coat off, boots off if you can manage," she said. "Mrs Harrow, you stay if Noreen wants you. I'm not spending all morning repeating myself to husbands by telephone."

"Stay," Noreen said before Elsie could pretend she should wait outside.

The exam room was small enough that Elsie's coat brushed the instrument trolley when she moved. Keane wrapped the cuff around Noreen's arm, pumped, listened, frowned, and did it again. The second reading took longer. She licked her pencil tip, wrote two numbers, underlined both.

"Any headache today?"

"Since dawn."

"Where?"

"Behind my eyes. And here." Noreen pressed the heel of her hand under her right ribs.

"Any lights in your vision? Sparkles, black spots, little flies?"

"Last night, when I stood from the chair. I thought it was the fire smoke."

Keane pressed two fingers into Noreen's shin for five seconds and lifted them. The dent stayed as if her leg were dough.

"How long have your boots been tight?"

"A week, maybe two."

"Wedding ring?"

"Stuck."

Keane gave a short grunt that was not judgment, only arithmetic.

"Right. We're not playing at this anymore." She turned the chart toward them so they could see. "Six weeks ago you were one-forty over ninety. Last review, one-fifty-two over ninety-six. Today you're one-sixty-six over one-oh-eight. This is an upward trend, and it's steep." She tapped each line with the pencil in time with the words. "Urine last month had trace protein. Today's sample has more. You have edema to mid-calf and facial puffing. That constellation is preeclampsia. In my training notes they call it toxemia. Call it what you like. It can kill you fast if it tips."

The word kill did not echo dramatically. It sat in the room like another instrument on the tray.

Noreen stared at the chart and could not make the numbers mean body. Numbers were Peter's tricks. Numbers were ledger lines that proved you had failed. She wanted Keane to say pain scale, fever, something she could feel and report.

"I had pressure with my first," she said. "It settled."

"You had swelling," Keane said. "This is different and worse."

She turned to Elsie. "You asked for exact, last time we spoke in the lane. You can both have exact now."

Keane set the pencil down and counted symptoms on her fingers, each one clean and hard.

"If she gets a headache that doesn't ease with rest, tea, or tablets, call me. If she sees flashing lights or loses part of her vision, call me. If pain under the right ribs sharpens, if she starts vomiting, if her face or hands swell suddenly more, if she passes very little urine, if she grows breathless at rest, call me. If she has a fit, protect her head, turn her to the side, and send for ambulance at once. Don't wait for husband permission. Don't wait for weather to improve. Don't wait to see if she settles. Women seize and stroke on this trajectory. Babies detach placentas. I'm saying this in plain language because plain language is what keeps people alive."

Elsie's hands were clasped over her bag so tight the knuckles looked chalked. Noreen could feel the force of her listening like heat.

"What do I do now?" Noreen asked.

"You rest." Keane's voice softened by one degree and no more. "Strictly. Left side when possible. Feet up above hip level. No lifting coal scuttles, no carrying wet laundry, no scrubbing floors, no climbing up and down those stairs for every small thing. Salt moderate. Fluids steady. I want you seen twice weekly at minimum, home visits if roads fail." She glanced at Noreen's hands. "And I need that ring off before your fingers trap blood. Soap and string tonight."

Noreen laughed once, a dry broken sound. "Who is doing my house, then?"

Keane met her eyes. "Whoever prefers you alive."

Noreen looked at Elsie. Elsie looked back, fierce and frightened and already calculating Peter's dinner, Arthur's tray, the coal bucket, the laundry line, and the whole machinery of two households, all the invisible work that kept men warm and convinced they were in charge.

"My husband has a broken wrist," Noreen said finally. "He can't do much."

"He can do whatever men discover they can do when there is no alternative," Keane said. "Can he boil potatoes one-handed? Yes. Can he fetch coal in smaller loads? Yes. Can he stop spending your pulse on pointless arguments? Also yes, though that is advanced training."

Elsie gave a startled snort she tried to hide as a cough.

Keane pulled a fresh form from the stack and began writing in tight block print. She wrote with green ink, always green, because she said black disappeared in bad light and this town lived in bad light half the year. The nib scratched hard enough to hear.

"I'm filing high-risk note," she said. "If transport worsens, I'll push for day-bed observation at Calder Infirmary. Beds are scarce and we both know it. I still put it in writing. Documentation protects you when people say you're overreacting."

Noreen's throat burned. "Will the baby come early?"

"Maybe. Not always. Preeclampsia can force our hand, or labor can come on its own. Our aim is time without pretending we own time." Keane set the form aside and looked at Noreen as if she were a person and not a case number. "How many weeks now?"

"Thirty-one by my dates."

"Then every day matters."

Silence sat for a beat, broken only by the radiator's hiss and stop.

Keane picked up the cuff again and tightened it. "I'm taking another reading before you leave. I want a post-rest number. Lie down on the couch five minutes. Mrs Harrow, roll that blanket and wedge her ankle." She paused, then added, "And both of you listen carefully. Secrecy kills in obstetrics more often than blood does. If there is anyone blocking access, withholding fare, delaying calls, you tell me now or you tell me after in writing. I can't treat what I'm not allowed to see."

Noreen's mouth opened and closed. Peter's name sat there like a fishbone. Beside her, Elsie had gone very still.

"He didn't want me to come," Noreen said. "He said it was unnecessary expense."

Keane did not flinch. "Did he prevent you?"

"He tried."

"How did you get here?"

"Cab," Elsie said. "Paid from housekeeping."

Keane's gaze shifted between them and landed somewhere beyond both, in the space where legal facts and lived facts refused to line up.

"Right," she said. "Then we work with what exists. I will chart transport barrier and husband opposition to attendance. If he objects, he can object to my face."

Noreen let out a breath she had not noticed she was holding. Relief felt shameful, as if she had betrayed somebody by wanting a witness.

Keane finished the second reading, a fraction lower and still ugly. She wrote it down and tore two slips from her pad.

"One for next clinic Monday, one for district check Saturday if roads permit. If roads don't permit, I still come unless I'm physically blocked. Keep a packed bag by the door from tonight: gown, nappies, towel, any papers. And put a list of emergency symptoms on the mantel where everyone can read it. Not hidden in a drawer."

"Everyone?" Noreen asked.

"Everyone who shares your roof." Keane capped her pen. "You are done pretending this is private discomfort. This is public risk."

Outside the room, the corridor had filled with the noon crowd. Women balanced toddlers on hips, husbands shifted from foot to foot near the payphone, a nurse wheeled a tray of sterilized instruments under a sheet. Life continued with terrible normality.

Noreen sat on the bench by the coat rack while Elsie wrestled her boots back over swollen feet. The leather scraped and stuck. Elsie did not rush. She warmed each boot mouth with her hands first, then eased the heel in inch by inch like fitting a child into a tight sleeve.

"Don't," Noreen said quietly. "You'll hurt your back."

"My back is not the point today." Elsie tied the laces and sat back on her heels. "Can you stand?"

Noreen stood. The floor rolled once and steadied.

When they stepped out under the clinic awning, the sky had gone that flat pewter that meant more snow by evening. The road was a churn of gray slush and horse muck. Elsie looked at the symptom slip in her glove as if it were a summons.

The slip listed headache that did not ease, lights in vision, pain under right ribs, vomiting, sudden swelling, little urine, breathless spells at rest, and fit.

The words were on paper, yes, but they were also tasks now, distances to cover, and doors to get through. It was a list of moments where one minute of delay could be the difference between a fright and a death.

Noreen touched Elsie's sleeve. "Say it."

"What?"

"What you're thinking."

Elsie watched a lorry throw brown spray across the curb, then said, very quiet, "I'm thinking we can't keep doing this like a secret errand. Not with that list in your pocket."

Noreen closed her eyes briefly. The wind cut under her scarf and found the damp at the back of her neck.

"If he knows everything, he will lock the doors," she said.

"If he controls every penny and every call, you'll seize in that kitchen while he argues about fare." Elsie's voice stayed level, but it had gone blunt in the way it did when she crossed her own fear. "I won't have it."

They stood close enough that their sleeves touched, two married women on a public pavement making no gesture anyone could convict and all the decisions that would damn them anyway.

"I owe you," Noreen said, because debt language was the only language the day had offered her.

Elsie shook her head. "You owe me nothing. You pay me back when you can if it helps your pride. That is not what this is."

Noreen gave a thin laugh that stung her throat. "Everything in this street is what it's not."

Elsie folded the symptom slip in half, then half again, and tucked it into the front of Noreen's coat where her hand could find it without searching.

"From now on," Elsie said, "if you get one of these signs, you knock my wall and you don't ask permission first. If you can't get to the wall, Arthur hears a kettle whistle better than any man on this street. He'll come. Keane said no waiting, and I heard her."

Noreen looked at her, really looked, at the pale strain around her mouth and the stubborn set of her jaw and the curve of her own late belly beneath the coat. Elsie was carrying life too, and still she was offering minutes and muscle she did not have to spare.

"This could ruin you," Noreen said.

"Tom already ruined enough. Peter can try."

She said it without drama, as if naming weather.

The next bus groaned into view, windows fogged, steps slick. Elsie climbed first, turned, and took Noreen's elbow with both hands. Noreen felt the careful pressure and understood, with a clarity that hurt, that love had crossed into logistics. It had become coins in wax paper, symptom lists on mantelpieces, who held a door, who answered a knock, who spoke first when a man said no.

Inside, the conductor punched their tickets and moved on. Noreen held the rail and breathed through the ache under her ribs. Elsie sat beside her, watching the street blur by, jaw tight, already sorting what could be shifted in both houses before evening. It was not romance now. It was systems.

On Barrow Lane the walls were thin, the money thinner, and death could come quietly between one cup of tea and the next. Elsie knew that in her bones by the time the bus turned the corner. Whatever they had called this before, whatever private name had kept it tender and hidden, it had changed.

From today, every choice they made about each other was also a medical decision.


# Chapter 13

By four o'clock the frost had climbed halfway up the kitchen window from the inside, feathery and white as cheap lace, and Elsie had already counted the same money three times.

Three pound notes lay flat under the sugar bowl so the draft from the ill-fitted back door would not lift them. A little hill of silver sat to one side, sorted into shillings and sixpences, and the copper had gone green at the edges where damp had got in. She kept a stump of pencil near her elbow and wrote figures on the back of last week's fish wrapper because she had used the clean page from Tom's old notebook for Noreen's clinic times.

Rent first. Coal second. Mrs Bhatti before Saturday, even if it was only a part payment and a promise. She wrote 

`rent 2-6`  
`coal 1-4`  
`Bhatti ?`

and then she stopped, because there was no arithmetic symbol for a man who came home with empty pockets and a grin that wanted to pass for charm.

The house sounded wrong without his tread. She could hear next door clearer than her own hallway. Noreen's kettle lid clicked and settled, clicked and settled again. A muffled male voice followed, Peter's clipped schoolroom tone even through the wall. Then silence, and the kind that meant someone had decided speaking cost too much.

Elsie tucked the money into the tea caddy and shut the lid. Her belly shifted hard to the left under her dress, a slow drag as if the baby had rolled to listen too. She rubbed the side where the heel had pressed and went to stir the pot on the hob, mostly cabbage and two rashers cut thin enough to lie about themselves.

When Tom came in, he banged the front door too loud, as if noise could stand in for confidence. Cold rode in with him, and the wet-wool smell from his overcoat, and ale, and tobacco ground into his scarf. He stamped his boots once and coughed into his fist.

"You should've seen North Bridge," he called, voice bright in that practiced way. "Buses crab-walking like old ladies on Sunday shoes. Whole route was a circus."

Elsie did not answer from the stove. She listened for the jingle of wage coins in his trouser pocket. There was none.

He came into the kitchen, grin set, hair flattened by his cap. "Smells decent in here. You put meat in? Christ, that's luxury."

She set the spoon down. "Post came."

"Oh aye? Anything nice?"

"From Calderbank Depot office. Brown envelope with your name on it. It's on the mantel."

He looked past her toward the front room, then back. The grin stayed but his eyes moved fast, quick as a boy checking whether his hand had been caught in the biscuit tin.

"Probably rota nonsense," he said. "They keep sending paper because they love the sound of themselves."

"Open it."

"Later, Els. Let me sit down first, I'm frozen through."

"Open it while I'm here."

He scratched his jaw, gave a short laugh. "You'd make a decent foreman, you know that?"

She did not move.

Tom sighed and went through to the front room. She heard the envelope tear, then nothing, then the paper fold once, carefully. He came back with it in his hand and an expression he tried to keep blank.

"Administrative thing," he said. "They've made a fuss over a short bag on Tuesday."

"Short by how much?"

"Not much. Bits. A few quid that'll turn up in some bloody drawer."

"How much, Tom?"

He slid into the chair by the table and spread the letter as if the paper itself might defend him. "They're saying fourteen pounds and ten from remittance, and another six and change from a late shift float. It's nonsense."

Elsie stared at him. "Twenty pounds is not bits."

"I didn't say twenty, did I? I said they're saying it. Auditors count one way in the morning, another way by tea."

"Did you take it?"

"I moved money around." He held up a hand before she spoke. "Listen to me first. I moved money because I needed two days to square a private matter. Two days, Els. It got caught because that jobsworth Martin came in early and did his little hero routine."

She could hear her own breath pulling sharp through her nose. "What private matter?"

"Nothing you'd thank me for dragging indoors."

"Try me."

"Bookie pressure." He said it quickly, like spitting out a fish bone. "There. Happy now?"

The kitchen seemed to tilt for a second and then set itself right again. The pot lid rattled and she did not reach for it.

"How much do you owe?"

Tom rubbed both hands over his face, as if he could warm them that way. "It's not a fixed thing. You know how accounts run."

"How much."

"Around one-forty, one-fifty, depending where you freeze it."

She sat down opposite him because her knees had stopped trusting the floor. "You owe one hundred and forty-seven, or near enough, and you stole from the depot to feed it."

"Don't say stole. Christ."

"What word do you want?"

"Borrowed against Friday. I had a line coming in."

"You had a bet."

"A line," he snapped, and then softened at once. "Love, I know how it sounds. But you didn't see the board that day. It was a banker. Anybody would've taken it."

"Anybody with my name on his pram papers too?"

He went still.

She had not planned to bring it in yet, but the line had come up out of her before she could choose it. She watched his face rearrange itself around the new calculation: how much she knew, who had told her, which lie still had room to breathe.

"That again," he said at last. "We sorted that."

"No. You told me not to fuss and I wanted the room to stay standing, so I let you walk away. That's not sorting."

"You're carrying enough as things stand. I kept the uglier bits off you."

"You put the uglier bits in my name." She leaned over the table until the edge pressed hard into her stomach. "Dates, Tom. When did you start taking depot money?"

He swallowed. "Three Fridays back. First time was five quid. Paid it Monday. Then twelve, then..."

"Then?"

"Then it got away from me."

"How many times?"

"Four."

"And who knows?"

"Depot office. Maybe transport police if Martin keeps barking. Nobody's gone to a station yet. This is still internal if I make it right before Monday hearing."

Elsie looked at the letter. The words jumped around, but she picked out enough: `disciplinary suspension`, `pending inquiry`, `attend 8:30 Monday`. At the bottom, typed clean and merciless, `Failure to account for public monies may result in police referral.`

She could taste metal from the kettle steam. "You're suspended."

"Temporary."

"Which means no wages this Friday."

He flinched. "Half pay while they look. Maybe."

"Maybe is not rent."

He slapped his palm flat to the table and forced cheer into his voice again. "All right. That's why we're talking now, eh? That's why I'm straight with you. We fix it before Monday and they can't prove intent. That's the game."

"Game."

"Figure of speech. Don't start hanging me with grammar." He leaned in, palms open, matey grin trying to crawl back. "I've got a route. I need a little bridge money, just enough to put back what they're shouting about. Then I show up clean, say records got crossed in storm overtime, and we're done."

Elsie heard next door's wall clock strike four, muffled and officious. The baby kicked once, a hard knock under her ribs.

"Where does this bridge money come from?"

He hesitated for a beat. "Your ring."

The word landed heavier than the coins in the tea caddy.

Tom rushed on. "Only for a week or two. Alfie Dent gives fair rates if he knows it's me. You pawn it Friday, I clear the short, I buy it back once wages restart. Nobody sees anything."

She turned her left hand over on the table. The wedding band sat loose now because her hands had thinned despite the swelling everywhere else. The gold was worn white at one point where it rubbed against the bucket handle when she scrubbed floors.

"No," she said.

"You haven't even heard the full plan."

"No."

"Elsie, don't be stubborn for pride. It's metal. It's a circle."

"It's mine."

"It's ours." His voice sharpened. "Unless that changed and nobody told me."

She held his gaze. "You want the truth? It changed when you signed my name and called it protection. It changed when you took fare money from a public bag and came home smelling like stout."

He pushed his chair back hard enough for one leg to scrape and shudder on the stone floor. "I came home, didn't I? That's more than some. I came home and told you what was what."

"Because a typed letter did half the telling first."

Tom's mouth twisted. Shame looked like anger on him; she had learned that years ago and still found herself checking for the bottle when he raised his voice.

"You don't get to stand there pure," he said. "You knew cash was tight and still you handed bits over next door every other day. Soup, tins, coal. You think I didn't notice?"

"I kept a pregnant woman fed."

"You've got one in your own house." He jabbed a finger toward her belly and then dropped his hand, as if embarrassed by it. "I'm trying to keep us out of court, Els. Help me do that."

She stood, slow and careful, hands on the table to lever herself upright. "Tell me everything before you ask me to bleed for it. Names. Amounts. Who's pressing you."

He stared at her, breathing through his mouth.

"Billy Cragg runs the book for this side," he said finally. "His runner's little Len with the cracked front tooth. I owed sixty-two before Christmas, then chased losses. Got to one-forty-seven by this Tuesday. Cragg wanted twenty by Thursday night or he'd start calling here. I took depot money to keep him off the door. Lost again on Saturday match that never should've turned. There. That's everything fit to print."

Elsie pressed her fingers against the table edge until the varnish bit into her skin. The numbers lined up now. They made a shape she hated because it looked solid.

"You put us under a man who sends boys to doors," she said.

"He sends reminders, not knives. Don't make it East End melodrama."

"And the depot hearing?"

"Monday morning."

"And if you can't replace it?"

He looked away first. "Then it's transport police. Maybe court. Maybe prison, if they fancy an example."

The kettle hissed dry enough to scream. Elsie reached past him and lifted it off the ring.

"Tea?" she asked, voice flat.

Tom blinked. "What?"

"You're shaking. Tea won't fix theft, but it'll stop your hands rattling long enough to listen." She set the kettle down and faced him again. "We're not done."

*

She did not put milk in either cup. There was half an inch left in the bottle for morning and she had made that decision before he asked.

Tom drank too hot and burned his tongue and swore at the cup. By the time he slammed it down, the window had gone dark and the cold had thickened in the corners.

"Right," he said. "Here's the bit you won't like."

Elsie sat opposite with her cardigan buttoned to her throat, hands in her lap so he could not see them tremble.

"You've already done that bit."

"No, listen. Ring gets us maybe twenty-five. I can raise another five off Stan."

"Stan?"

"My brother."

"The one who still owes you from Easter?"

"He'll cough if it's family disgrace on the line."

"He'll cough if you tell him a version with you as victim."

"Call it what you like. Between that and a story at Monday hearing, we can contain it." He leaned forward, lowering his voice as if the wall itself took notes. "If they ask where short money went, you say you had me hold housekeeping cash because you were worried about thieves in the lane. You say I mixed bundles by mistake with depot takings during overtime. With weather chaos, they might buy that."

Elsie felt heat rise up her spine, not from the stove. "You want me to lie to your employer."

"I want you to keep your husband out of a cell."

"By putting me in the lie with you."

"You're my wife."

"Wives aren't laundries."

Tom gave a short, disbelieving laugh. "Now who's doing slogans?"

She leaned in, voice low. "You're not taking this ring. And I'm not standing in front of depot men to say cash from my kitchen ended up in your remittance bag by magic."

"Don't talk like you're above this," he snapped. "If I go down, you go down socially anyway. You think Barrow Lane will pat your hand? They'll call you the thief's woman. They'll call the baby thief's blood."

"They already call me plenty when I buy on credit."

"This is different and you know it." He pointed toward the front room where their wedding photograph sat on the mantel, silver frame gone black at the corners. "That ring says you're with me. If you keep it on while refusing to use it, that's not marriage, that's theatre."

The urge to throw the cup at him came so suddenly she almost smiled at it. She set the cup down instead, carefully, and laced her fingers together until the knuckles hurt.

"No," she said. "That ring says I did my part. I kept this house warm when there was no coal. I kept food moving when your wages wandered. I took shame in spoonfuls because I thought we were still on the same side. You made me collateral and never asked. So no, Tom. You're not using this as a loyalty test."

He rubbed his hands over his scalp, flattening his hair and lifting it again in the same motion. "Christ, Els, don't make me beg in my own kitchen."

"Then don't beg. Listen." She counted on her fingers. "One: no pawning ring. Two: no lie with my name in it. Three: tomorrow you go to Stan and raise what you can without forging, without gambling, without me. Four: Monday you tell depot hearing you took the money and why."

"You're insane." He laughed once, sharp as a bark. "You just wrote my conviction speech."

"I wrote the only path where this stops growing teeth."

"You want me to confess intent?"

"You already had intent. That's what theft is."

"Don't use that word at me again."

"Then stop earning it."

The silence that followed had weight. Outside, someone dragged a dustbin along the frozen flags and the metal shrieked all the way to the corner.

Tom stood and began pacing between stove and sink, three strides each way. "All right. All right. Alternative. You pawn ring yourself so nobody ties it to me. You keep ticket. If everything settles I buy back and you get to say you saved us. Everybody wins."

"No."

"You'd rather nurse a principle while transport police knock?"

"I'd rather stay out of a criminal statement I can't control."

"You think truth protects you?" He turned, face blotched with cold and rage. "Truth's for men with solicitors. People like us survive on stories that pass inspection."

"Your stories are why we're here."

He stopped pacing. "Say the line," he said.

"What line?"

"Say you'll stand with me. That's all I need."

"I'm standing with the part of you that can still tell the truth."

"Don't give me chapel talk."

"I'm not in a chapel."

He stepped closer until she could smell stale ale sweetening on his breath. "Then say it plain. You with me or not?"

Elsie met his eyes and felt the room narrow to the width of the table between them.

"Not like this," she said. "Not through theft. Not through my ring. Not through my mouth lying for you."

He stared as if he had misheard a foreign language.

"After all this," he said, quiet now, almost gentle, which frightened her more than the shouting had. "After years, you'd choose a piece of gold over me."

"I'm choosing the line where this house stops being your hiding place."

He gave her a look she had seen only once before, when the stillbirth had ended with clean sheets and no baby cry and he had stood at the foot of the bed unable to speak. Hurt and fury braided together until they were one rope.

"Fine," he said. "You want lines? Here's one. If I walk out tonight, I don't carry this rent next week. I don't carry coal. I don't carry your little social smile with the neighbors either. You can explain my absence however clever you like."

"That's not a line. That's a threat."

"It's arithmetic." He snatched his cup and hurled the dregs into the sink. "You think you've been doing sums alone?"

She got to her feet too fast and had to catch the table edge. Pain pulled low across her abdomen, a hard belt, then eased.

Tom saw it and softened for one beat. "Sit down," he muttered. "Don't start anything with the baby because of this."
