"Don't make my pregnancy your argument." Her voice dropped until it almost vanished. "You want to leave, go. You want to stay, you stay under terms I can live with."

"Terms." He laughed again, too loud. "You've turned into a solicitor overnight."

"No. I turned into a witness."

He flinched as if she had slapped him.

"Jesus," he said. "Who are you now?"

She thought of Noreen's swollen ankles in clinic shoes, of Keane's dry voice in the hallway yesterday: `If she seizes in this weather, you may not get a second chance.` She thought of the pram receipt with her forged name. She thought of the baby inside her and how little room was left for pretending.

"I'm the person left to clear this when you've spent your charm," she said.

For a moment he looked ready to strike the table again, maybe the wall, maybe anything that sounded louder than shame. Instead he grabbed his coat from the hook, shoved his arms into it, and headed for the front room.

Drawers opened. Shut. Opened again.

Elsie followed, one hand on the hallway wallpaper to steady herself. "What are you doing?"

"You said go. I'm going. Stan's got a settee."

"Take your own clothes. Leave the ration book."

He turned with a shirt half-hanging from his bag. "Don't be ridiculous."

"Ration book stays here."

"I need my coupons."

"You can get replacements through your brother if you're out of household. You don't take this house's food with you."

His mouth opened, shut. "You're serious."

"About food? Always."

He swore under his breath and threw the book onto the sideboard. It skidded into the clock.

"Happy now?"

"No."

He jammed socks and a shaving tin into the bag, grabbed his bus cap from the peg, and paused at the door with his hand on the latch.

"When they come asking," he said without turning, "remember you had a chance to help me make this small."

Elsie's hand went to her ring by reflex. She pressed the band into her palm until the edge marked her skin.

"When they come asking," she said, "I'll tell what I know and no more."

He made a noise that could have been a laugh or a choke, then yanked the door open.

Cold blew in sharp enough to sting her teeth. His boots pounded down the two steps, suitcase knocking against his knee, and then the gate banged once, twice, like punctuation.

The house settled around the silence he left.

*

For five full minutes Elsie did not move. She stood in the hallway with one hand on the peeled banister and listened to every sound she had not noticed while he was filling them: the tick of the clock, the soft simmer from the kitchen, a cough through the shared wall, the steady drip where thaw had started to find a crack above the front window.

Then she went to the table and opened the tea caddy.

Three pounds, twelve and six in silver, one and nine in copper. Not enough for rent and coal together. Not enough if Monday brought a police knock and a landlord with opinions. She drew Tom's letter toward her and laid it beside her own scrap list.

She added a new column:

`if no Tom wage`

Her pencil dug so hard it tore the paper.

`rent short 0-14`  
`coal half scuttle left`  
`milk 2 days`  
`clinic fare for Nora ?`

She stared at the last line until the word blurred. She crossed out `Nora` and wrote `Noreen`, as if the full name might make the problem sturdier.

From next door came a faint knock on the shared wall: two taps, pause, one tap. Their new code since chapter ten's quiet mornings, a question dressed as plaster.

Are you there?

Elsie tapped back once.

In a minute the back latch lifted and Noreen slipped in through the scullery door, wrapped in a grey cardigan worn inside out so the seam would not bite her swollen wrists. She carried an enamel mug and a folded dish towel under one arm. Her hair was pinned up badly, escaping in damp curls at her neck, and one slipper heel had been crushed flat from being stepped on in a hurry.

"I heard the gate," Noreen said quietly. "And him saying Christ knows what in the lane. You all right?"

Elsie nodded, then shook her head because there was no useful shape for the truth.

Noreen set the mug on the table. "Barley broth. I put too much pepper in because Peter won't eat it that way. Lucky you."

Even now, even with her ankles puffed above her socks and a bruise-yellow tiredness under both eyes, she found room for mean little jokes like that. It was one of the things Elsie trusted most in her.

"Sit," Noreen said. "You're white as the sink."

"I'm fine."

"You're vertical. That's not the same."

Elsie sat.

Noreen lowered herself onto the opposite chair with both hands braced on the table, breath hissing once as her back protested. She glanced toward the front hall, then back. "Did he hurt you?"

"No." Elsie touched her ring again before she could stop herself. "He wanted this pawned. Wanted me to lie for him at depot hearing."

Noreen's face hardened, then flickered with something like pity for Tom before she smothered it. "And?"

"And he stole from work to pay gambling debt. About twenty from depot short bag so far. Owes bookie one-forty-seven. Hearing Monday."

Noreen let out a breath through her teeth. "Sweet Jesus."

"He said if I stood with him we'd make it small."

"By making you an accessory." Noreen rubbed her lower belly absentmindedly, fingertips tracing slow circles through the wool, the way she did when worry climbed too high. "What did you say?"

"No ring. No lie. Confess or don't, but without me in it."

Noreen gave a tiny nod, then reached into her cardigan pocket and pulled out a wrapped eucalyptus sweet. She always kept one there because the iron tablets made her gag and mint made it worse. She unwrapped it, sniffed, and set it on the table without eating it.

"So he left," she said.

"Settee at his brother's."

"Then he left." Noreen's voice went flat on the word, no soft edges. "That's the fact."

Elsie felt tears sting and hated them instantly. "I keep hearing the gate."

"You'll hear it all night." Noreen pushed the mug closer. "Drink. Then we sort what tomorrow looks like, not what your heart thinks tonight should've been."

Elsie swallowed broth that tasted of pepper and bay and cheap pearl barley, thick enough to feel like food. Warmth spread into the hollow behind her ribs where panic had sat all evening.

"I don't know what to tell people," she said.

"Tell them what keeps you safest." Noreen shrugged, grimacing when the movement pulled her side. "Night rotations, brother ill, depot overtime, choose one and repeat it till spring."

"If police come, story falls apart."

"Then you don't start with lies you can't carry. Keep it narrow." Noreen counted on her fingers the way Keane did with clinical instructions. "You know he had debt. You know he left after an argument. You don't know his movements after he went. That's true."

Elsie stared at her. "You're good at this now."

Noreen barked a laugh. "Practice."

For a moment they sat in the kitchen's thin warmth, shoulders hunched against drafts, the wall clock ticking them forward whether they were ready or not.

"Peter knows?" Elsie asked.

"He knows there was shouting. He pretended not to hear words because hearing words means obligations." Noreen reached for the eucalyptus sweet, then put it back untouched. "He'll ask in the morning and I'll tell him as little as possible. Arthur heard the gate too, likely. He was in the spare room mending that ridiculous cuff of his where sparks burned it again."

Elsie pictured Arthur with his frost-cracked knuckles and patient quiet, stitching work shirt cuffs with electrician's tape on the table because thread kept snapping in the damp. Another witness, another set of eyes she had not chosen.

"Social cover's gone," she said. "It's one thing when a husband vanishes for a night. Bag in hand is another."

"Aye." Noreen looked at her directly. "And cover isn't food."

Elsie let that sit.

Noreen leaned back carefully, one hand at her lower spine. "Let's do sums. What have you got cash and coin?"

"Three twelve and six, plus coppers."

"Rent's two-six."

"And coal's one-four if Langley still has sacks."

"Then rent gets paid first so nobody has legal reason to enter your rooms." Noreen tapped the table with one finger, brisk now, practical. "I'll stretch at ours for a week and walk to Bhatti with you Saturday. She'll carry tab if she can see your face and not just Tom's promises."

"You need your own food. Keane said salt down, rest up, no drama."

Noreen rolled her eyes. "Keane says many correct things in one breath. Rest is the one this street never stocked."

"Nora."

"I know." She pressed her lips together and nodded once. "I know. I've been seeing little lights at the edge again when I stand too quick. I won't pretend." She gave Elsie a look half-defiant, half-weary. "But I'm not leaving you alone in this kitchen tonight with that face."

Elsie reached across and laid her hand over Noreen's. The skin was warm and dry, knuckles rough from washing nappies in water that never stayed hot long enough.

"Thank you," Elsie said.

Noreen's thumb moved once under her palm, a private stroke gone as soon as it happened.

From next door Peter called, voice formal even in irritation. "Noreen? Where have you gone?"

Noreen closed her eyes for a second. "There he comes, as reliable as tax." She stood, using the table as leverage, and winced. "I'll come back first light. We make a script before milk rounds."

At the door she turned. "Hide the ring somewhere stupid, not obvious. Men always check sewing tin first."

Despite everything, Elsie gave a breath of laughter. "Where then?"

"Flour bin if you've got any flour left. Nobody digs there unless they're saints or starving."

When Noreen had gone and the scullery latch clicked, Elsie took the ring off for the first time since her wedding day.

The pale line beneath it looked indecent, skin that had not seen air in ten years. She held the ring between finger and thumb, felt its heat fade, then walked to the larder and pushed it deep into the last three inches of flour in the tin, under a folded tea cloth so she could not see the spot and make herself mad by checking every minute.

She washed the cup, banked the fire, and laid Tom's depot letter flat under the breadboard where damp would not curl it.

At half six, Mrs Pritchard from the end house knocked to return the enamel pie dish from Christmas and let her eyes travel once over the hallway where Tom's boots were missing.

"Quiet in here," Mrs Pritchard said.

"Tom's with his brother tonight," Elsie said, voice neutral. "Family matter."

"Oh." Mrs Pritchard held the dish a little tighter. "Right. Well. If you need paraffin, our lad got an extra can."

The offer carried curiosity like a second handle.

"Thank you," Elsie said. "I'll remember."

After she shut the door, she leaned her forehead against the cold wood and counted to twenty.

By eight the street had gone muffled again, snow under fresh crust, distant shouts from the pub corner blown flat by wind. Elsie sat at the table with the ration book open and a list of meals for seven days written in her careful hand: cabbage broth twice, potatoes and onion if Bhatti had any, barley stretch, bread heels toasted, egg only if she could trade for one.

She added one more line:

`tell no lies you can't keep straight`

She took the pencil to a fresh scrap and copied what she would say if Monday brought questions.

He left Friday evening after an argument about money.  
He told me he was going to his brother Stan's settee.  
I don't handle his depot wages.  
I won't speculate on his work accounts.

In bed, the mattress felt too wide and too cold on the side where he had slept. The springs complained every time she turned. Through the wall she heard Noreen vomiting quietly into a basin, then the soft murmur of water, then Arthur's low voice in the hallway offering to fetch more coal bricks from the shed. Peter answered with a curt no that meant yes if no one named it that.

Elsie lay on her left side and watched the moonlight make bars across the ceiling through frost-scratched glass. Her baby moved once, then settled.

She did not pray. She made plans.

If Tom came back drunk, bolt first and speak through wood. If police came, kettle on, chairs out, no shaking hands on display. If Stan came with messages, ask for cash not apologies. If gossip started, let it tire itself against silence.

Near midnight the street went still enough that she could hear meltwater ticking from the gutter into the blocked drain, one drop, two, then a run.

By morning she would be a woman with a husband elsewhere. By Monday she might be a thief's wife in every mouth on Barrow Lane. By next week, if Tom's wages stopped fully, she would be choosing between coal and clinic fares with a child pressing down in her own body and another neighbor's blood pressure rising next door.

She put her hand over the hard curve of her belly and spoke into the dark, not loud enough for anyone else to hear.

"We'll keep the door," she said. "We'll keep the truth as near to plain as this street allows."

The words sounded small in the room, but they held.


# Chapter 14

Noreen woke to the sound of rain that was not rain.

It was ice gritting against the front window, a dry furious hiss, and the window answered with little clicks like teeth. The coal in the range had sunk to a red eye. Her feet were already swollen before she put them on the rug, the skin stretched shiny where her slippers rubbed. She sat up slowly because the headache from the clinic warning had come back behind her right eye, a steel pin turned one quarter at a time.

The first pain crossed her belly while she reached for the chamber pot. It did not rise and fall. It took hold low and deep and dragged her breath out of her like someone pulling wet linen through a wringer. She stayed bent over the mattress until it eased. Then she counted. Keane had told her to count anything she could trust: clock ticks, kettle whistles, the distance between pains. Numbers did not panic.

She got to six minutes. Then five. On the third pain she stood and warm water spilled down her calf, clear at first, then clouded pink when it hit her heel.

"Elsie," she said to the wall before she remembered a wall did not answer.

Arthur was in the spare room on the other side of the landing. She knocked once and he was up at once, boots in his hand, shirt half buttoned. He slept in a wool cap with a rent at the crown because the back room chimney drew wrong and dropped soot on the pillows.

"It's started," she said. "It's not practice. It's proper." 

Arthur did not ask whether she was sure. He looked at the water on her nightdress hem and nodded once. "I'll fetch Mrs Harrow and ring Keane. Phone at the substation still works if the line hasn't iced." He jammed his cap down and glanced at her feet. "You want the fire up now?"

"Yes. And the kettle. Two kettles if they boil." She gripped the bedpost until another pain climbed through her spine. "Where's Peter?"

"Town Hall side. He said he'd walk back after the late ledger shift." Arthur's mouth flattened. "No one walks quick in this."

He was gone before she could answer. She heard his tread on the stairs, then the front latch, then the storm swallowing him. Noreen braced both hands against her belly and breathed the way Keane had shown her, in through teeth, out in a slow thread that made room for the next one.

Elsie came through the back door in Tom's old duffel, her own coat hanging open over her stomach because nothing buttoned now. She had wrapped a scarf over her hair and forgotten to tie the ends, so the wool tails swung when she moved. Her face was pale in the firelight, the color of skimmed milk, but her hands were steady.

"I'm here," Elsie said. "Arthur's gone for Keane and ambulance. Sit. No, don't sit that far back, you'll trap your tailbone. Here." 

Noreen laughed once at that and the laugh broke in the middle because the next contraction had started. Elsie crouched in front of her and put both palms on Noreen's knees, grounding them. She smelled of smoke, Pears soap, and the sharp iron scent that came when snow hit hot coal.

"You're shaking," Noreen said when it passed.

"Course I'm shaking. Doesn't matter. Tell me how far apart."

"Five. Then maybe less. My water went." Noreen touched her own mouth as if she could keep words from spilling too quickly. "My head's bad again. I've got lights on the edge."

Elsie swore under her breath, a small hard word. "Keane said headache with lights means no waiting about. We'll set the front room and keep you warm. Have you bled much?"

"Only streaks so far." 

Elsie nodded and stood with one hand braced at her back. The movement cost her; Noreen saw it in the pause at the top, that tiny freeze while she caught her own breath around her own child. Then Elsie was all task.

She pulled sheets from the airing cupboard, rejected the first set for damp, took the older coarse pair that had dried nearest the grate. She sent Noreen to the privy before pains grew stronger, then changed her into Peter's old clean shirt because buttons were easier than a nightdress knot with wet hands. She brought towels from both houses, counted them out loud, and folded them by size on the sideboard.

"These small ones are for the baby. The large ones are for you. Clean rags go in this basin. Dirty ones go in the coal scuttle once it's empty. Don't argue."

"You're bossy when frightened," Noreen said, breathless.

"I'm bossy when there isn't time."

Another contraction hit and Noreen leaned into Elsie's shoulder. Through the wall, the Harrow clock struck two. Somewhere on Barrow Lane a bottle crate blew over and clattered down the cobbles until it smashed.

Arthur came back twenty-five minutes later white to the brows, eyelashes crusted with frost, carrying Keane's canvas bag and a paraffin tin under his arm.

"She'll be five minutes behind me," he said. "She made me take these because she said I'd drop her and not the bag. Ambulance knows. They said maybe an hour, maybe more. They've got a jackknifed lorry near the viaduct."

"Did you get through clear?" Elsie asked.

"Clear enough. Operator had to patch me twice." He put the paraffin by the range and flexed his fingers one by one, as if checking each still belonged to him. The knuckles were split and dark where old skin had opened again in the cold. "I primed the spare lamp. Want it in here or landing?"

"In here," Noreen said before Elsie could answer. "I don't want shadows tonight."

He nodded and lit the lamp. His hands stopped shaking only when flame steadied in the glass.

Keane arrived as promised, cheeks raw from wind, hair escaping her pins in silver loops that had gone damp and then stiff. She carried her own boots in one hand for the last step over the threshold and came in stocking-footed so she would not track slush to the bed. The socks were men's grey work socks darned three times at the heel.

"All right," she said, not loud, not soothing. "Tell me what happened and when."

Noreen gave her the times between breaths. Keane listened with the brass watch she wore pinned upside down on her bodice, then warmed the stethoscope head inside her sleeve before setting it on Noreen's skin. She always did that. Noreen had noticed it first at clinic and nearly cried from gratitude at that tiny refusal to make a woman flinch.

Keane examined her quickly, efficiently, eyes narrowing only once. "You're in established labor. Early for dates, yes, but labor all the same. We don't move you unless I lose either heartbeat. Roads are a hazard and you've got pressure signs. We deliver here."

Noreen nodded because there was nothing else to do. Elsie asked, "How early?"

"Likely thirty-four weeks, maybe a shade less. Small baby expected. Cold is our second enemy, bleeding is third. First enemy is delay." Keane snapped open the bag. "Arthur, boil everything that can be boiled. Knife, scissors, enamel bowl. Keep a rolling kettle, not a polite one. Elsie, I need you gloved and ready with me."

"I've got washing gloves," Elsie said.

"Good. Wash your forearms to the elbow with carbolic. Nails now. Noreen, pass urine if you can. Empty bladder gives me room."

Another pain took Noreen and she gripped the mattress ticking. She could smell carbolic soap, paraffin, wet wool, and beneath all of it the old sour mattress heat of the room. Keane spoke over the contraction in short steps she could hold onto.

"Breathe down low. Keep your jaw loose. Don't waste it in your throat. Save it for the push when I ask." 

From the front room Arthur fed the fire until the grate glowed orange-white. Coal was low. He split two broken chair rungs and fed those too, muttering apologies to the furniture like it might forgive him. Every few minutes he stepped to the back door and listened for engine noise that never came.

By three o'clock pains were under four minutes and biting hard. Noreen labored kneeling over the back of a dining chair because lying flat made her dizzy and made the pin behind her eye drill deeper. Sweat ran down her spine though the room stayed cold at the skirting boards. Elsie stood behind her, fist pressed into the small of her back through the shirt, counting breaths in a calm voice that thinned only at the ends.

"Three in, six out. Again. Three in, six out. You've done this all week with the stairs. Same lungs."

"Stairs don't split a woman in two," Noreen said.

"No," Elsie said. "But you swore at those as well and beat them."

Keane checked the fetal heart between contractions, shifting the horn and frowning at the rhythm. "There are dips on the tail. It's not catastrophic yet. Keep going."

Outside, wind hammered the guttering so hard that soot rained down the chimney breast. Arthur set a pan lid over the nursery basin to keep grit out and went back to the door. He had moved from question to routine, and routine was how he managed fear: check lamp, check fire, check road, check kettle, repeat.

At half past three, Noreen vomited thin tea into the basin and immediately apologized. Keane ignored the apology.

"Good," she said. "Body's working. You're near transition."

Noreen wanted to strike her for the word good. She wanted to sleep standing up. She wanted Peter there and not there at once. Another contraction cut through thought and left only noise, a low animal sound she did not recognize as hers until Elsie said, "That's right, use it." 

The front door banged. Arthur went to it and came back with two ambulance men who looked more like dockworkers than saviors, both red-faced from the cold and carrying equipment wrapped in army blankets. One of them had half his moustache singed off, a clean crescent over his lip where he had leaned too near a heater some other night. He said, "Road's shut at the top. We parked by the chapel and hauled this lot on a handcart." 

Keane did not look up from Noreen's knees. "Good. Set oxygen by the hearth. Bring your transport cot in and warm it. Baby will likely need transfer at once." 

"And mother?" the other man asked.

"Mother stays till she's stable. You don't get both in one trip with those roads." 

The man glanced toward Noreen and then away, not from squeamishness but from arithmetic. He was counting distance, snow depth, fuel, and time.

Noreen was already past embarrassment. She clenched the chair back and pushed when Keane told her, not before. The room narrowed to six commands: breathe, hold, curl, push, pant, rest. Elsie's hand never left her shoulder.

She smelled singed wool and realized belatedly that Elsie had leaned too close to the fire, coat sleeve smoking at the cuff. Arthur slapped it out without comment and moved the coal shovel farther from her foot.

"Don't you dare catch fire in my house," Noreen gasped.

"Bad timing, that," Elsie said, and Noreen laughed again, then bore down into another contraction so fierce it seemed to pry her pelvis apart with iron bars.

Keane's voice sharpened. "Head's there. Slow now. No heroics. Pant."

Noreen panted, cursed, panted again. She felt the burn and then a sliding release that emptied her in one hot rush. Keane's hands moved fast. The room went quiet for half a second that lasted a year.

Then a small wet cough.

It was not a cry. It was a cough like a kitten bringing up milk.

"Baby's out," Keane said. "The baby's tiny, very tiny. Come on, little one, breathe for me." 

She rubbed the baby's back with a rough towel, suctioned the mouth and nose with a rubber bulb, rubbed again. The baby's skin was blue at the lips, red-black at the knuckles, the limbs no thicker than Elsie's wrist. Keane clamped and cut the cord, handed the child to the singed-moustache ambulance man at the warming cot, then changed her mind and took the child back to give two measured breaths through a tiny mask, counting under her breath.

"One and two. Pause. Again." 

A weak cry came then, high and reedy, and Noreen's whole body jerked toward the sound.

"Let me see." It came out hoarse, less command than plea.

Keane brought the baby close enough that Noreen could touch with two fingers. The child's foot was no larger than a matchbox, heel translucent. Noreen slid a fingertip along the sole once, as lightly as if she might tear it.

"You're here," she said. "Stay here."

The ambulance man fixed an identification band around the tiny ankle and another around Noreen's wrist, a strip of stiff paper inside plastic, numbers written in blunt pencil because ink froze. The band was too big on her from edema gone to slack. It rattled when she moved.

"Special care unit," Keane said. "They're set for prem babies tonight. You won't be with the baby straight off. Hear me? That's not abandonment. That's treatment."

Noreen nodded, tears and sweat and hair all the same wet now. "Tell them I sing off-key," she said to nobody and everybody. "Tell them the baby can complain about that later." 

Elsie bent and kissed her forehead, quick and fierce. "I'll tell them worse than that."

The ambulance men wrapped the baby in warmed blankets around a hot-water bottle tucked safe at the feet and lifted the transport cot. The weak cry faded down the hallway and out into storm. Noreen turned her face toward the door until she could not hear it at all.

Then she felt blood pour out of her.

It was warm at first, then cooling as it spread under her hips. It was too much, and it came too fast. Keane's hand was between her legs instantly, pressing, assessing.

"Placenta not complete," Keane said. "And we're flooding. Elsie, gloves now. Arthur, fresh boiling water and every clean towel left. No one panics."

No one panics, Noreen thought, and began to shake so hard her teeth knocked.

Keane's voice dropped lower, more deliberate, the cadence she used when systems failed and only hands remained.

"Noreen, listen to me. You're hemorrhaging. I need your permission to do painful things quickly."

"Do it," Noreen said.

"Say yes clearly."

"Yes. Do it."

Keane gave an injection into Noreen's thigh, ergometrine from a glass ampoule she snapped with her teeth because her fingers were wet. She massaged Noreen's uterus hard through the abdomen, knuckles pressing deep until Noreen screamed and nearly bucked off the bed.

"Stay with me," Keane said. "Pain means muscle's waking. This is good pain now. This is good pain." 

Elsie climbed onto the mattress behind Noreen, bracing her shoulders with her own body despite the weight of her belly. "Bite this," she said, pushing a folded towel to Noreen's mouth. "Not your tongue."

Noreen bit and tasted bleach and old sun from line-dried cotton. She felt pressure, pulling, another hot gush. Keane worked with relentless hands, not cruel, not kind, exactly enough.

"Placental piece retained," Keane said through clenched teeth. "I'm going in. Hold her steady."

Elsie swore again, softly, and tightened her grip. Arthur stood at the door with a stack of towels and looked as if he wanted to run through a wall for them and found no wall that would help.

He set the towels down and turned his back without leaving, giving privacy where he could in a room that had none. He stared at the wallpaper roses and recited transformer fuse sizes under his breath like prayer.

Noreen left her body for a few seconds at a time. She was on the rug in her mother's kitchen at nine years old, kneading bread with a chipped mug for a cutter. She was sixteen and laughing in the rain outside the dance hall. She was nowhere. Then Keane's hand dragged her back with brutal certainty.

"Look at me," Elsie said into her ear. "Look at my coat button. The blue one. Count the holes."

Noreen focused on the button as if it were a lighthouse lens. It had four holes, and its rim was chipped. The thread was doubled because Elsie never trusted a single line to hold. She counted through another contraction of pain that was not labor now, only survival.

Blood soaked two towels, then three. Keane packed, pressed, waited for tone, pressed again. She asked Arthur to fetch the paraffin lamp closer and then cursed when the flame guttered.

"Fuel's nearly gone," Arthur said.

"Then warm bricks in the grate and wrap them. Keep her from crashing." 

He did it at once, using tongs and old pillowcases, moving like a man handling live wires. The room smelled of hot clay and blood and paraffin smoke thick enough to sting eyes.

At last the flow slowed from gush to heavy seep. Keane kept one hand on Noreen's abdomen, feeling for firmness as if she could hold life in place by will and pressure alone. She checked Noreen's pulse at the neck, counted silently, checked again.

"You're still here," Keane said.

Noreen opened her eyes. "Would've annoyed you if I wasn't," she whispered.

"Very much," Keane said.

Elsie laughed once and then had to sit down hard on the chair because dizziness washed over her. Keane saw it immediately.

"You all right?"

"Just stood too quick." Elsie pressed a palm to her own belly until it passed. "Baby kicked. Didn't like the shouting."

"Your baby can file complaints later," Keane said, echoing Noreen without smiling. "Drink this." 

She handed Elsie sweet tea gone lukewarm. Elsie drank and went back to Noreen's side.

It took another forty minutes for a second ambulance to claw its way into Barrow Lane. The men brought plasma and a stretcher, swearing at the drift in language stripped of poetry. Keane gave report in clipped sequence, each point exact: premature delivery, neonatal cyanosis, transfer initiated, postpartum hemorrhage estimated over a liter, uterotonics given, retained tissue removed, pulse thready then improving, immediate hospital admission required.

They shifted Noreen carefully onto the stretcher. The mattress beneath her looked butchered. Elsie could not stop staring at the shape her blood had made, a dark map branching out toward the bed edge like river deltas in school atlases.

Keane snapped her fingers once near Elsie's face. "Stay with me. Help me with blankets." 

They layered Noreen in wool and two coats. The plastic transfer band knocked against the stretcher rail each time the men lifted, a hard dry clack that sounded indecently small after all that blood.

At the front hall, the door burst inward with another gust and Peter stumbled in, glasses fogged, overcoat crusted white to the thigh. His cast was wrapped in a grocery bag tied with string. He took in the stretcher, the blood on Keane's apron, Elsie's coat, Arthur's face, and his own house reduced to triage.

"What has happened?" he said. Then, louder, "What has happened here?"

Noreen tried to answer and only coughed.

Peter moved toward the stretcher, already assembling authority around himself like a coat. "I am her husband. You should have waited for me before making transfer decisions."

Keane stepped between him and the medics without raising her voice. "You were unavailable. Labor established at approximately two fifteen. Fetal distress worsened by three forty. Infant transferred at four eighteen. Maternal hemorrhage at four twenty-three. I documented each intervention and witness."

Peter's jaw set. "You had no legal right to remove my child from this address without my consent."

"I had clinical duty to prevent death," Keane said. "Your child needed oxygen and heated transport. Your wife needed to not bleed out on this floor." 

He turned to Noreen. "Nora, tell her to stop speaking to me as if I am a bystander."

Noreen looked at him through half-lidded eyes, skin grey with exhaustion. She lifted her wrist with effort and showed him the transfer bracelet.

"You are a bystander," she said. Her voice rasped, but each word landed. "Tonight you are."

Peter stared at the band, then at Elsie, as if he could reorder the room by deciding where to place blame first. He drew himself up, grammar tightening with rage.

"This household has been managed in my absence by people who do not understand consequence. I will address that when she is stabilized."

Arthur spoke before Elsie could. "Consequence is she and the baby are alive. That's what was managed." 

Peter wheeled on him. "You are a lodger under emergency order. Keep to your assigned status."

Arthur shrugged once, tired. "Assigned status tonight was boil water and not let them die. I kept to it." 

Keane handed a folded sheet to one of the ambulance men, then another to Peter. "Here is the transfer destination and the neonatal identifier. Visiting will be restricted until mother is fit. Here is my report copy. If you challenge any part of timeline, challenge me directly at clinic in writing." 

Peter took the paper with his good hand and did not look at it.

"You are overstepping," he said.

"No," Keane said. "I'm stepping exactly where your wife needed somebody to stand."

The stretcher moved. Noreen caught Elsie's sleeve as they passed, fingers weak but insistent.

"Don't let him tidy this," she whispered. "Don't let him turn it into numbers."

Elsie bent close. "I won't. I'll go to the hospital as soon as roads open. I'll bring you news if they won't let you travel." 

Noreen's mouth twitched, an attempt at her usual crooked smile that only half arrived. "Bring proper tea. Hospital tea tastes like mop water." 

"I'll bring leaves, not bags," Elsie said.

The ambulance men carried Noreen into wind and white grit. The door shut behind them on a sudden silence that felt obscene after hours of commands and cries.

Peter stood in the hallway holding papers that shook in his hand despite all his effort to appear still. On the table beside him lay the basin of bloodied towels, the clipped cord, Keane's empty ampoule wrappers. Proof refused to become polite.

Keane stripped off her gloves and dropped them into the scuttle. "Mr Walsh, the fire must stay up. Pipes will freeze if it falls and she'll return to a broken house when she is discharged. You will clean that bedframe with boiled water and carbolic. Don't let neighbors in to gawp. Do you understand?"

He looked at her as if she had insulted his lineage. "I understand instructions perfectly well."

"Good. Then follow these."

She turned to Elsie. "You need to lie down before you fall down. Your face is paper." 

"Not yet, I won't." Elsie was already gathering clean cloths and a bucket. "Not while he's standing there like an inspector."

Peter made a small disbelieving noise. "Mrs Harrow, I would advise restraint in your tone."

Elsie did not look at him. She wrung out a cloth in boiling water and laid it over the mattress stain. Steam rose pink. "I'd advise you to heat your front room before your wife comes home," she said. "Advice for advice." 

Peter opened his mouth. Keane cut him off.

"Enough. She's not your employee." 

For a long second no one moved. Wind rattled the letterbox. Somewhere down the lane a child cried and was hushed. Then Peter stepped aside because there was nowhere else for him to step, and because he had just watched three people do work he had always claimed as his right to direct.

Arthur carried the coal bucket in from the yard and set it down with a grunt. "Last of it," he said. "I'll queue at Bhatti's when she opens, if she opens." 

Keane nodded. "Put it on Peter's tab and mine if needed." 

Peter flinched at that, tiny but visible. It was money again. It was always money. Elsie saw it and filed it away, not out of malice but because this was how the future would be negotiated: receipts, signatures, who stood where at four in the morning when blood was on the floor.

Keane washed her hands in water gone rust-red and wrote one final note on a ruled card, pressing hard enough to tear the paper at the corners. She left the card weighted under the sugar tin where Peter could not pretend he had not seen it.

Before she left for the clinic she crouched by Elsie's chair and spoke low.

"You did well. You followed direction under pressure. If anybody questions that, send them to me."

Elsie nodded. Her hands had begun to tremble now that there was time for tremor. "Will the baby make it?"

Keane did not offer kindness she could not guarantee. "The baby reached special care alive and breathing. That's where certainty ends for tonight." 

She stood, pulled on her boots, and lifted the canvas bag that seemed heavier now though half its supplies were gone. At the door she paused.

"Sleep in shifts," she told Arthur and Elsie. "One ear on the phone if line returns. He'll try to run this as narrative. Don't let him erase sequence." 

Then she was out into the white dark again.

Arthur banked the fire. Peter went into the front parlor and shut the door with measured care, as if gentleness with hinges might repair the rest. Elsie stayed in the bedroom alone a moment longer, cloth in hand, staring at the transfer bracelet number copied on Keane's scrap: tiny digits, blunt pencil, proof of a life carried out before sunrise.

She pressed the cloth harder against the mattress until her shoulders hurt. Through the wall she heard Peter on the telephone at last, voice low and formal, giving names and dates, trying to build the night into a ledger he could survive.

In the bed, the hollow where Noreen had labored was still warm.

Elsie laid her palm there and kept it there until the heat went thin.


# Chapter 15

The ward smelled of boiled sheets, sour milk, and bleach. Noreen woke with her tongue thick as felt and reached for the jug on the locker. Her arm only got halfway there before her stitches pulled and a hot wire of pain ran under her belly. She stopped, breathed through her teeth, and watched the water tremble in the jug from footsteps in the corridor.

Someone was crying two beds down, not loud, just regular. A baby was making a thin lamb sound from behind the nursery doors. Not hers. She knew her own child now by absence, by where the noise did not come from.

The plastic bracelet they had clipped on her at transfer sat against the bone of her wrist: WALSH, FEMALE INFANT, SPECIAL CARE. The letters had rubbed grey where she thumbed them through the night. Her hand still smelled faintly of iodine and old blood. When she lifted the hem of her gown to check the pad, the blood was dark rust now instead of bright, but there was plenty of it. She was still leaking life onto institutional cotton while her daughter lay in a different building under a heat lamp.

A trolley squeaked past the end of the ward. Metal cups. Toast in a covered tin. The woman in the next bed, Mrs Dunn from Sowerby, turned over and pushed her plait off her shoulder with a practiced flick.

"You should ring for tea before they run out," Mrs Dunn said. She had a face full of freckles and a housecoat with tiny blue anchors on it, ridiculous and stubbornly cheerful in March mud weather. "If you wait till matron does rounds you'll get the sad pot."

"I don't want tea," Noreen said.

Mrs Dunn gave her a look over the edge of the blanket, not unkind. "Then ring anyway. Keeps your hand occupied."

Noreen pressed the bell.

Staff Nurse June Firth came in with her cardigan half-buttoned and a men's watch strapped loose over her cuff, the cracked glass mended with clear tape. She always smelled of peppermint because she chewed mints to cover cigarettes between rounds. She took one look at Noreen sitting up and put a palm out as if stopping traffic.

"Easy," she said. "You're not on your feet yet."

"I need to go to special care."

"No."

Noreen swallowed. "You can wheel me."

"No." Firth moved to the end of the bed, checked the chart, and tapped the page with her thumbnail. "You lost a dangerous amount of blood. Your pressure dropped again at four. You fainted trying to stand for the commode. I'm not sending you on two buses and a corridor walk in this state."

"She's there on her own."

"She's not on her own. She's with nurses and a registrar and a machine that's doing exactly what your body can't do this morning."

"She's mine."

Firth softened for half a breath, then hardened again into ward voice. "I know who she's for, Mrs Walsh. I know it every time I write her number. But policy is policy and weather is weather. No maternal transfers until stable mobility, and no exceptions while beds are doubled up. Half this ward's from blocked roads and half special care's from blocked roads. We're all running short."

Noreen looked at the chart without seeing it. "When can I go?"

"If your pulse behaves and you keep food down, maybe forty-eight hours. Maybe longer."

"Maybe," Noreen said, and hated the word.

Firth put two fingers on the bracelet around Noreen's wrist and turned it so the label faced her. "You can send milk on the eleven o'clock run. We can get you started after breakfast. That's not the same thing. I know it's not. It's still something."

She went away before Noreen could answer.

The toast came. Noreen chewed one corner and could not make it pass. Her breasts ached in a heavy, spreading way, skin hot and tight under the gown, as if the body had not received the message that the baby was three miles and three locked doors away. Milk wanted to happen in one room; the child who needed it was happening in another.

By half past nine the ache sharpened. Every small movement dragged. She rang again.

A younger nurse came with a tray: two thick glass bottles, rubber stoppers, labels, and a chipped enamel bowl of hot water. Her name badge said C. MERCER. She had ink on the side of her left hand where she had tested a fountain pen and forgotten to wash it off.

"I'll show you," Mercer said, pulling the curtain. "It's awkward at first. Everybody hates the first go."

"Did she feed at all?"

Mercer shook her head. "Tube feeds. They gave donor for now. Don't look at me like that. Donor keeps tiny ones alive. Your milk will go with the next lot if we can get any."

Noreen sat higher, wincing. Mercer folded a towel, wedged it behind her back, and talked her through it as if teaching someone to mend a torn sleeve: warm cloth first, hand position, press back then forward, do not pinch. The first drops were yellow and sticky and so little they looked like a mistake. Noreen cried anyway, silent, while Mercer pretended not to notice and handed her the bottle.

By the time Keane arrived near eleven, Noreen had filled barely a finger's depth in one bottle and nothing worth capping in the second. Keane shook rain out of her coat onto the lino and set her canvas bag on the chair.

"Do not apologize for quantity," she said before Noreen could speak. "The first day is often this. It comes in proper later."

Keane's chapped hands were pink at the knuckles. Her scarf had come loose and one end was pinned with a brooch shaped like a thistle, dented from years of catches and doors. She looked older than she had in the Walsh kitchen, or maybe hospital lighting scraped everybody down to bone.

"I want to see her," Noreen said.

"I know."

"They keep saying policy like that word should make me grateful."

"It's not for gratitude. It's for queue management and infection control and fear of lawsuits nobody here can afford." Keane pulled the chart closer and read, lips moving. "Your blood pressure's still unstable on standing. Your haemoglobin is very low. If you collapse in a corridor, they lose both of you for an hour while people fetch people. They're trying to avoid that." She looked up. "I'm explaining, not blessing it."

Noreen wiped her face with the heel of her hand. "How is she?"

Keane reached in her bag and took out a folded note, softened with damp from being in her pocket. "Female infant Walsh. Weight four pounds three ounces this morning. Oxygen hood overnight, now in incubator air with saturation acceptable. Mild retractions still. They are watching temperature and sugar."

She delivered it exactly as if reading bus times, each item clipped to fact and then let go. No "she'll be fine." No lies.

"Did she cry?" Noreen asked.

Keane paused. "Yes. Thin, but she did."

That landed harder than any number on the paper.

An orderly came to collect the bottles. Mr Baines, with a railwayman's cap he wore even indoors and fingers permanently brown from carbon paper, held the crate against his hip and counted labels aloud under his breath. He had a stammer on hard consonants, so he tapped the crate with his thumb each time he hit one and waited himself through it.

"W-Walsh," he said, checking the tag twice. "Eleven run. If weather lets, that reaches county by one."

"Can I send a note?" Noreen asked.

Baines looked toward Keane, then back. "One line, love. Not a letter. Sister there says long notes get wet and unread."

Noreen tore the corner off yesterday's menu and wrote with Mercer's pen: Warm her feet if she hates the blanket. She had no idea if newborns hated blankets. She wrote it anyway, because it was a sentence she could place in the same box as the milk.

Baines tucked the scrap under the bottle band and went.

Rain thickened against the windows. Not snow now, but steady brown-water rain that found every crack in old stone and ran down the inside of frames. Thaw weather. The drip from the gutter outside beat time on the sill.

Peter arrived at visiting hour with his hair parted too straight and his right wrist still in plaster, the sling clean as if he had changed it before leaving work. He carried a paper bag of oranges and a file tied with red tape.

"You look better," he said. He stood at the foot of the bed rather than sitting. "Color is returning."

"You can tell from all this flattering light," Noreen said.

He ignored it. "I spoke with admissions and with the consultant's secretary. The present arrangement cannot be altered until you are medically declared mobile. I asked twice. I was very clear."

He laid the file on the blanket, red tape centered like a gift ribbon.

"What is that?"

"Receipts and necessary forms. Taxi to clinic. Additional coal on Tuesday night. Pharmacy charge. Replacement sheets, since ours were ruined. I have put dates against each item so there is no confusion later."

Noreen stared at him. Her body felt hollowed out and packed with sand; she was too tired for fury and too awake for sleep.

"There was blood," she said. "I nearly died."

"Yes." He said it as if confirming an appointment. "And I responded. I am ensuring there is a complete record of what your crisis required from this household."

"From me," she said.

"From us," he said, then corrected himself with careful emphasis. "Primarily from me, because you were incapacitated."

Firth passed the bed at that moment and slowed, reading the room without making a show of it.

Peter lowered his voice but not his diction. "You will need rest and cooperation if you want timely access to the child. The hospital is already wary of disruption. I have represented us as orderly people. Do not undermine that."

The word orderly hung there, scrubbed and heavy. Noreen thought of blood on the Walsh kitchen flags, Keane's hands red to the wrists, Elsie feeding the fire with chair legs when coal ran out. Orderly.

"What does cooperation mean?" she asked.

"It means no scenes. No contradictory instructions to staff. No neighbors intervening in clinical matters."

"You mean Elsie."

"I mean anyone who confuses boundaries." He set the oranges on the locker, one by one. "I am carrying extra burdens and I will expect that to be remembered when decisions are made."

He spoke like a man balancing council accounts after hours, columns neat, all emotion translated into debit and credit so nothing had to be felt by hand.

Noreen looked at his face and, for the first time since she had married him, did not search for the kinder version underneath. She did not think, He is frightened and saying it badly. She thought, He is counting.

Keane stepped back to the bed with deliberate noise from her shoes. "Mrs Walsh needs to express again at one," she said. "Visiting is ten minutes."

Peter gave Keane a tight nod, then returned to Noreen. "I shall come tomorrow with the post and whatever update they provide from special care. Eat your supper. Do not argue with staff."

"Are you going to her?" Noreen asked.

"I went this morning. Incubator row three. She has your mouth."

That was the first useful thing he had said all day. It hit her with such force she had to grip the sheet.

"Did you touch her?"

"Not permitted." He adjusted the sling at his shoulder. "I observed through the hatch."

Observed. Noreen shut her eyes.

When she opened them he had already picked up the red-taped file and retied the knot, as if even in hospital he could not leave a ledger unattended.

After he left, Firth came back with a mug of weak tea and set it near Noreen's hand.

"He is always that tidy?" she asked, not quite casual.

"Only when he wants something."

Firth snorted once. "Then he's like most men with paperwork." She checked Noreen's pulse, fingers warm and brisk. "You're not wrong to hate this, you know. The rules, I mean. People talk like mothers should be grateful for any crumb and never mind the bite marks."

Noreen gave a dry laugh that hurt her stitches. "I don't even get crumbs. I get reports."

"You get reports today," Firth said. "Tomorrow, maybe a wheelchair if your standing pressure behaves. That is not comfort. It is a target."

At one o'clock Mercer returned, and Noreen pressed her thumb into her own sore skin, counted breaths, and filled a little more of the bottle than before. The milk looked thin as rainwater at first, then clouded. A body obeying chemistry when nothing else obeyed.

Outside, somewhere beyond the rain and boiler rattle, a train horn drifted low through the valley. Baines's eleven run would be halfway there by now, her milk sloshing in a crate between other women's names.

She pictured the incubator row from Peter's description and tried to fit a face to the number. Row three. Tiny mouth. Warm her feet.

That evening she took the back page from the hospital menu and folded it into four squares. In the first square she wrote, in pencil stolen from the bedside crossword: What he controls. In the second: What I can carry. In the third: Who will help anyway.

She left the fourth blank.

***

By the time they discharged her, the lane had turned from packed ice to black mud with a skin of grey water on top. Every cart wheel cut a trench and left it full. The thaw made different noise from snow: drip, slap, gutter-rush, doorframes swelling, boots sucking free of muck. Nothing stayed dry.

Walking from the hired car to the Walsh door took less than a minute and felt like crossing a field after surgery. Noreen held the wall with one hand and her belly with the other while Peter paid the driver in exact coins, counting each piece into the man's palm. Her milk leaked through the pad in her brassiere before she reached the hall.

Inside, the house smelled of damp plaster, boiled cabbage, and coal dust. The scullery bucket under the leak was half full and ringing with drops. Arthur Hale's coat hung behind the kitchen door, sleeves patched at both elbows with darker wool. He was at the table rebuilding a torch from parts spread on newspaper, tongue caught in one cheek while he soldered, patient as a watchmaker.

He stood when she came in and wiped his hands on a rag. "Welcome home, Mrs Walsh. If that bucket overflows before supper, shout. I keep forgetting it when the phone rings."

Arthur's voice had the same reserve as before, but he had put a folded square of old blanket on the chair nearest the fire so she could sit without asking. He pretended that was for anyone, not especially for her.

"Thank you," she said.

Peter set her case down by the stairs. "Arthur has been useful during your absence."

Arthur gave Peter a brief nod and returned to the torch, offering no opinion. The solder hissed. Rain ticked at the back window. In the corner, the tin bath they had used for emergency hot water on labor night was scrubbed clean but still warped at the rim.

Noreen sat and let her bones settle. Home was the same shape and no longer her old place inside it.

By habit she listened for the baby's breath from the Moses basket beside the hearth. Silence answered. The basket was there, lined with the yellow flannel she had hemmed in December, waiting and empty.

Peter brought in tea and, with his good hand, arranged everything within reach as if laying out instruments. Cup. Plate. Bread. A folded sheet of foolscap.

"We need a workable schedule," he said.

"I've just come through the door."

"Exactly why we need order immediately. You must express at six, ten, two, and six again. The ten o'clock bottle can go with me to clinic records when I report before work. The two o'clock can go by Arthur if his shift starts late."

Arthur looked up. "Can do, if needed."

Peter continued. "If weather worsens, we use the eleven train run from county through Baines, as before. I have noted all of this."

He tapped the foolscap. Columns and times. Her body reduced to timetable.

Noreen drank the tea. It tasted of metal from the kettle scale. "And if I want to go myself?"

"When cleared by the district nurse and when the fares can be justified." Peter sat opposite her, straight-backed. "We cannot spend blindly now."

"Blindly." She let the word sit between them.

Peter's eyes went to his notes. "I have advanced three pounds six and four since Tuesday night. Coal, transport, linens, pharmacy, extra rations for Arthur while he covered practical tasks. There will also be clinic fares if this drags."

"If this drags," she repeated, and almost laughed.

"Do not start with tone," he said, still calm. "I am describing facts. You require support. Support has cost. Cost creates obligations."

There it was, clean as chalk on a board.

Noreen pressed her thumb against the edge of the teacup until the heat bit. "To whom?"

"To me," Peter said. "And to the stability of this household, which I maintain."

Arthur busied himself with the torch and did not look up. The radio on the shelf crackled through weather warnings and then dropped to static.

A knock came at the back door before Noreen answered. Arthur rose first, opened it, and Elsie stepped in with rain on her coat shoulders and two string bags cutting red lines into her fingers. She looked pale under her hat, and the curve of her own late pregnancy pulled her weight forward when she stood still.

"I brought things," Elsie said, and shook water from her sleeves onto the mat. "Clean wraps from my line, your blue nightdress mended where it split, and those rubber teats Keane said are less awful than the ward ones."

She set the bags down and, without asking, started unloading on the table in practical order: laundered cloths, wrapped soap, two sterilized bottles in newspaper, a stack of cut labels, safety pins in a match tin so they would not rust.

"Mrs Bhatti said you can add to my book for two weeks," Elsie added. "Milk and bread only. No tins unless you ask her yourself. She was fierce about that bit."

"Thank you," Noreen said.

Peter stayed seated. "We are managing, Mrs Harrow."

Elsie did not meet his tone with heat. She kept her eyes on the labels as she straightened them. "Good. This is me helping manage, then."

"Medical arrangements are already in place."

"Aye, I heard all about arrangements from the whole lane." Elsie lifted the mended nightdress by the shoulders and checked the seam against the light. "This house can keep your arrangements. It still needs dry cloth and clean teats."

Peter's jaw tightened. "There is no need for commentary."

"There's rarely a need, and yet here we are," Elsie said, mild as dishwater.

Noreen watched the exchange and felt something inside her settle, like a chair leg finding level on uneven floorboards.

Arthur cleared his throat. "I'll take the two o'clock bottle, Mr Walsh. Past the substation anyway." He clicked the torch on and off once, as if proving its charge.

Peter stood. "Very well. We will keep this efficient."

When he left for the front room to fetch his coat, Elsie moved closer to Noreen and spoke low, eyes still on the bottles.

"Keane says you can ask for a social worker letter if he starts playing gatekeeper with visits. She'll back it. You don't have to decide today."

"He already made a timetable," Noreen said.

"Course he did." Elsie looked up then, her expression sharp and tired and very steady. "You make your own one on top of his. Quietly."

Peter came back in, and Elsie stepped away at once, handing him the list of sterilizing times as if that was all they had discussed.

"I should go before the lane floods proper," she said. "Nora, I'll come tomorrow with more cloth if you need it."

"Come regardless," Noreen said.

Elsie nodded and left. Arthur followed to hold the door against the swollen frame. Rain blew in and spotted the flags.

After supper Peter brought out the foolscap again, now with a second page clipped behind it.

"Housekeeping," he said. "Because your concentration may be affected by blood loss, I have drafted a temporary arrangement. I will hold the ration book and cash. You can request purchases each morning. I will review before leaving for work."

Noreen looked at him for a long moment. "Like a child with pocket money."

"Like a convalescent who cannot handle strain."

"And when I'm no longer a convalescent?"

"When I judge that circumstances are normal."

He did not hear himself. Or heard and did not care.

Noreen signed nothing. She said she was tired and went upstairs before he could convert refusal into argument.

In the bedroom, the wallpaper seam had peeled another inch from damp. She sat on the bed, peeled the wet breast pad from her skin, and hissed through her teeth at the pull. Milk had dried crusty on the cloth. Blood still marked the inside of her thighs where the pad had shifted on the ride home. Her body was all demand and no authority.

She took the folded hospital menu from under her case lining. The pencil line had smudged but held.

What he controls:

wages

ration book

front and back keys if he wants

my clinic fare unless I hide coins

what story he tells people

special care visiting if he keeps acting as messenger

What I can carry:

one case

documents flat in nightdress hem

infant bracelet

two vests, shawl, nappies enough for one day

my own body, if it stops shaking on stairs

Who will help anyway:

Elsie (cloth, cash, walking)

Keane (letters, pressure chart, referrals)

Arthur (quiet lifts, no questions)

Mrs Bhatti (credit, gossip filter if paid respect)

She stared at the blank fourth square until rain blurred the window and the lane lamp came on yellow. Then she wrote: What he does not get.

my gratitude as payment

the whole truth of what I feel

my daughter as proof I stayed obedient

She did not write Elsie's name there. She did not write love or fear or sin or anything from church language. She wrote practical things, because practical things were harder for men like Peter to argue with.

At ten she expressed milk again, kneeling by the bed because sitting hurt less than standing. The bottle took longer this time. She watched each line rise along the glass and imagined it translated into minutes inside an incubator she had not seen.

Peter knocked once and came in without waiting.

"Have you finished?"

"Nearly."

He stood by the wardrobe, sling hanging empty now that his wrist had improved enough to hold the door. "I will take that bottle in the morning. Do not forget the label."

"I won't."

"And Noreen." He hesitated, choosing words like stamps. "I expect a measure of appreciation for what this has required."

She capped the bottle carefully. "You want me to say thank you for keeping score while I bled."

His face tightened. "I want acknowledgment that households do not run on sentiment."

"No," she said quietly. "They run on someone's unpaid labor and someone else's story about who paid for it."

For a second he looked genuinely startled, as if furniture had spoken.

"You are not well enough for this tone," he said.

"I'm well enough."

"Do not mistake exhaustion for moral clarity."

She met his eyes. "Do not mistake accounting for love."

He left without closing the door fully. The draft from the landing moved the curtain in and out like a breath.

Near midnight Arthur tapped lightly and passed a hot brick wrapped in flannel through the gap.

"For your feet," he said. "The pipes are sulking again."

"Thank you."

He shifted his weight, awkward and kind. "Sister at county rang while you were up. Baby took thirty mil through tube at nine. Kept it down."

Noreen pressed the hot brick to her shins and shut her eyes. Thirty mil. Kept it down. More intimate than any blessing she'd heard all week.

"Did she say anything else?"

"She said her hands are long. Like she'll play piano when she's forty."

Noreen laughed softly, then cried at the same time, both noises small.

Arthur pretended to inspect the banister knob until she had herself again. "I'll be off at six. Leave the bottle by the kettle and I'll carry it to Baines if Mr Walsh is delayed."

"You don't owe me that."

"No," he said. "But it's on my way."

After he went, she slid the bottle into the enamel jug with cool water and tucked the paper list inside her pillowcase. She lay down on the side that hurt less, hand around the transfer bracelet, and listened to thaw water running through the gutter like distant applause for nobody in particular.

Before sleep took her she set tomorrow in order: milk at six, label by quarter past, coins hidden in the hem of the green cardigan, ask Keane about letters, ask Elsie for a spare key ring, say nothing unnecessary to Peter, and keep writing.

The train horn sounded again from the valley, long and low through rain. Somewhere beyond Barrow Lane, bottles with women's names were moving through the dark toward warm machines and tiny mouths. Noreen could not hold her daughter yet.

She could still begin.


# Chapter 16

By the time the thaw found Barrow Lane, the house had started to sweat.

Water ticked off the cracked gutter outside the bedroom and ran down the wall behind the wardrobe in a dark seam, steady as a metronome. Noreen stood with one hand on the wardrobe door and one hand pressed under her breast where milk had leaked through the pad again, waiting for Peter to move from the landing so she could pull the lower drawer clear. Her stitches ached when she bent. Her head felt full of hot cloth.

Peter did not move.

He stood in the passage in his shirt sleeves, his casted wrist held close to his ribs as if the plaster itself had rank and dignity. He had set his ledger and correspondence in a stack on the hall table and aligned every corner before breakfast. Now he watched her with that municipal patience he used in arguments, the one that said time belonged to him and she was borrowing it badly.

"What are you looking for?" he asked.

Noreen kept her eyes on the drawer. "A dry nightdress. The damp's got into everything."

"Ask me and I shall hand you one. You are not to rummage through papers while you are still weak."

"I'm not rummaging. I'm trying not to smell like old blood."

He clicked his fountain pen cap open and shut with his good hand, a small sharp sound under the drip outside. He did it when he was holding his temper in a narrow box.

"Mind your language," he said. "This is still my house."

Still. As if he had heard a sentence already and was waiting for the stamp.

Noreen pulled the drawer out anyway. She did it slowly so her belly wall would not seize. Stockings, blouse, two folded towels. Beneath them, wrapped in waxed paper, was what she needed: the clinic card, her ration book, her National Insurance card, the marriage certificate she hated needing, and the cotton tape with the plastic infant transfer bracelet tied through it so she could not lose it in sleep. She slipped the bundle under her cardigan before he could see.

Peter saw.

"Give me that." He held out his good hand. "Now."

"It's my papers."

"Your papers are household papers. You are not an independent filing system."

He stepped in before she could back away and caught the edge of the waxed paper. The cast knocked against her shoulder, a clumsy hard nudge. Not a strike, not something you could show. Just force in a respectable wrapper. The bundle split. Cards and folded pages slid onto the bedspread and one smaller envelope, old sugar-paper, fell face up with Elsie's handwriting on it.

Nora.

Noreen lunged for it. Peter got there first.

He lifted the envelope between two fingers as if it were damp washing. "From Mrs Harrow," he said. "Private, is it?"

"Give it back."

"No."

He tore the flap with his thumbnail and unfolded the single page. Noreen had forgotten one corner carried a grease thumbprint from Elsie's kitchen and a penciled note at the top that had made her smile when she first read it, before the baby came too early, before blood and gauze and iron taste.

I can still feel your mouth on my wrist where you bit me not to cry out.

Peter read that line. Then the rest. His face did not change at once. He read to the bottom and read again, lips moving around each word like an accounts clerk verifying totals.

When he looked up his voice was cleaner than soap.

"You have been fornicating with her. In my house." He folded the page with neat edges. "While I worked. While you carried my child."

Noreen's laugh came out thin and wrong. "You were not here half the nights that mattered."

"Do not answer in riddles."

"Do not talk to me like I'm one of your forms."

His mouth twitched, almost a smile, then flattened. "You have given me what I need."

He set the letter on the bed as though marking evidence. "If you attempt to remove that baby from proper oversight, I will apply for custody immediately. I will state instability after hemorrhage, moral contamination, neglect risk, and association with a known debtor household. I will state immoral acts between women. I will ask that your access be supervised."

The room tipped. Noreen put a hand against the wardrobe to keep from sitting down on the wet floorboards.

"She's in special care," she said. "You'd do that while she's in special care?"

"Especially then. Institutions prefer order."

"You're threatening your own daughter to frighten me."

"I am protecting my daughter from scandal."

His grammar tightened before every cut. She had seen it for years and still, each time, some part of her expected a husband and got a magistrate instead.

"If this reaches parish ears," he continued, "you will not be viewed as a suitable mother. Do you understand what that phrase means in practice?"

She did. It meant signatures she had never seen and rooms she could not enter. It meant someone deciding who held the pram handle. It meant her milk drying in her bra while another woman fed her child from a measured bottle.

"You won't do that," she said, and heard how little weight it had.

"I will do exactly what keeps the name Walsh from filth." He lifted the letter again. "You are to cease all contact with Mrs Harrow. You are to remain here, recover properly, and present as a wife and mother. In return, I will refrain from immediate action."

"In return." She tasted the words like grit. "You make even mercy sound like rent."

"Everything is rent when someone else is paying."

That line, cold and proud, would have crushed her a month earlier. Today it made something sharp and useful in her chest.

"I bled out on this floor while you were missing," she said. "Keane and Elsie kept me alive. The child is alive because of them. You don't get to write that as if you balanced a column."

"I do get to write it. I write records for a living."

He stepped aside and pointed to the open drawer. "Pack that back exactly as it was. You are not to leave this house without me."

He turned and went down the stairs, each step careful because of the cast, the banister creaking under his shoulder. At the bottom he paused and called up without looking at her.

"I shall return from the office at four. We will draft a statement for Father Mullan and for the unit social office. You will sign it."

The front door shut.

Noreen waited until she heard his shoes on the wet pavement, then moved so fast her vision speckled. She shoved the documents into her cardigan, tucked the transfer bracelet into her brassiere where she could feel its hard loop against her sternum, and crossed to the shared wall. She put her mouth near the wallpaper seam and spoke low.

"Elsie."

Nothing.

She hit the wall twice with the heel of her hand, the old signal for coal in chapter one of this winter, when the worst thing between them had been pride.

A kettle lid clattered next door. Then Elsie's voice, close through plaster.

"I'm here."

"He found the letter."

Silence so complete Noreen could hear the lane water running in the gutter trap.

Then Elsie said, careful and quick, words arranged like crockery so nothing would crack, "Put your papers in your coat lining, not the bag. He'll search the bag first. I'll fetch Keane. Don't come out front. Use the yard gate when I knock three times."

"Elsie-"

"Not now. Three knocks."

Noreen rested her forehead against damp wallpaper. Somewhere below, Peter's clock on the mantel struck ten, each chime thin and legal.

She had six hours before he came back with statements and a pen.


By noon the street smelled of wet coal, thaw mud, and cat piss from the alley where the snow had finally loosened. Noreen sat at Elsie's kitchen table with her coat still on, breath short from crossing two yards and one narrow gate as if she had run miles. Every few minutes milk let down in a sudden sting and dampened the front of her dress. Elsie pretended not to look at the stains and set another clean cloth near her elbow each time without comment.

Elsie had tied her hair up with blue parcel string and forgotten to trim the ends, so they stuck out like two stiff tails over her ear. She did that when she was scared: grabbed whatever was nearest and kept moving. Her fingers were reddened from lye soap, one knuckle split open and mended with sticking plaster. When she spoke, she spoke in instructions, not comfort.

"You've got your card?" she asked.

"Yes."

"Ration book?"

"Yes."

"Infant bracelet?"

Noreen touched her chest. "Here."

Elsie nodded once and looked at the saucepan, counting under her breath as if numbers could hold the room level. "I've put bread and dripping in paper for later. Two safety pins in your collar. I stitched twelve shillings into your hem. Not much, but it's there."

"Twelve shillings? Elsie-"

"Don't argue. I sold the good blanket to Mrs Bhatti last week and told you it had moth."

A laugh rose in Noreen and broke into a cough. "You liar."

"Only when useful."

Three hard knocks hit the back door. Keane came in carrying her canvas bag and a damp macintosh folded over one arm, rain beading on the shoulders. She smelled faintly of cloves and antiseptic and had a tea stain on her cuff she never seemed to notice. Her hands were raw at the knuckles, the skin split in white lines from constant washing.

"Right," she said, setting the bag down. "No speeches. Sit still, Noreen. Sleeve up."

She wrapped the cuff around Noreen's arm, stethoscope cold against skin, eyes on the second hand of her watch. The watch had stopped at 3:17 months ago. She used a wind-up kitchen timer clipped to her bag instead and trusted that more than any wristpiece.

"Pressure's high again," Keane said. "Not chapter-twelve high, but high enough with what you've just told me. Bleeding now?"

"Spotting yesterday. Not today."

"Head pain? Light flashes?"

"Sometimes when I stand."

Keane nodded, not surprised, and wrote in block letters on a card. "You can't recover under surveillance and threat. You need supervised lodging now, today, before he files anything that boxes you."

Elsie set mugs down. "Saint Brigid's full. Mrs Denton's niece got turned away Monday."

"I know it's full." Keane took the tea, swallowed once, made a face at the sugar, and swallowed again anyway. "I'm calling Sister Imelda myself."

The back room had no phone. Keane used Elsie's hall where the line worked on clear days and prayed on wet ones. Today it crackled but held. Noreen and Elsie could hear only Keane's side: clipped, polite, then sharpened.

"Yes, Margaret Keane, district midwife, Calderbank patch... No, I know admissions are closed... She is post-hemorrhage, hypertensive, and at active domestic coercion risk... You can call it whatever phrase your board likes; I am telling you she is not safe in that house... Sister, if she seizes there tonight and I have documented refusal of shelter, that is going in my incident report with your name spelled correctly."

A pause. Keane listened, chin tucked, fingers drumming once on the wallpaper, then still.

"Thank you," she said. "One cot, temporary overflow. Curfew eight. No visitors in dormitory. She can manage. She has no infant with her, child is in special care. Yes, I know. Yes. I will sign responsibility at intake."

She hung up and came back to the kitchen.

"You have a place," she said. "Laundry annex cot, not pretty. They want you by half six. Parish van can collect at five-fifteen on return run from the convent linens. Arthur can help load from the lane corner so there is less theatre on your doorstep."

Noreen stared at her hands. The nails were bitten to crescents. Blood from one cuticle had dried black. "How long can I stay?"

"A week confirmed. Maybe two if we can get parish welfare to sign off. After that, they will ask for contribution from maintenance or wages."

"I've no wages."

"Then we file emergency support and maintenance claim. That takes time and he may fight it."

There it was, plain as weather. A bed tonight and nothing promised after.

Noreen felt the room tilt toward absence: no money of her own, no cot for the baby there, no guarantee she could keep visiting special care if bus routes drowned again. Still, there was a door at Saint Brigid she could shut from the inside, and no man with a ledger in the hall.

"I'll go," she said.

Elsie sat down hard opposite her. "You'll go today."

Keane opened her bag and laid forms on the table. "We do this in order. One: property list, so he can't claim theft. Keep it brief. Two: consent for transfer to hostel. Three: note that contact with child remains under maternal rights pending court order. He may bluster. He doesn't get to rewrite law by speaking firmly."

"He'll still try," Noreen said.

"Of course he will."

Keane's voice slowed, each step named, as if she were counting contractions. "When he shouts, don't debate morality. You repeat one line: 'I'm leaving for medical recovery and child welfare.' Don't explain more. Don't answer accusations about your private life. Don't sign anything he has drafted. If he blocks you, Arthur and I witness and we call police if needed."

Elsie looked up. "Police will laugh."

"Perhaps. But two witnesses and a district midwife note make laughter expensive later." Keane met Noreen's eyes. "Do you consent to leave now?"

Noreen nodded.

"Say it." Keane waited.

"I consent to leave today for recovery and my daughter's welfare."

"Good."

Keane pushed the first form across and held out a pencil stub. "Write legibly. Bureaucracy is a weapon, but it cuts both ways when we keep records."

For the next hour they built a departure out of specific nouns: two blouses, one cardigan, nursing pads, ration book, clinic card, a hairbrush with three teeth missing, the infant bracelet, and one photograph of Noreen's mother taken outside a chapel in Galway, rain on the lens like tears she had always denied. Elsie wrapped bread, dripping, and two apples in yesterday's Chronicle. Keane added sterile pads and iron tablets from her bag without writing them down.

When Noreen reached for the drawer where Elsie had once hidden an extra tin of milk, Elsie caught her wrist and shook her head.

"Leave the tin," Elsie said softly. "If he counts, he'll know."

Noreen swallowed. "I hate that you're right."

"You can hate me later."

They checked the clock. Four-ten.

Rain thickened. From the shared wall came the scrape of Peter's chair being set back, then his tread crossing the Walsh hallway. He was early.

Keane snapped her bag shut. "Time. Coat on. Case by the back gate."

Elsie lifted the case and swore under her breath at the weight. "What did you put in here, bricks?"

"A life," Noreen said, and almost smiled.

Elsie did not smile back. She looked like she might break the cup in her hand if she set it down too hard.

"Three knocks," she said. "Then you move."


At five-thirteen the parish van turned into Barrow Lane, pale blue under a skin of road filth, wipers squeaking over glass that never fully cleared. On both sides of the street curtains twitched. Thaw had brought people back to windows. Nothing in a terrace stayed private once the weather loosened.

Arthur was already in the lane mouth in his board-issued coat, cap pulled low, wet wool dark at the shoulders. He worried the brim between thumb and forefinger while he waited, then dropped his hand the moment he saw Noreen with the case. He never offered opinions first; he offered tasks.

"Mind the third step," he said quietly. "Loose stone by the drain. Give me the handle."

He took the case from her without fuss and tested the weight as if gauging a cable load. "Not too bad. Van's clear at the back."

Keane stood by the open side door of the van with a clipboard under her arm, hair escaping its pins in the damp. "Name and date of birth at intake, then straight to bed rest," she said. "Curfew's strict. Sister Imelda will test you by talking soft and writing hard. Answer cleanly, no extra history."

Noreen nodded and turned toward her own front door one last time because she had to collect the second bag, the one with papers sewn into the lining.

Peter filled the doorway before she reached it.

He had changed into his good coat and combed his hair flat. In his left hand he held two sheets of typed paper. In his right, the sugar-paper letter from Elsie, now creased along the same fold lines as his statements.

"You are not stepping into that van," he said.

Noreen stopped three feet from him. Rain tapped the enamel number plate above his head.

"Move," she said.

"No. You are in a temporary condition. You are making decisions under influence." He lifted the letter. "Under her influence."

"Peter-"

"Do not Peter me in the street. Listen carefully." His diction sharpened into church-lectern clarity. "If you proceed, I file tomorrow. I file for custody supervision on grounds of moral unfitness and postpartum instability. I file notice to the unit that all access must be jointly approved. I inform Father Mullan that you have been conducting perversion with a married woman."

From two houses down, a window sash went up a fraction. Somebody coughed and pretended not to listen.
