Noreen felt the transfer bracelet press cold against her skin through blouse and coat. Her fear was not an abstraction. It had a plastic edge and her daughter's surname printed in hospital block letters.

"You are threatening me with my baby," she said.

"I am describing consequences." Peter's voice dropped. "Come inside and this stays within walls. Go with them and every wall in Calderbank will hear."

He glanced at Arthur, then at Keane. "Mr Hale, this is domestic business. Nurse Keane, you are overreaching your remit."

Arthur shifted the case to his other hand. "Road's pooling again by the viaduct," he said to no one in particular. "Driver needs to go now if he's taking the low route."

Keane stepped closer, boots sinking in thaw mud. "Mr Walsh, your wife has consented to medical recovery lodging. I can refer her directly. If you obstruct movement, I document obstruction and notify police and maternity social office tonight."

Peter gave a brief, unbelieving laugh. "Medical recovery at a hostel? Do not insult language."

"Language isn't the issue," Keane said. "Safety is."

He looked back at Noreen and the veneer cracked for a second. Not grief, not love. Rage at losing sequence.

"You will have no income there," he said. "No wages, no housekeeping, no account access. You cannot buy your own bus fare to the unit. You cannot buy soap. You cannot buy milk cloths. She cannot keep you." His eyes flicked toward Elsie's doorway, where Elsie stood in apron and coat, fists white around the knot of her sleeves. "And she cannot rescue herself."

Elsie stepped down one stair. "I didn't ask-"

Noreen cut across before Peter could turn this into another spectacle. "Stop." Her voice shook once, then steadied. "I know I might have no money. I know I might get turned out in a week. I know what you're going to call me."

Peter lifted his chin. "Then act sensibly."

"No." She took one step forward. "You did not hold me when I was bleeding. You did not sit the nights with the pump while my body thought my baby was here. You did not carry what happened. You carried paper."

His face reddened high in the cheeks. "I carried this family."

"Like debt."

For a moment she thought he might strike her with the cast, clumsy and brutal. Instead he thrust the typed pages toward her.

"Sign acknowledgment that you are leaving voluntarily against my advice," he said. "Sign now."

"I won't sign your words."

"Then hear mine." He lowered his voice to a hiss she knew from behind closed doors. "I will make certain they call you unfit before this month is out. I will make certain your daughter hears what you are when she is old enough to ask."

Noreen felt something go quiet inside her, not numbness, not courage exactly. A kind of clean line. She thought of the dormitory Keane had described, narrow cot, other women breathing in the dark, curfew bell. She thought of waking without waiting for his shoes in the hall. She thought of a week. A week could be used.

"You can try," she said. "But I'm leaving for recovery and my daughter's welfare."

She said it again, as Keane had instructed, steady and plain. "I'm leaving for recovery and my daughter's welfare."

Peter's hand trembled on the papers. Rain spotted the top sheet and blurred one typed line before he could shield it.

Arthur moved then, not between them in any theatrical way, simply into the practical gap, taking the second bag from inside the door where Noreen had set it earlier.

"All set," he said. "Step's slick. Hold the rail."

Keane touched Noreen's elbow, light and firm. "Van. Now."

Behind them Peter called out, voice suddenly formal enough to be read into minutes.

"I shall petition first thing in the morning. I shall notify parish and clinic. You are making a catastrophic mistake."

Noreen did not turn back. She had learned that looking back gave him another surface to write on.

She climbed into the van. The metal floor smelled of damp laundry and diesel. Arthur passed up the cases. Elsie came close enough to press a wrapped parcel into Noreen's lap: bread, apples, a small bar of carbolic soap cut in half to make it look like two. Her eyes were dry and furious.

"Eat before curfew," Elsie said. "And don't let the nuns see you crying if you can help it. They smell weakness like cats smell fish."

Noreen caught her sleeve and squeezed once. "You kept me alive," she whispered.

Elsie looked away toward Peter's doorway. "So did you."

Keane climbed in and banged the side panel. The driver engaged gear. As the van pulled out, Peter stood in his doorway with the letter in one hand and his statements in the other, rain soaking both, still trying to keep the pages flat.

At the lane corner Mrs Bhatti stood under her shop awning with a crate of potatoes, watching openly. Two boys stopped kicking a punctured ball and stared. A woman from number nine crossed herself as if for a funeral.

Public fact, Noreen thought. There it is.

The van hit the pooled water by the viaduct and rocked. Keane steadied the case with her boot and checked Noreen's face.

"Breathe in through your nose," she said. "Slow."

Noreen did. Diesel, damp wool, soap, the sweet rot of thaw mud through the door seam. Her body hurt in specific places: stitch line, breasts, lower back. Each ache was proof she was still in it.

Saint Brigid House rose at dusk behind the old convent wall, brick darkened by rain. Light leaked from basement windows. Inside, the corridor smelled of boiled cabbage and bleach. A bell rope hung by the office door with a sign: CURFEW 20:00 SHARP. NO EXCEPTIONS.

Sister Imelda took Noreen's details without looking up much, spectacles low on her nose, cardigan sleeves rolled to forearms thick from carrying laundry baskets. A silver thimble hung on a chain at her neck, tapping the desk each time she wrote.

"Occupation?" she asked.

Noreen hesitated.

"None at present," Keane said.

"Means of support?"

Noreen swallowed. "Not settled."

Sister Imelda wrote that phrase exactly: NOT SETTLED.

"You understand this is temporary emergency lodging," she said. "One cot, shared washroom, chapel attendance Sundays, chores as assigned when medically fit. Visitors only in front room during set hours. If there is legal dispute, we require paperwork. We are full and have no nursery places."

"I understand," Noreen said.

She signed the intake book. The pen scratched hard through cheap paper, louder than it should have been.

A young woman led her up two flights to the laundry annex dormitory. The woman wore men's socks under her shoes and had stitched her initials into the cuff of her cardigan in red thread. She spoke only once.

"Mind the fourth board. It lifts."

The cot was narrow and iron-framed. A brown wool blanket lay folded at the foot. On the sill, condensation ran in clear lines down to a chipped paint ledge. Somewhere in the house a baby cried, then another answered.

Noreen sat on the cot and took the transfer bracelet from inside her blouse. The plastic had warmed to her skin. She looped it around the bedpost and tied it with the cotton tape, once, twice, firm enough that she would feel it if she reached out in the dark.

No guarantee of income. No guarantee of custody. No guarantee of anything beyond this one bed and the bell that would ring at eight.

Still, the door shut with her on the right side of it.

Outside, rain kept falling on Calderbank, washing soot down every brick in town and carrying it all, black and slow, toward the river.


# Chapter 17

By midafternoon the snowbanks had lost their shape and turned into a dirty, collapsing wall along Barrow Lane. Water ran under them in brown veins. The gutter outside Elsie’s front step had been full since breakfast and now it had risen over the iron lip, spread across the lane, and started feeling for lower ground. Every few minutes there was a heavy slap from the eaves as another sheet of meltwater dropped and smacked the paving stones.

She had rolled two old towels and pushed them hard against the back door, then three flour sacks over those, and the water still found a way in. It came cold and thin and determined, a steady silver line under the warped wood, making the flags shine. When she bent to wring out the first towel, her belly tightened so hard she had to brace her palm on the table and wait for the line of pain to finish writing itself from her spine to her pubic bone.

She stayed still until the pain dropped away. Then she looked at the clock over the mantel, not because that one was right but because it was louder than the kitchen clock and she could hear it over the gutters. The second hand dragged its red tip around and around while she breathed through her nose and told herself it might still be the kind of practice pain that warned and then went quiet.

The next one came nine minutes later and stole her breath halfway through lifting the coal scuttle.

“Not now,” she said to the room, to the water, to the child. Her voice sounded too polite for what was happening.

She set the scuttle down, both hands on its rim, and waited until she could straighten. Across the wall she could hear nothing from the Walsh side but a faint, regular tapping that might have been Arthur in the spare room knocking rust off something metal. Peter had kept the front curtains closed most days since Noreen left for Saint Brigid House. The house next door looked occupied by furniture and rules, not people.

Elsie wiped her hands on her apron, pulled on her coat, and stepped into the lane with one hand under her belly. The water was above her ankles at once, cold enough to numb and then sting. She made for Mrs Bhatti’s shop because the telephone in the back there still worked when the lines in the terraces did not, and because she knew she could not stand in her own kitchen waiting for the next pain without doing something that looked like action.

The lane smelled of wet ash and rot where the snow had been holding everything down for months and had suddenly decided to give it back. Coal smoke lay low, heavy in the damp. Somebody had set two kitchen chairs in a doorway as a makeshift barrier and one had already floated sideways, wedged against a drain cover.

Mrs Bhatti was at the till with her sleeves rolled to her elbows and a tea towel around her waist. She had taken off her cardigan and hung it over a hook above the bread crates. The muscles in her forearms looked harder than the butcher’s.

“You should not be out,” she said as soon as Elsie came in dripping. “Sit. I will ring.”

“It’s starting,” Elsie said. She hated how flat it sounded. “Can you get Nurse Keane?”

Mrs Bhatti had already lifted the receiver and was thumbing the dial. “Clinic first. Then district board. Then your husband.”

Elsie leaned on the counter while the ringing buzzed in her ear. The contraction came in front of witnesses this time. She shut her eyes and gripped the edge of the sweet jar rack. Through the glass she watched her own hand whiten around striped barley sticks.

Mrs Bhatti did not touch her. She stood close enough to block view from the door, her body making a wall, and spoke into the phone with clipped certainty. “Yes. Mrs Harrow, Barrow Lane. Established pains. Thaw flood in the road. No, I’m not saying she can travel.” She listened, nodded, then held the receiver out. “Keane.”

Keane’s voice came through crackling and distant, as if she were standing under rain. “Elsie, listen carefully. How far apart?”

“Nine minutes. Then eight. Maybe less. I can’t keep still through them.”

“Any bleeding?”

“No. Not like before.”

“Good. Stay in the house. Keep warm. Get clean sheets, basin, kettle on. I’m coming from Saint Brigid side and I’ll bring Noreen if she’s up to it. Don’t try the clinic. The low bridge is under water and they’re turning ambulances back.”

The line hissed.

“And Tom?” Elsie asked before she could stop herself.

A pause, then Keane again, practical as a cupboard latch. “If he’s useful, send for him. If he’s not, we proceed anyway.”

The line clicked dead.

Mrs Bhatti set a hand lightly between Elsie’s shoulder blades when the next pain came. “My Rafiq will carry your coal up while you wait. He can run a message to Tom’s brother too.”

Elsie nodded because nodding was easier than saying thank you. She had not seen Tom in four days. The last word from him had come through a boy from the depot: sleeping at his brother’s, sorting shifts, back soon. Back soon had stretched and thinned and then vanished.

By the time Elsie got home with Rafiq sloshing behind her carrying half a sack of coal against his chest, the water had reached the first brick of the doorstep. Arthur was outside the Walsh house with his trousers rolled and boots unlaced, clearing sticks from a blocked drain with the handle of a screwdriver.

He looked up, saw her face, and dropped the tool at once. “It’s started?”

She nodded.

“I’ll do your sandbags,” he said. “Then I’ll go for whoever you want.”

Arthur always spoke like he was offering a wrench, not his opinion. Even now his voice had that linesman habit of no waste in it.

“Tom first,” Elsie said. “Brother’s flat on Wakefield Road. If he’s not there, check the Fox and Crown.”

Arthur gave one short nod. “I’ll take the utility bike. Might make it if the culvert isn’t gone.” He hesitated, then added, almost shy, “I’ve got spare dry blankets in the Walsh airing cupboard. I’ll bring them.”

Inside, the house felt too warm near the range and too cold by the floor, a split climate that made her shiver while sweat ran down her ribs. She stripped the bed, carried sheets to the front room, and folded them with care she did not have for anything else. During each contraction she counted nail heads in the skirting board to keep from thinking ahead.

Noreen came with Keane just before dusk, both of them wet to the thigh and breathing hard. Noreen had tied a scarf over her hair but loose strands had escaped and stuck to her cheekbones. She still wore the narrow plastic hospital bracelet from her own baby’s transfer, turned inward on her wrist so the label did not show. Her coat bulged with folded cloths and two bottles wrapped in newspaper.

“You brought half the linen cupboard,” Elsie said, trying for lightness.

Noreen kicked the door shut with her heel. “I brought what mattered.” Her smile was quick, then gone when Elsie bent over another pain. “Ah, love, that one’s sharp. Lean on me.”

Keane had already dropped her canvas bag on the table and was snapping open metal clasps. She set out a blood pressure cuff, forceps wrapped in boiled cloth, a bottle of disinfectant, cord ties, notebook, pen. Her wedding ring was looped on a thin chain around her neck, tucked out of the way inside her blouse.

“Get your coat off,” Keane said. “We need your pulse before you pretend you’re fine.”

“I’m fine.”

“You are laboring in a flood. You are not fine. Sit.”

Elsie sat.

Keane’s hands were rough and steady. She took blood pressure, timed two contractions with a watch whose glass was cracked through one corner, listened low with the Pinard horn, then checked dilation with brisk economy and no apology.

“Four centimeters. Proper labor. Baby’s head is down and engaged. We stay put.” She glanced toward the window, where floodwater reflected a trembling strip of sky. “No carriage, no car, no ambulance is getting through tonight. We make this room work.”

Noreen had already dragged the narrow mattress from Elsie’s bedroom and laid it near the range, covered first with oilcloth, then folded blankets, then the clean sheet Elsie had ironed two weeks ago when waiting had still felt like a controllable thing.

Elsie watched the setup as if it belonged to another woman. “Tom should be here,” she said quietly.

Noreen looked at her and did not lie. “He should.”

Keane tied her apron tighter. “He can hold the basin if he arrives. He is not required for delivery.”

Another contraction took Elsie by surprise and folded her over the table edge. She heard herself make a short animal sound she had not planned. Noreen was beside her at once, palm hard on her lower back in circles that found the one place the pain would answer to pressure.

“Breathe with me,” Noreen said. “In, out, in, out. You’ve done hard things before. This is one more and we do it together.”

Elsie breathed. She felt Noreen’s hand trembling from exertion and held still anyway.

Arthur came back once with sandbags and blankets, then left again for Tom before full dark. Keane sent him with a note for the civil defense post and another for the clinic to say home confinement had been initiated under flood obstruction. She dictated as she wrote, each word precise. The habit calmed Elsie more than soothing did.

Outside, the water kept climbing.

***

By nine o’clock the lane was a moving brown sheet lit by window lamps and the occasional sweep of a torch. Water touched the bottom rail of Elsie’s front door and made it thud in its frame every time a bigger wave came down from the top of the street. The gutters did not sound like gutters anymore. They sounded like kettles poured out continuously.

Keane kept time and sequence. Empty bladder. Sip water. Change position. Rest jaw. Count through the peak. She gave instructions in short chains, then explained when Elsie demanded a reason. That was how Elsie knew she was scared: she kept asking why for things she already understood.

“Because if you lock your jaw your pelvic floor locks too.”

“Because if you lie flat your back labor worsens.”

“Because pain does not mean danger by itself.”

Noreen stayed close enough for Elsie to smell the sour-sweet milk stain drying on her blouse. Between contractions, Noreen squeezed milk by hand into one of the wrapped bottles and set it near the coolest part of the room, face turned away for privacy she no longer got much of. She apologized once for taking time.

“Don’t,” Elsie said. “Feed him when you can. You don’t need my permission.”

Noreen gave a short laugh with no humor in it. “I know I don’t. I still ask.”

When the next contraction rose, Elsie bit down on a clean corner of sheet and pushed her forehead into Noreen’s shoulder. Noreen smelled of rain, soap, and the yeasty ghost of the bakery where she had worked before marriage put her indoors full-time.

At ten Arthur returned alone, soaked through and breathing like he had been running stairs.

“Brother’s flat was dark,” he said from the doorway, careful not to drip onto the clean linen. “Neighbor said he hadn’t seen Tom for two nights. Fox and Crown closed early because the cellar flooded. No sign.”

Elsie gripped the chair back until her knuckles hurt.

“Did you check the depot?”

“Gatekeeper said he’s not rostered this week. Didn’t know where he was sleeping.”

The contraction hit then, huge, and she could not answer. She leaned over the chair, knees bent, and felt the pain take all language from her body and replace it with pressure. Noreen counted her through it. Keane’s hand was on her belly, feeling the contraction and the baby’s position at once.

When it passed, Elsie looked at Arthur and heard herself say, “Don’t go again.”

Arthur nodded once. “Right.”

It landed in her then with a clean, cold certainty: Tom was not delayed. Tom was absent. It was not weather. It was not roads. It was him.

She had thought she was past the part where she waited for him with a table laid and excuses ready. Labor stripped that thought down to bone. Every minute of pain made the waiting look stupid.

Keane sent Arthur to the scullery with a bucket and bleach to keep busy and out of the way. He obeyed without offence, sleeves rolled, moving like a man who had learned the value of being told exactly what to do in a crisis.

Near midnight Elsie’s waters broke in a warm flood that ran down her thighs and pooled dark on the oilcloth. She looked down and saw a green-brown stain mixed in, thin but there.

Keane saw it too. “A little meconium. Not heavy. We keep moving.”

“Is she all right?” Elsie asked.

“She’s coping. You keep coping and we meet her.”

The answer was honest enough to hold.

Contractions came every three minutes then two. Time stopped being clock and became pattern: rise, hold, crest, tear, drop. In the drops she shook, teeth chattering though sweat soaked her shift. Keane checked dilation again and said, “Eight. Nearly there.”

“Nearly where?” Elsie said, and then laughed once because the question was pointless.

Noreen wiped her face with a flannel cooled in basin water and wrung it out with both hands. Her own hands had the faint yellow tint from chlorhexidine that never quite left after hospital visits. Elsie noticed everything because there was nothing else to do between pain storms but notice.

At one point the electricity dipped. The lamp in the hall dimmed to orange thread, then recovered. Arthur appeared with two hurricane lamps lit, set them on high shelves out of splash range, and vanished again without needing thanks.

Pressure changed. It stopped being all-around pain and narrowed into a downward force that made Elsie bear down before she chose to.

“Not yet,” Keane said. “Pant through it. Blow.”

“I can’t.”

“You can. Short breaths. Like this.”

Noreen demonstrated, quick shallow puffs, eyes locked to Elsie’s. “Copy me. Come on. You are not alone in this room.”

Elsie copied because copying was easier than arguing. The urge passed. It came back stronger.

Keane checked once more. “Ten. Good. Now you push when I say and not before. On your side first.”

Elsie rolled with help, one leg braced on Noreen’s thigh. The first push burned but did not move enough. The second moved something deep and final. Keane’s voice cut through rain and gutter roar.

“Again. Chin down. Put it where it hurts.”

Elsie pushed and felt her body split around the child’s head. She yelled, a raw sound that startled even her.

“There,” Keane said. “Head is there. Stop. Pant. Good girl.”

The phrase should have annoyed her, but Keane said it like a worker praising another worker, not a priest blessing a penitent.

She panted. She felt stretch, sting, then release as shoulders rotated. The room narrowed to three faces and one task.

With the next contraction she bore down and the baby slid free in a wet, shocking rush into Keane’s hands.

Silence for half a second.

Then a rough, outraged cry tore through the room.

Elsie started crying at once without deciding to. She laughed and cried together, chest hitching. Noreen made a sound like a prayer breaking in the middle.

“Girl,” Keane said. “Strong voice. Good tone.”

Keane suctioned mouth and nose, rubbed the baby’s back with a dry cloth until color deepened from dusky to red, then laid her slippery and furious on Elsie’s chest. Heat bloomed where skin met skin. The baby’s fists were the size of walnuts, clenched so tight the knuckles blanched.

Elsie touched her with two fingers first, as if checking whether she was allowed.

“You are here,” she whispered. “You are actually here.”

Noreen tucked the sheet around both of them and pressed her lips to Elsie’s damp temple, quick and firm, before stepping back to hand Keane cord ties.

The work did not end with crying. Keane watched for placenta, measured blood on pads by eye and touch, palpated uterus until it hardened under her palm. Elsie hissed when cramps came with each massage.

“Stay with me,” Keane said. “Placenta needs to come complete. Push once more.”

Elsie did, exhausted and shaking. Keane inspected the placenta under lamp light, turning it with two gloved hands.

“Intact,” she said. “No retained bits. Bleeding is acceptable.”

Acceptable. The word felt miraculous.

When Keane finally sat back on her heels, it was past three and the rain had thinned to a steady drizzle. Floodwater still moved in the lane, but slower now, less angry.

Arthur knocked softly and asked through the door if they needed anything from the relief van parked at the top road.

Keane answered before Elsie could. “Tea if they have it. Sugar for the mother.”

Arthur said, “Right,” and went.

Elsie looked at the child on her chest, then at Noreen. “He never came.”

Noreen met her eyes. “I know.”

There was no softening left in either of them for the fact.

***

Dawn came as a gray dilution in the window glass. The lane looked flayed open, every rut and pothole visible under shallow moving water. Someone upstream had lost a coal bin lid and it drifted past like a black raft. A pair of children in rolled trousers poked at floating debris with broom handles until a woman shouted them inside.

The baby rooted and fussed against Elsie’s chest. Keane helped position her to feed, guiding tiny mouth to nipple with fingers gentler than her voice ever was. Pain bit hard at first latch and then eased into a heavy tug.

“She knows what she’s doing,” Keane said. “You both do.”

Elsie watched the small jaw working and felt a terror so sharp it was almost clean: this could still be taken, even now. By fever, by flood, by a signature in the wrong hand.

Keane packed used instruments into a tin with boiled water, then opened her notebook on her knee. “We need a plan if roads stay blocked and you deteriorate, or if baby needs transfer before you can travel. Who’s your emergency contact for consent if you’re unconscious?”

Elsie stared at her. The question sounded administrative but carried a blade inside it.

“By default it would be husband,” Keane said, not unkindly. “Unless you specify otherwise in writing. Given current circumstances, I’m asking now while you’re alert.”

Current circumstances. A phrase that included everything without saying betrayal out loud.

Noreen had moved to the table to wash cloths in a basin, sleeves pushed up, forearms thin and marked with new needle bruises from recent blood draws. She kept her eyes on the water as if giving Elsie privacy and witness both.

Arthur returned with a dented kettle from the relief van and a loaf wrapped in ration paper. He set them down and stepped back, cap in both hands. His knuckles were cracked in red grids, deeper where old frost splits had reopened in the damp.

“Road chief says no vehicles down here till afternoon,” he said. “If rain comes hard again, maybe longer.”

Keane nodded and kept writing timings from memory: full dilation, delivery time, placenta complete, estimated blood loss.

Elsie looked toward the mantel where her wedding photograph still stood in its cheap frame, tilted because one corner of the backing had warped. Tom in his good suit, grin too broad for the camera. Her own face younger, tighter around the mouth.

She felt no impulse to protect him anymore. Labor had burned that reflex off.

“Give me paper,” she said.

Keane looked up. “For what?”

“For what you asked.”

Keane tore a clean sheet from the back of her notebook and slid it over with a fountain pen. “State your full name, child, and instruction. Date and time. I will witness.”

Elsie shifted the baby to Noreen, who took her with both hands and an expression Elsie had only seen once before, when Noreen first held her own son before transfer and would not blink in case blinking counted as looking away.

Elsie sat at the table. Her legs shook from effort and blood loss, but her hand stayed steady enough once she started.

_I, Elsie Harrow of 14 Barrow Lane, being in full possession of my mind after delivery, direct that if I am unable to give consent for my daughter, Noreen Walsh is to act as emergency guardian for medical and immediate care decisions until I recover._

She stopped, then added one line more.

_This instruction is made because my husband, Thomas Harrow, is absent and not reliably reachable._

She signed with the same careful script she used on cleaning ledgers and school forms.

Keane read it once, lips moving. “You understand this may provoke dispute later.”

“Yes.”

“You understand I can attach it to my clinical record and notify clinic intake when transport opens.”

“Yes.”

Keane signed beneath as witness, date and time precise to the minute. Her pen scratched hard on the paper. She blotted the ink with gauze, folded the note once, and slipped it into a waterproof sleeve from her bag.

Noreen stood by the stove with the baby at her shoulder, swaying from habit more than intention. Her own body still moved cautiously, protecting a core that had not healed. Milk had soaked through one side of her blouse again in a pale crescent.

“You are sure?” she asked quietly.

Elsie met her gaze. “I’m done waiting for men to arrive in time for decisions. I was sure the first contraction that took me to my knees.”

Arthur had turned his face away to the window, giving them dignity, but his ears reddened. In a lane of thin walls, nothing stayed private for long.

Keane closed her bag with a metallic click. “Then that is the instruction. I’ll state it to relief control as part of handover, and I’ll repeat it at clinic. If anyone challenges, they challenge me first.”

There was power in that, not legal in the grand sense, but immediate and practical, the only kind that had mattered all winter.

A knock came at the door. Mrs Bhatti stood on the step in gumboots, skirt hem soaked, holding a parcel of clean nappies wrapped in newspaper.

“I boiled these at dawn,” she said. Her eyes went from Elsie to the child to Noreen and then to Keane’s open notebook on the table. She missed nothing. “Do you need more coal?”

“We need quiet and dry cloths,” Keane said.

“You will have both.” Mrs Bhatti set the parcel down, lowered her voice, and added to Elsie, “My boy says your husband was not at his brother’s yesterday either. If anyone asks, I will say what is true: women got this child born.”

She left before thanks could become sentiment.

Keane rose to check the door seals and sandbags once more. Noreen brought the baby back and settled her against Elsie, careful of the tender stitches and afterpain.

Elsie looked at her daughter’s face, crumpled and furious and alive, then at the note tucked safe in Keane’s bag.

It was not law enough to protect them from everything that could still come. It was enough to mark the line of who stood where when the water rose.

Outside, runoff kept moving down Barrow Lane toward the river, carrying winter away in cold brown sheets. Inside, the room held new heat: kettle steam, milk breath, damp wool, and the stubborn pulse of two women who had learned to choose each other before the world approved.

When the baby fell asleep at last, mouth parted, hand unclenching on Elsie’s skin, Elsie let her own eyes close for one minute exactly.

She did not pray for Tom to appear.


# Chapter 18

The thaw had a sound she had forgotten was possible: not wind, not wheels, not men shouting down the lane, but steady dripping from every eave on Barrow Lane, a patient tapping that went on before dawn and kept on after dinner as if the houses had started leaking time. Elsie woke to it with the baby on her chest, damp hair stuck to her own skin, the room smelling of milk and stale coal and the iron tang she still carried between her legs ten days after labor. She eased herself upright, biting down when the pull in her belly caught, and sat until the room settled into one shape. The child made a thin goatish complaint, opened her mouth, and rooted blindly into Elsie's shirt.

In the kitchen, envelopes had swelled in the damp and curled off the table edge. The top one was from Calderbank Baby Goods, the red print feathered at the corners where meltwater had got into the hall and then dried again. Under it lay a note from Saint Brigid House, folded sharp enough to cut. The last was from Mrs. Bhatti, not a bill this time, only a line in blue pencil: *Tell me if you need lentils before Thursday van. Do not wait.*

Elsie set the kettle, hooked her thumb under the baby's nappy tie, and checked it by touch. She had stopped being surprised by how often she touched everything now to know if it was wet, too cold, missing, crusted, split, emptied. Her fingers had become instruments. The first note she opened was the shop one because she trusted the hand that wrote it.

Outside, a shovel scraped and then knocked on stone three times. Arthur Hale stood in the Walsh front garden in his board jacket, the knees gone shiny, rolling extension cord around his elbow in neat loops. The council van idled at the curb with mud up to its wheel hubs. He looked up when Elsie pushed the lace curtain aside and gave her a nod that meant both morning and goodbye.

He came over ten minutes later when she left the door on latch for Noreen. He had his kitbag on his shoulder and the tobacco tin in his left hand where he kept spare screws, fuses, and mints all in one rattle. A peppermint sat in his cheek and moved when he spoke.

"Council pulled the billet order," he said. "Road teams are back. They want us on the high lines near Dewsbury before the afternoon melt. I left two dry sockets in your scullery and one in next door. Keep anything electric off the floorboards till the drain clears."

Elsie held the baby closer because the hall still breathed cold up through the tiles. "You eating before you go?"

"I had bread in the van. Proper treat." He gave a short smile, glanced at the child, and pulled one knitted glove halfway off, as if asking permission with his hands before touching anything. "She looks sturdier than yesterday."

"She's loud, anyway."

"That helps." He tapped the doorframe with two fingers, habit more than fidget, then looked toward the Walsh side where water had pooled at the step. "I laid a plank over that rut. It'll float if it fills, so test it before you commit your full weight."

He spoke like that even in farewell, in fixes and warnings, offering what could be done in the next half hour and not one inch beyond. He smelled of wet wool and metal filings. When he reached into his breast pocket for the key, he took out a folded photograph by mistake first, saw it, and slid it back without looking at it. Elsie pretended not to notice. He did not talk about himself unless a question was strictly practical.

"Tell Noreen I'll drop her suitcase at Saint Brigid after shift if she wants," he said. "The green one with the busted wheel. It's still in my room."

"I'll tell her."

"Right." He stepped back, then added, almost shyly, "And if Tom turns up and starts something, send for me through Mrs. Bhatti. She knows where our crews are this week."

The van took him away in a spray of dirty water. Elsie watched until it turned at the end of the lane. When she closed the door again, the house felt both safer and less defended, as if one layer of witness had been stripped off.

Noreen knocked with her heel because both her hands were full: a string bag of jars, a wrapped loaf, and her own satchel with the strap darkened where rain had soaked through. She came in breathless, face paler than her old winter pallor, hair tucked under a scarf that had once been cream. The edema had gone from her ankles, but blood loss had left its own mark. She moved carefully at first, then too fast in little bursts, as if she could trick her body into not requiring recovery if she kept it occupied.

"I've brought the heel of yesterday's soda bread and one decent onion," she said. "Don't praise me, I charmed it out of Sister Pauline by claiming you had no appetite."

"I don't have one."

"Exactly. I did not lie."

She set the bag down, kissed the baby's forehead with lips dry from the cold, and then held out her right hand. Around her finger hung the plastic hospital bracelet from the special care nursery, the tiny tag creased where she'd bent it too many times.

"They're changing visiting hours again," she said. "That's what this morning was. More rules."

Elsie poured tea before asking because tea gave her something to do with her eyes. "How bad?"

"Depends if you're fond of humiliation before noon." Noreen took the cup and cupped it with both hands, inhaling steam like medicine. "The matron says I can keep my bed and still go to the nursery if I produce three things: a priest's note, a fixed address, and proof I'm not running loose with disreputable company."

She gave the last phrase with bright mockery and then looked at Elsie's face and softened. "Her words were kinder than that, but not by much."

Before Elsie could answer, another knock came. Three hard raps, measured and official. The baby startled and spat milk down Elsie's sleeve.

The man on the step wore a black raincoat and shoes too clean for Barrow Lane. A leather folder sat under his arm, edges protected by a clear plastic sleeve. He introduced himself as Mr. Yates from Calderbank Baby Goods and asked, without greeting, "Mrs. Harrow?"

Elsie stood in the doorway with the child on her shoulder. "Yes."

"I have called regarding the arrears account. Twelve pounds and eight shillings outstanding on your pram hire-purchase. We posted warning in January and notice in February. Our warehouse has released spring stock. Management is now reviewing delinquent contracts for recovery."

His gaze dropped to the infant, then to the hallway where the pram chassis stood against the wall, one wheel tied with string where the rubber had split.

"You intend to recover that?" Elsie asked.

"I intend to prevent court escalation," he said. "If we can agree schedule today."

Noreen had come up behind Elsie without noise. "You can agree schedule with her and leave the speeches in your folder," she said pleasantly. "She's just fed."

He ignored her. "The contract is in both names. Your husband has not responded to summons by post."

"He's not here," Elsie said.

"Then he should be found."

The baby began to cry in earnest, long outraged pulls of breath, and Elsie felt her own body answer in ache and leak under her blouse. She shifted the child, looked at the man, and said, "Tell me what payment keeps you from turning up with bailiffs while I'm bleeding on my own floor."

Mr. Yates blinked once. It was the first expression that looked human on him.

"One pound ten this week, and one pound weekly thereafter, reviewed at month's end."

"I haven't got one pound ten this week."

"Then I file non-cooperation."

Noreen put her tea down on the hall table with a small click. "She has one pound today," she said. "No more. You'll take that and write the week as goodwill, because any magistrate you bring this to will ask why your company sent a collector to a postpartum woman before she could stand straight."

Yates turned to her then, annoyed now, and took stock: hostel coat, parish badge pinned crooked, wet shoes leaving marks on the tile. "And you are?"

"A witness with a good memory."

For a second Elsie thought he might refuse on pride alone. Then he opened the folder, drew out a printed schedule, and took a fountain pen from behind his ear.

"One pound today," he said. "Then one pound weekly every Thursday by four o'clock. Miss two installments and we proceed."

Elsie fetched the coin purse from the tea caddy shelf. The coins were warm from the stove shelf where she'd hidden them in an old sock. She counted them into his palm while the baby screamed against her neck. He wrote the receipt in cramped block letters and tore off the top copy. His cuff carried a frayed stitch at the wrist. He tucked the pound away with hands that shook slightly. It struck Elsie that he was likely under his own numbers somewhere, sent down lanes like theirs to keep his seat.

"Good day," he said, and stepped back into the rain.

Noreen shut the door with her hip. "I would've liked five minutes alone with him and his warehouse spring stock."

"You got us a week." Elsie set the baby down in the basket by the range and pressed her hand to her own breast until the ache eased. "That's better than liking anything."

They sat at the table with the receipt between them like a medical report. Elsie took out Arthur's old outage map, turned it blank side up, and drew lines with a blunt pencil.

"Right," she said. "Spring terms. Proper this time. We can't keep winging it by whoever panics first."

Noreen nodded. "Say it and I'll argue where needed."

Elsie wrote headings in block letters because that made the page feel less like fear.

**Money. Heat. Babies. Beds. Witnesses.**

"Money first," she said. "I've got six shillings till Friday after Yates. Mrs. Bhatti is carrying us for lentils and bread but I'm not stretching her tab past Easter. I can start with Mrs. Danvers two mornings next week if I can stand and if she doesn't flinch at blood on the mop water."

"I'll do your stairs on those mornings and say it's for me, not charity." Noreen rubbed the nursery bracelet with her thumb until the plastic squeaked. "Saint Brigid takes one-third of any wages if they call it board contribution. Cash in hand is safer if we can manage it."

"Heat."

"I'm still at the hostel nights. Curfew's half eight, door bolts at nine. Sister Pauline keeps the key on a chain at her belt like she's jailer in a play, but she does it because two girls got followed back last month. She's not wrong."

"Can you stay here some evenings?"

"If I sign out and back by curfew, yes. Not every day. They watch patterns."

Elsie nodded, adding marks. The baby grunted in her basket and waved one fist as if objecting to paperwork.

"Babies," Elsie said, softer. "Mine's here. Yours is in that nursery with glass between you and skin. What do they actually allow?"

"At the moment? Thirty minutes if a nurse likes your face. Longer if your husband arrives and behaves. No evenings if bus is late. No carrying unless weight's up and doctor says."

"And Peter?"

Noreen's laugh came out thin. "Peter says he'll 'consider cooperation' if I come home and stop making scenes. He had that phrase ready in his mouth before I sat down."

Elsie wrote Peter's name and circled it hard enough to tear the paper. "We'll need his signature for now. That's the law, not consent."

"I know."

A shadow crossed the frosted glass of the door. Then a softer knock, two taps and a pause. Sister Agnes from Saint Brigid stood there in a navy coat shiny at the elbows, a knitted beret pinned too tight, and a paper parcel under her arm. She smelled of lavender soap and damp cellar stone. When she talked, she sorted rosary beads in her pocket with fast practiced fingers, one bead per clause.

"Mrs. Harrow, forgive the intrusion," she said. "Mrs. Walsh, Matron asked me to deliver your amended attendance card."

Noreen took the card but did not invite her in. Sister Agnes looked past her anyway and saw the map-paper budget spread on the table.

"I should remind you," she said gently, eyes staying on Noreen, "that Saint Brigid expects residents to pursue reconciliation where possible. The parish is willing to support legal mediation and spiritual counsel. There are concerns in the neighborhood about appearances."

"Neighborhood has concerns about everything," Noreen said. "Last week Mrs. Coates told me floodwater was God's verdict on laundry left overnight."

Sister Agnes almost smiled, then steadied. "This is not about gossip alone. Nursery access paperwork must show stability. The hospital has tightened safeguarding after the January abduction rumor."

"Rumor," Noreen repeated.

"Rumor, yes. But policies follow fear."

Elsie felt anger come up clean and hot, without fuzz. "What does Noreen have to do to see her own baby?"

Sister Agnes met her gaze now. "She must remain resident at Saint Brigid, keep curfew, attend weekly interview with Father Doyle, and avoid overnight stays outside approved addresses. If husband contests, case conference may require his attendance."

"So she can live apart," Elsie said.

"Conditionally."

"Then that's what she's doing."

Sister Agnes nodded once, then held out the paper parcel. "For the baby," she said. "One wool cap. It came from donation box, but I mended the seam myself. It should keep ears warm in this wet wind."

The cap was yellow with one green stripe and a tiny darn near the crown where someone else's name had once been stitched. The detail landed harder than the warnings. Noreen took the parcel and said, with no irony at all, "Thank you."

After she left, they listened to her shoes squelch down the path, then to Mrs. Coates' gate creak across the lane and not close for a suspiciously long time.

"Witnesses," Elsie said, pointing at the last heading. "Arthur's gone. Keane's still ours. Mrs. Bhatti if needed. Sister Agnes maybe, depending which way the wind's blowing."

"And each other," Noreen said.

Elsie looked at her. "That's not maybe."

Noreen sat back, one hand on the table edge, knuckles pale. "Say it plain then. If Peter asks who comes and goes, if Tom asks what this is, if Father Doyle asks why I'm not home. Say it so we can keep saying it the same way."

Elsie thought of the note in her tin, the guardian declaration she had signed while pushing her daughter into the world, Keane's witness line still damp when she'd folded it. Tom did not know. Peter suspected too much. The lane heard everything except what mattered.

"You're kin to us," she said. "Not church kin, not blood kin, ours. You mind her if I'm stuck or ill. I keep a bed and kettle for you if Saint Brigid throws you out. We don't ask husband's leave for that."

Noreen's eyes filled and then cleared before tears could fall. "Right," she said. "Then I mind her Tuesdays and Fridays till curfew, and Sundays after nursery. You come to Saint Brigid laundry room Wednesdays with bottles and we'll pretend it's domestic instruction."

They bent over the map and made marks until the page looked like a military plan, arrows between Harrow House, Saint Brigid, clinic, shop. By noon the rain had hardened, and children ran boats made from cigarette packets down the gutter stream. Mrs. Coates watched from behind her curtain. Elsie opened the front door wide on purpose and shook out a cloth nappy where everyone could see.

"Mrs. Walsh left her basket," she called, loud enough for two houses either side. "She'll be back after nursery forms."

Noreen gave a tiny snort. "You lay it on thick when you're angry."

"I like to save it for public service."

When Noreen finally left for the bus, she crossed Arthur's plank with arms out slightly for balance, laughing under her breath when it bobbed in the pooled water. Elsie watched until she reached the corner and turned toward town, one hand pressed to her satchel where the bracelet and new attendance card rode together.

The house settled into its afternoon noises: drip from the eaves, the kettle ticking on the hob, the baby hiccuping herself awake. Elsie put the signed payment schedule in the tea tin beside the guardian note and closed the lid hard enough to ring.

By evening, she had terms on paper and no idea whether paper could hold against men, law, or weather. But paper was what existed, and she had chosen it instead of begging.

*

Noreen sat in Saint Brigid's office at three-forty with damp stockings cooling against her calves and a headache that came in a bright line over her left eye. The office had one gas fire that worked only when Sister Pauline kicked the valve with her heel, and two framed prints of saints who looked as if they'd never had to queue for a bus in slush. A brass clock above the ledger ticked too loudly for the size of the room.

Matron Quinn was a broad woman with forearms like a baker and ink stains on two fingers. She kept a tin of butterscotch drops in her top drawer and handed one to anxious residents as if sugar could settle law. Today she offered one to Noreen, then one to Peter without comment.

Peter declined. He had come in his municipal coat, collar pressed sharp, right wrist still in a plaster cast wrapped now in a clean linen sling. Rain had beaded on his glasses, and he had not wiped them yet. He looked both careful and furious, which was his best suit.

Margaret Keane leaned against the filing cabinet in a dark cardigan, bag at her feet, reading the nursery form as if she expected it to be wrong and wanted to catch it in the act.

"Let's keep this practical," Keane said. "Baby needs maternal contact for feeding progression. Mrs. Walsh needs consistent access. Transport is still patchy. We are not using the unit as leverage in a marital argument."

Peter folded his left hand over the cast. "I have not done that. I have asked for order."

"You have asked for obedience," Noreen said, before she could decide to be strategic.

Matron Quinn raised a palm. "One voice at a time. Mrs. Walsh, your request is for daily nursery access between four and six, plus one weekend morning. Correct?"

"Yes."

"Conditioned on residence at Saint Brigid and curfew compliance. No overnight absences. You may nominate one support person for transport if approved by matron."

Noreen heard the opening and took it. "I nominate Mrs. Elsie Harrow."

Peter made a sharp disbelieving sound. "That is not appropriate."

Keane did not look up. "Appropriate is whoever gets her there on time and keeps her fed."

Peter turned to Matron Quinn, formal voice tightened to wire. "If this institution intends to facilitate the breakdown of a lawful marriage, I shall write to the council board and to Father Doyle."

"Write where you like," Quinn said. "The question on this line is transport."

"Then put me down."

Noreen laughed once, no humor in it. "You missed three visits in a fortnight and blamed rain on clear days."

"Because I was working to pay for your keep."

"You're paying because the child needs milk and I need a bed you can't lock from outside."

The cast hit the arm of his chair when he shifted. Pain flashed across his face before he hid it. "If you return home, all of this resolves immediately."

Noreen felt the old pull for one second: the map in her head where easiest route was always back through his kitchen, his money tin, his temper schedule, his apology tea. Then she remembered blood running down her thighs onto the stair runner in January and Peter calling it nerves. She remembered him at her hospital bed calculating visiting fare before he touched her hand.

"No," she said. "I'm not returning home now."

He went very still, then more formal still. "Then I cannot endorse irregular companionship that exposes my household to ridicule."

"Your household is not where I sleep."

"You are my wife."

"On paper," she said. "That's what we're handling today. Paper."

Keane set the form down and looked at Peter square. "Mr. Walsh, you can sign parental acknowledgment and maintenance commitment without cohabitation clause. It's standard when placement is separate."

"Standard for whom?"

"For mothers who are safer away." Keane's voice was dry as lint. "We have many."

Matron Quinn slid the form across the desk with one blunt finger. "Sign section three and four. Section five remains blank pending legal counsel. Mrs. Walsh keeps access under house rules. If she breaches curfew, pass is suspended forty-eight hours. If she attends as scheduled for four weeks, pass extends and support person approval stands."

Peter stared at the page long enough for the clock to tick twelve loud beats. Then he took the pen with his left hand and signed in rigid schoolmaster script. The signature slanted uphill by the last letter where writing left-handed fought him.

"This is temporary," he said.

"Everything's temporary," Noreen said. "Even winter."

Quinn stamped the card, tore off the copy, and handed it to Noreen. The paper was still warm from her palm: *Resident permitted nursery access, conditional*. It was not freedom or justice or mercy. It was access.

Outside the hostel, rain had stopped and the sky had opened into a dirty silver light that made puddles look deeper than they were. Noreen walked to Barrow Lane with her shoes in her hand for one stretch where floodwater had eaten the pavement edge. Her stockings clung cold around her knees. By the time she reached Elsie's door, her calves trembled from the effort and she hated that they did.

Tom Harrow stood in the hallway holding a bunch of daffodils wrapped in yesterday's racing pages. He had shaved for once, nicked his chin, and tried to hide the cut with a square of toilet paper that had come loose and stuck now to his jaw. He smelled of cheap soap over ale.

"Nora," he said too cheerfully. "You look done in."

"Hello, Tom." She hung her coat and kept her bag on her shoulder.

Elsie sat at the table with the baby in the crook of one arm and a bottle warming by the stove. Her face had the set she wore when she was choosing exact words and would not be moved off them.

Tom held out the daffodils to her. "For the house," he said. "Couldn't find anything decent this late."

Elsie looked at the flowers, then at him. "Put them in the sink."

He did, awkwardly, and splashed muddy water on the tiles. "I've come to talk proper," he said. "No rows. I know I fucked it. I know what I did. I've started back on shifts, part rota, and I'm sorting the bookie in chunks. I can come home and take nights with the baby, give you rest."

Noreen moved to the stove, turned down the flame, and let silence work. Tom hated silence more than shouting.

Elsie spoke without raising her voice. "How much do you still owe? Not slogans, number."

Tom rubbed his neck. "Around ninety. Maybe a touch over once interest bastards get their bite."

"Any police at the depot?"

"No charge yet. Maybe none. Supervisor said keep my head down."

"Where are you sleeping?"

"Len's sofa. His missus wants me out by Sunday."

"And your plan if I let you back?"

He gave a helpless spread of hands. "Work, pay, keep straight. What else?"

Elsie shifted the baby to her shoulder and looked past him at the sink where the daffodils had already begun to shed yellow dust on the enamel.

"That's not a plan," she said. "That's weather talk."

Tom's voice softened, pleading now. "Els, I missed the birth. I know. I hear it in my sleep every night. I came back because I want to be here. Isn't that worth anything?"

She closed her eyes once, briefly. Noreen knew that look; it was the moment Elsie stepped from hurt into decision.

"It's worth visits," Elsie said. "Tuesday and Saturday, five to seven, here, with me present. You bring one pound for arrears each Thursday and proof on paper. You don't get a key. You don't sleep here. If six weeks pass and you keep every term, we review."

Tom stared as if he'd misheard. "I'm her father."

"Yes. Act like it."

"You're punishing me."

"I'm pricing risk."

The phrasing hit him harder than any insult. He took a step toward her, then stopped when Noreen set the kettle lid down with a hard clang.

"Don't crowd her," Noreen said.

Tom turned on her. "This is my house."

"No," Elsie said. "It's my tenancy till you prove otherwise. You left."

A knock came before Tom could answer, crisp and impatient. Peter stood on the step in a dark overcoat, cast cradled in its sling like evidence. He glanced from Tom to Noreen to Elsie and seemed to reorder his whole approach in one breath.

"I will be brief," he said. "Mrs. Walsh, I have signed your nursery access under protest. I am prepared to resume financial support at full rate if you return to the marital home by Sunday vespers. If you choose continued separation, support will be limited to statutory minimum pending counsel."

Tom gave a short bark of laughter. "Hell of a sales pitch, Pete."

Peter ignored him. His attention stayed on Noreen, controlled and cutting. "I am offering restoration of respectability and stability. That offer is not indefinite."

Noreen felt tired enough to shake. She put her satchel on the table, took out the stamped pass, and laid it flat beside Elsie's payment schedule from Baby Goods. Two pieces of paper with different logos and the same threat written underneath.

"You are offering control in exchange for obedience," she said. "I'm done buying that."

"You cannot support yourself."

"Not alone. I'm not alone."

Peter's jaw moved once behind closed lips. "Do you understand what allegations could be made in custody proceedings?"

"Perfectly." She kept her voice warm, almost conversational, because rage would blur her and he liked blur. "That's why I'm keeping curfew, signing everything, attending nursery, and documenting every pound you send. You can threaten court. I can read forms too."

He looked at Elsie then, as if naming her would soil the sentence. "And this arrangement with your neighbor is to continue?"

Elsie answered before Noreen could. "Yes."

Peter shifted to legal diction, each word clipped to full shape. "Mrs. Harrow, you are actively interfering in my marriage."

"No," Elsie said. "I'm feeding a woman who nearly died in my front room and helping her see her child. That's not interference. That's memory."

For a moment no one spoke. Rain began again, soft against the back window.

Peter drew himself up. "Then we proceed formally."

"Do that," Noreen said. "And send maintenance by postal order every Friday. Address on file at Saint Brigid. If it misses two weeks, Keane says magistrate first hearing is quick these days when infants are involved."

Keane's name landed where argument could not. Peter knew exactly who documented what in this town.

He nodded once, stiffly. "You have chosen your course."

"I have," Noreen said.

He left without looking back, boots striking hard on the flooded path. Tom lingered, uncertain now, two fingers worrying the loose paper on his chin until it peeled away and showed the small cut bleeding again.

"So that's it," he said to Elsie, quieter. "I'm timetable and receipts."

"For now."

"You don't trust me at all."

Elsie met his eyes. "No."

It was the cleanest mercy she had ever given him.

Tom swallowed, nodded once as if agreeing to terms at a betting window, and pulled his coat collar up. At the door he hesitated.

"I am sorry," he said, full form strange in his mouth, deliberate this time. "Not sorry-for-being-caught. Sorry."

Elsie's face changed, not toward forgiveness, not away from it either. "Then keep the terms," she said. "That's the only language left."

After he went, the house felt suddenly larger and poorer, emptied of male volume but full of consequences. Noreen sat down hard on the nearest chair. Her legs had gone to wool.

Elsie poured tea, then changed her mind and poured broth instead, thick with yesterday's lentils and too much salt. Noreen drank it standing up by the range because if she sat she might not rise before curfew.

"You got the pass?" Elsie asked.

Noreen held up the stamped card. "Four weeks on probation like I'm a delinquent choirgirl. Curfew, interviews, no missed sign-ins. But it's daily access if I keep every rule."

"You'll keep them."

"I'll keep enough." Noreen tucked the card into the inside pocket of her satchel, then added with a crooked smile, "I can be good when good gets me in the door."

Elsie laughed once, tired and real. She lifted her daughter from the basket and settled her against Noreen's shoulder for a minute, just long enough for the tiny body to grow warm and trusting against both of them.

"Listen," Elsie said quietly. "If I don't wake one night, if fever comes back, the guardian note stays in the tea tin behind the barley. Keane knows."

Noreen's eyes cut to hers. "Don't say that now."

"I'm not being dramatic. I'm being exact."

"Fine." Noreen kissed the baby's head and passed her back. "Then you hear me exact as well. If Saint Brigid throws me, I come here first. If your payments slip, I pick up shifts and we split bread before pride. If Peter files anything, we answer in writing and in person with witnesses."

"Done."

"Done."

Curfew pressed at the edges of the clock. Noreen wrapped the yellow wool cap around her hand so she would remember to take it to nursery in the morning and pulled on her coat. Elsie walked her to the step with the baby under a blanket.

Barrow Lane at dusk looked like a country after fighting. Snow still clung in grey heaps along the wall bases, but the middle of the street had gone to slick black mud, rutted by vans and boot heels. Gutter water ran fast now, carrying coal grit, cigarette ends, and one child's paper boat that had somehow survived half the lane. Windows opened for the first time in months, letting out cabbage steam, radio static, arguments, laughter. Life was noisy again and no less precarious for it.

Across the lane, Mrs. Coates pretended to shake a rug while watching them. Farther down, Mr. Patel from number nine set bricks in a line to make a dry path for his twins and cursed affectionately every time one kicked them loose. Above, clouds broke in strips, pale gold behind soot.

Noreen stepped onto Arthur's plank, tested it with her heel, then crossed. Halfway over she turned back.

"Tomorrow after nursery," she said. "I'll bring the bigger bottles and that mending needle you like."

"I'll have your share of lentils soaked," Elsie said.

"And the map. We forgot Sundays."

"We won't forget again."

Noreen lifted two fingers in salute and went toward the main road, coat hem dark with spray. Elsie stood in her doorway with her daughter asleep against her collarbone and watched until Noreen disappeared past the corner shop light. Then she looked down the lane where thawwater kept moving, not clean, not certain, but moving in the direction they had chosen.
